CAAIEO CIGARETTES.
THE RED PAGE.
It seems that Aubrey Beardsley will always re-
main more fantastic than forcible. Yet, apart
from the technical skill they display, there is real
power in the fifty drawings which he has just col-
lected m a volume. Not only are many of his
conceptions strikingly artistic in idea, but they
nave nearly always mental suggestion and value.
Characteristic beardsleyism does not lie in the
mere mingling of black and white in dense masses
and strong contrasts—as some of his imitators
sepm to suppose. Nor does it lie altogether in his
exaggerations of draughtsmanship, though these
are indeed distinctive. It is the spirit, savour
emanating from all his drawings which gives them
that quaint air of intellectual other-worldliness.
Urdinary artistic conceptions become refracted
and distorted in passing through the strange
medium of his mind, and leave upon ordinary
the kln d of impression made by a face
which looks at you from a curved mirror. But
Beardsley is neither to be denounced for his
eccentricity nor praised for his nonconformity.
wv®/ a,ued , for his originality, to which
much latitude may be allowed in a far-too-con-
ventional age. His embodiment of the ideas of
merlin and Herodias, here reproduced, is well
worthy consideration.
• * .
Much of the best of Phil May’s work remains
embalmed in The Bulletin. It was while in
Australia that he perfected his fine talent, and
lor humor, vigor, and effective ease he has never
surpassed some of those earlier drawings. So the
not-long-ago published. ‘Sketch-Book’ was in
some degree disappointing to his Australian
admirers, in that it did not show an advance in
capacity and achievement commensurate with the
artist’s advance in sphere and reputation. ‘ Gut-
ter-Snipes ’ —a series of fifty sketches of London
children—leaves a better impression of May
in the maturity of his powers. Perhaps
his result comes rather from exaggeration than
from pure caricature—which consists not in
emphasising individual and local peculiarities to
an unnatural point, but in accurately representing
the subject in an actual or probable situation
which is, however, incongruous with the subject’s
chief or usual aspects. The two examples chosen
for reproduction on this page do not illustrate
this suggested tendency of ‘Guttersnipes,’ but
are charmingly natural examples of clever illus
trative art. And the picturing of life and character
throughout the whole series of drawings is mosl
able and admirable.
* * * ,•
Le Gallienne’s ‘ The Quest of the Golden Girl ’
is meant to taste like Sterne, with a difference—
and achieves the difference. The author, in fact, is
beginning to beat his gold a little too thin. Yet
the book has enough of pristine charm to make it
pleasantly readable, though the woman-elements
on which it largely relies for effect have no par-
ticular piquancy. There are occasional quaint
conceits and captivating phrases—
... the cunning little frieze of Bacchus and Ariadne
followed by a troop of Satyrs and Bacchantes, which the
artist [of a decorated stocking-top] had designed to en-
circle one of the white columns of that little marble
temple which sat before me.
. . . fed from my youth upon the honeycomb of,woman.
... the wild white peace of those silent hours when
we lay thus married and maiden side by side.
No silken madam, by your leave,
Though wondrous wondrous she be,
Can lure this heart—upon my sleeve—
From little pink-print Hebe.
And so on.

* * * *
To all outward seeming G. O
Craig’s “ The Federal Defence
of Australasia ” (Geo. Robert
son and Co.) is a Book. It
is paged and chaptered like s
book, printed like a book, bounc
like a book. In reality, how-
ever, it is only a series of news-
paper artioles, and to throw
r ‘hese into consecutive and co-
herent shape the author has
lacked either art or oppor-
tunity. Thus to stand under
his shower-bath of hetero-
geneous facts and impressions
is like standing under the tilted
cornucopiaof the goddess Flora.
You may expect to be hit with
normal powder or a pumpkin,
Federal forts or a plum, a
bunch of grapes or an item about
Australia’s chances against in-
vasion. So Mr. Craig’s seem-
ing-book is more tedious and
less effective than it should be.
But he has much knowledge
of his subject, and much
capacity to treat it; so his
seeming-book is more inter-
esting and more effective than
it might have been. And he
tells us, as an expert, that
our arms and our ammunition
are obsolete ; that our defences
are of doubtful virtue; that
“our naval squadron ” would be practically use-
less in war, because as a fleet tt cannot steam
more than 10 knots an hour or so, if so much, and
would thus be outclassed and out-manoeuvred by
Russian vessels of similar type, while the 20-fenot
French, German and Japanese merchant cruisers
would whenever they sighted it add eou.iderably
to the multitudinous laughter of the sea.
* * * *
It is pleasant to turn aside from the push of
superficial and pretentious books to the modest,
but very fine and faithful studies by Charles
Grant published in Macmillan’s Library (“ Stories
of Naples and the Camorra”). They have keen
psychological and natural interest, and seem to
leave a more exact and truthful impression of
phases of Italian life than the work of either
Ouida ot Marion Crawford. Grant, indeed-
he was a valuable man of letters with wide
Jturopean experience, born 1841, died 1889-deals
with Ins subjects in quite a scientific spirit, and
his_effeet comes not from rhetorical exaggeration
or dramatic contrast, but from his talent for com-
posing and lighting a simple human picture.

PHILADELPHIOS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Tb . ere . 8 * feeling half-Platonic born of longings embryonic
And with scintillating passion subtly starred ;
» s a kind of stopping-station 'twixt esteem and
adoration,
And the name of it is ‘ brotherly regard/
It is absent with your sister, and (no matter how you’ve
kissed her)
With the girl to whom you specially belong ;
But whenever it is present, it’s superlatively pleasant,
And it possibly is demi-semi-wrong.
Now in certain circumstances—moon-
light, supper, sundry dances—
The cosmic forces gain an added
power:
But she mocks your voice unsteady
when you hint that you are
ready
To forget the bars of friendship for
an hour.
She won’t raise a blush to please you,
she will torture you and tease
you
Till you wish that you were any.
where but there;
Then— O wonder past all guessing !
you will feel her fingers pressing,
And the merest maddening tickle of
her hair.
Soon (it’s puzzling that you started
so indifferently-hearted)
You grow insanely anxious and
, intense:
Yonr veins begin to quiver, and you
swear you won’t forgive her ,
If she calls you back again to com-
mon-sense.
But at last the blush comes trembling,
and she stops (or starts?) dis-
sembling—
You have given up pretending long
before—
The little Cupid-devils troop to mingle in your revels,
As she says she’ll never face you any more.
TThe Pagan
THE DEAD MARCH OF THE
WATERS.
By the blazing messmate seated as the
skies begin to darken,
And the eager flames ascending seem to
flout the tempest’s din,
Swells, mysterious and mournful, over all
a surging . . . hearken! . . .
’Tie the Dead March of the Waters . . .
and the tide is creeping in.
Near the headland, where the she-oaks
swing their melancholy tressee,
And the ti-tree turns to twilight all the
sultry glare of noon,
Where the wrack in clinging clusters every
basalt crag caresses,
Broods, in solitary silence, Desolation
Point Lagoon.
There, when youth’s brief course grows
sluggish, and its lures have given
over,
Are the merry mountain waters to the
bitter ocean wed,
And the tides roll heavy laden with the
gages of a lover,
And in secrecy and sorrow doth the sea
give up its dead.
Evermore from furthest ocean drift wan
garlands to that wooing ;
Stiff and twisted, bruised and broken,
are they oast upon the strand;
On each face a p illid calmness, half a smile
at its undoing,
Half a shattered hope of rescue, and a
clenching at the hand.
Though I’m aging to that music, I shall
never cease to fear it;
Still I draw up to the fireside, happy but
to tie within.
Think it foolish? . . . Listen ! . . . there
now ; that low moaning ! can you
hear it?
T ■t i ■ Dead March of the Waters . . . and the tide is
creeping in.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A LOCUST-THING.
There comes a voice from tall gum-trees,
A shrill or sleepy sound,
When Spring serene with fingers green
Quickens the underground :
What wind-swayed minstrel sweetly sings
In unison with all green things ?
It is the locust, caged so long
In the damp and silent mould,
Till its shape was grown and life had sown
Its wings with green and gold,
And it heard in the rustle of leaves above
A voice that wooed it in lyric love.
The locust may sing a song to-day,
And to-morrow sing it again ;
But ere a week has gone its way—
Quickening, killing on tree and plain,
The song shall cease, fine wings decay,
And green and gold turn brown and grey.
Yet, though the minstrel’s life be brief,
’Tis free of all distresses ;
Care cometh not, nor wet-eyed grief,
To its green leafi nesses ;
And while it lives it pipes its need,
And when it dies it dies indeed.
The moral is—when love looks bright,
Lend ear to what the ages sing :
“ It lasts a noon, it lasts a night,
For love it is a looust-thing.
Beware, beware red lips ! brown hair !
It dies upon the grave of Spring,
For love it is a locust-thing.”
RODERIC QUINN.
BY THE SEA.
A sullen sky, a dull, dark sea,
A single seagull screaming,
No gleam of light to left or right.
And I alone a-dreaming !
A sweep of sand, a ridge of rock,
A line of billows breaking
With muffled roar along the shore
That sets my poor heart aching.
For so against a vessel’s prow
The self-same sea was beating—
The self-same sea—but then to me
A sweeter song repeating.
For then it sang that Love is long,
And now that Life is lonely ;
There’s little music in the song
That falls on my ears only.
Ah me ! Ah me ! that summer sea,
Those days of sunny weather,
Those moonlight nights upon the deck.
And you and I together !
While steaming down the rocky coast
We watched the red sun sinking,
And, like a kindly eye almost,
One star was winking, blinking !
The golden splendor faded soon,
That night in sweet December,
And then arose the mellow moon,
Ah, friend ! do you remember?
We talked of books, we talked of men ;
You with sarcastic jesting;
Till, falling into graver strain—
Some thought your sneer arresting—
You dropped your cynic mask outright,
Your words were true and tender;
I think I saw your soul that night
That night of moonlit splendor !
The waters flow, the wild winds blow,
Those halcyon days are over ;
I do not know—’twas long ago—
If you were friend or lover.
The wild, weird waters ebb and flow,
Their constancy derides us ;
And here am I, and there are you—
God knows what sea divides us !
I do not dread that you are wed —
I guess your situation :
While smoke-wreaths curl around your head,
You, lost in meditation,
Under a bunya-bunya’s shade,
Forget that sweet December ;
For men forget with scant regret —
’Tis women who remember !
Well, shipboard is the place, we know,
For holiday flirtation ;
You only did as others do
In such a situation.
But fun to you was pain to me ;
Changed is my whole existence;
There haunts me yet a strange regret
With shadow-like persistence.
I linger in the sullen South,
This dreary sky above me ;
Regret, and pain, and dreams are vain,
For, oh ! you did not love me.
O stormy sky ! O dull, dark sea !
O sea-gull, wailing, screaming !
There is no light to left or right;
I am alone, and dreaming !
M.L.
DORA WILCOX.

SATURDAY, MAY 8, 1897.
The Boom That Never Came.
Last week the usual unemployed deputation
waited on Premier Reid, of New South
Wales, with the usual request that work
should be found —almost any kind of work
at almost any kind of wages—to keep them
and their families alive through the coming
winter. A little over three years ago, before
the latest outbreak of Foreign-trade in New
South Wales, the same Reid proudly an-
nounced that he would settle the unem-
ployed difficulty, and abolish the unem-
ployed, and make it possible for every
worker to find work, within a few weeks
after his assumption of office. This was all
to be achieved by the blessed influences of
Foreign-trade and free competition, and by
the dumping down of vast quantities of
foreign goods on the Sydney wharves. And
the country half believed him, quite obli-
vious of the fact that it had already enjoyed
forty dr fifty years of Foreign-trade without
any of these desirable things happening. It
was almost persuaded that Foreign-trade was
some beautiful new experiment which it had
never attempted before, and it returned a
fat and foolish jester to office by way of
giving the panacea a trial—just as -if it
hadn’t been already tried and found want-
ing a dozen times or more. Yet the old,
melancholy unemployed deputation still rolls
up. and this time Premier Reid states that
he has abandoned all hope of ever finding a
remedy for the trouble, so the panacea is
dead, and the great remedy is exploded, and
things are just as they were.
* * * *
After nearly three years of the new order
of things it is about time to ask why the
promised boom doesn’t come, and when its
arrival may be expected. Three successive
financial statements have been delivered
under the Reid regime, and each one of the
three has shown a huge deficit. The Govern-
ment, by various quaint juggles and devices,
has professed to call each one of them a
surplus, but the fact glares forth conspicu-
ously in the pages of the State Statistics,
published by the Government’s own official
Statistician and issued from the Government
Printing Office, that they were deficits, and
the attempt to deny the fact is merely add-
ing falsehood to incapacity. The same abso-
lutely reliable authority proves that the
Reid deficit, for the 2h years covered by the
last three financial statements, is five times
as large as the Dibbs deficit for the three
years of Protectionist administration. It
also shows that apart from railway expendi-
ture, which is mainly under the control of
the Commissioners, and for which the
Treasurer is scarcely responsible in any
sense, the economical Reid Government
spends almost exactly as much revenue as
the wildly extravagant Dibbs Government
which preceded it, and spends quite as much
loan money. The great aggregate fool of a
public, unfortunately for its own political
soul, doesn’t read the Statistical Register
of New South Wales. It takes its facts from
the city newspapers, and the city im-
porters’ organs have lied so persistently and
so circumstantially about Dibbs as a frantic
spendthrift and about Reid as an economist
and a financial genius, that all the evidence
in creation would hardly suffice to obliterate
the popular fixed impression on the subject.
Nevertheless, the figures, printed in large
type by State authority for all who care to
read them, show that the Reid era has been
one of heavy expenditure, lavish borrowing
and constant deficit, and, in this respect at
all events, the promised boom hasn’t come,
and shows no sign of coming.
* * * •
It hasn’t come, either, in any other direc-
tion that the public is aware of. The amount
spent in relief to the poor is larger now than
it was in the worst days of the Protectionist
Administration. The railway revenue has
diminished, which is a tolerably reliable sign
of lack of prosperity. The population of
Sydney has decreased by 11,000 under the
beneficent influences of the Foreign-trade
system. The population of the province at
large is still increasing, but it is not increas-
ing nearly so fast as it did in the brief period
of partial Protection. The wail of the un-
employed is as loud as ever it was, and the
authorities of Sydney Benevolent Asylum
announce that the present period is the very
worst in all the long experience of that insti-
tution. New South Wales has had the
largest harvest on record—one quite large
enough to supply all local requirements—and
meanwhile foreign grain pours in by the
dozen shiploads, and renders the local pro-
duct practically unsaleable. Yet bread is no
cheaper, despite this enormous foreign
supply. Neither is anything else notice-
ably cheaper; the large amount of cus-
toms revenue which was sacrificed has
produced no visible compensating advan-
tage whatsoever. The value of property
is lower than it was before the blessing
of Foreign-trade returned to New South
Wales, and the municipal valuations show
that it is still falling. Also money is so
cheap that even the spendthrift Reid Govern-
ment can get more than it wants to borrow
at 3 per cent., and unusually cheap money is
an invariable sign of depression.
* * * *
In the commercial columns of the Sydney
papers the statement appears from day to
day, and has appeared almost every day
since the great Foreign-trade revival was
due and didn’t come, that “ business is de-
pressed,” “there is little doing in the
market,” there is “no demand”—the changes
are rung on every variety of phrase that can
express the utter hopelessness of that boom
which has been so long promised. The
reports of local financial institutions grow no
more cheerful. There are whole days when
there is hardly an advertisement for an able-
bodied man in the Sydney papers, while the
list of houses to let often spreads over eight
closely-printed columns. If these things are
the signs of the promised Foreign-trade
boom, then it is a strange, insane, upside-
down boom, and strange heralds go before it.
The reply which the Sydney importers’ organs
make to all criticisms on these matters is that
“ things are worse in Victoria,” which, even if
it be true, and its truth has never been proved,
is wholly and utterly irrelevant. New South
Wales is more fertile than Victoria, if agri-
cultural statistics count for anything. It is
twice as old a settlement, therefore it should
have more industries and more accumulated
wealth. It has 3| times the area and practi-
cally the same population, therefore it has
far more room for expansion. It produces
nearly twice the mineral wealth of Victoria,
and has thus another great and solid and
continuous advantage over its neighbor. By
reason of its greater area the New South
Wales Treasury obtains £1,500,000 a year
more land revenue than that of Victoria,
which relieves its people from a tremendous
burden of taxation, and saves the necessity
of making the income-tax ss. 3d. in the £
instead of fid. The question at issue is not
whether New South Wales is better off than
Victoria, with which there is no point of
comparison. It would be just as much to
the point to demonstrate that Sydney is
richer and more progressive than Pinchgut.
Nor is it even a question whether New
South Wales is as well off under Foreign-
trade as it was under Protection. The party
now in power promised that it would be in-
finitely better off, and the result, so far,
showsjthat that party lied in every separate
item of its statement. It climbed into power
on the false pretence of a great and marvel-
lous restoration of prosperity, and a resur
rection of the dry bones of the boom, and an
abundance of employment, and not one of
those things came to pass. Even if it could
be proved, however, that things are as good
under Foreign-trade as they were under Pro-
tection, which is not easy of proof, and say-
ing nothing at all about the false promise
that they would be vastly better, the case
for the party now in office is a bad one. Ihe
importers’ policy has been tried in New
South Wales for about half-a-century, so it
has had abundant time to do all the good
that is in it. Protection was a mere casual
interlude ; it had a trial for barely three
years, so it never had an opportunity to really
show what it was capable of. If Protection
also had a fifty-years’ trial, and showed no
better results than Freetrade, it would then
be a fair thing to toss up, or to draw lots out
of a bag, and thus decide which policy to
adopt. If one policy, on a fair trial, can
only show as good results as the other, then
there is no more reason why one should be
adopted than the other. But, so far, there
has been no fair trial. The Protectionist
Government was crushed by the Sydney
press, principally by continuous lying about
a mainly-imaginary “ Dibbs deficit.” The
present Foreign-trade Government is kept
in office by the same press, which, marvel-
lous as it may seem, has managed to keep
three successive Reid deficits out of sight
despite the positive proof of their existence
afforded in the State Statistician’s report
merely by denying their existence. But
even the most enthusiastic organs of the im-
porting interests can’t quite obscure the fact
that the Foreign-trade boom, so long pro-
mised and so long waited for, shows no more
sign of arriving now than it did two years
ago. And, considering all the time which
has elapsed, it is a fair thing to ask when it
is likely to turn up.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Court of Appeal.
After a bitter fight the Convention resolved to
stand by the decision of the judicial committee,
and abolish appeals to the British Privy Coun-
cil, making the Federal High Court the last
resort of the local litigant. The lawyers in the
Convention were pretty evenly divided in the
matter. The barristers, who may become
Judges of the Federal Appeal Court, and who
will have the work of arguing appeals there,
whereas, when they go before the Privy Coun-
cil, the work and the fees are scooped by
English barristers, were mostly in favor of the
proposed reform. The solicitors, who aren’t
eligible for judgeships as things now are, and
who were mad right down to their boots to see
all the loot going to the other branch of the
profession —the supercilious branch which wears
the tail of the dead horse on its head and looks
down on the common six - and - eightpenny
functionary—inclined the other way. But the
laymen and the common-sense of the Conven-
tion were against the ridiculous practice of
sending an Australian judge to the other end
of the world to hear Australian cases, and send-
ing the plaintiff and the defendant and the
grievance there after him; and the solid
phalanx of solicitors and the inevitable Dobson
were extinguished by a big majority. The
arguments advanced in favor of the Privy
Council system were mostly weird affairs.
Probably the weirdest slab of ignorance
of the lot was Joseph Abbott’s plea that
the old circumlocution should be maintained
because “ it would be a calamity if, throughout
the British empire, we had a uniform law ex-
cept for Australia.” In advancing this plea J.
Abbott lost sight of four important points. In
the first place, the law isn’t uniform even now,
for the Britisher himself has no appeal to the
Privy Council; that institution is one of the
things that are good enough for the beastly
colonial, but the Britisher doesn’t take any.
Therefore, by abolishing the ’Privy Council
appeal we are getting nearer to uniformity with
the dear old mother country, instead of going
further away from it. Secondly, the Britisher
doesn’t have to drag his case to the furthest
ends of the earth to get a final decision, and by
ceasing to do so we are again getting nearer to
uniformity. Thirdly, there is no such thing as
uniform laws in the British empire ; there are six
different sets of laws eveninside the British isles.
Fourthly, there is no visible calamity. Most of
the speakers who upheld the ancient way of
doing things wailed bitterly about depriving
the litigant of the “ right ” of carrying his case
to England, and not one of them even men-
tioned that that equally implied the wrong of
dragging the other litigant to England along
with him. There was also a lament about
“ imposing a barrier between the people and
their sovereign,” which beautiful irrelevance
quite ignored the fact that the sovereign
has no more to do with appeals than has
the hangman or the Warden of the
Cinque Ports, and that a litigant may
appeal to the Privy Council all his life with-
out his sovereign even hearing of his exist-
ence. Also there was a bitter cry from Joseph
Abbott to the effect that the Federal High
Court, there being no appeal beyond it to a
Hinglish authority, “could do just what it
liked.” The fact that the last court of appeal
can always do very much what it likes, and
that the Privy Council does what it likes now,
and that there must be some final authority
that does what it likes if there is to be any
finality at all, is a very plain and visible fact,
but apparently it was beyond Joseph Abbott’s
range of vision. Altogether the debate tended
to show that an eminent legal authority can
be made out of very unpromising materials, and
that a very narrow and illogical mind is no bar
to the accumulation of much six-and-eight-
pence.
-
Self-Indicted.
“The spirit of paganism”—according to Car-
dinal Moran —“is increasing in our midst,”
with a result which he professes to have dis-
covered in a growing iniquity of the present
generation. The Cardinal is right in his ante-
cedent. What he calls the spirit of paganism
is rapidly on the increase amongst us—after l{j
centuries of bell and book and candle and
Cardinal ! and by the swiftly-growing “ indif-
ference to divine ” —which is to say, priestly—
“ authority,” a decaying despotism is being
forced to substitute fudge for fulmination
and to fall back upon assertions which spell
either ignorance or mendacity. But since
the priest essentially a professor of fig.
ments—has always been consequently a more
or less audacious juggler with facts, nobody
need take much to heart the Cardinal’s assur-
ance that the world is growing worse—in the
teeth of the most direct possible evidence that
it is steadily growing better. Better, too, not
in spite of, but by reason of that anti-sacerdo-
talism which the clergy find it convenient to call
paganism. But true paganism, like Christi-
anity, was priest-bossed and bamboozled ; it
hadn’t those side-lights upon the alleged super-
natural which are fast rendering it evident that
any one man knows exactly as much as any
other—which is to say nothing —about post-
mortem possibilities, and that, by conse-
quence, the pretender to any superior
knowledge on the point is not only
a fraud but an impertinence. The
world grows—and must grow—more moral-
as it grows more enlightened, and sacerdotalism
—which, for the sake of its own very existence,
has been, all along, the consistent enemy of
enlightenment—has been therefore also, with-
out any figure of speech whatever, the con-
sistent enemy of morality. The more facts
men are acquainted with, the fewer fetishes
they will worship ; and, if science—which is
simply knowledge of provable facts—is destruc-
tive of priestly arrogations, then the denser
the ignorance, the better for the priest. There-
fore, to know —which is to investigate, which
is to withdraw the intellectual nose from the
sacerdotal fingers—is to be guilty of the sort of
paganism which is not so much demolishing as
unbuilding the brain-prisons of the ages, and
driving a doomed ecclesiasticism to cringe where
it would rather crush, and maunder where it
used to thunder.
Turk and Twaddle.
If the Greeks who are faring from Australia to
the fray had looked at the matter right-side up
they’d have stayed quietly where they were
and cabled home the amount of their passage-
money. The services even of Achillks and
Ajax wouldn’t be one-tenth as valuable, six or
seven weeks from now, as would be at the pre-
sent moment the wherewithal to equip the
Spiridions and Dkmftris already on the spot.
For—putting aside the possibility that, by the
time the couple of dozen heroes arrive at the
“ theatre of w'ar,” the curtain will be down and
the lights out —want of coin is almost certain
to rob Greece of whatever little chance lack of
men might have left her. Even equal-num-
bered with the enemy, her prospects wouldn’t
be resplendent —for the Turkish Tommy Atkins,
besides being about as brave “ as they make
’em,” is patient as death and stubborn as a
stone wall—and, as things are, if the “ Great
Weaknesses ” don’t intervene there will simply
be no more Greece. And The Bulletin—
sympathetic though it is inclined to be
with the little-’un in every encounter —
would be in no wise violently afflicted at
that consummation. The hysterical diatribes
of mountebank Stead and windbag Glad-
stone are responsible for an infinity of twaddle
about Turkish brutality and bloodthirst, and
the British evangelical estimate of the Moslem,
as compared with the Eastern Christian, has
long been the laughing-stock of those who
haven’t to take their Turk upon trust. As for
the “ sons of the Greeks,” who are at present
“arising” to such very foolhardy purpose-
globe-trotting has badly discounted theßyronic
legend, and no creedless observer who has had
modern opportunity of balancing Crescent
against Cross can have much sympathy with the
cheap British fire-eaters who wanted the Syd-
ney Greek Consul the other day to forward
them forthwith to the slaughter of the Un-
speakable. The Turk, when he overthrew the
rotten and emasculated Lower Empire, was,
ipso facto, the fitter to survive, and no amount
of race-rancour or creed-clamor can blind those
who really know anything about him to
the fact that he is by a long way
the fittest to this day. It isn’t Eng-
land, in any case, with so huge a slice
of her army disabled by sexual disease —
that can best afford to abide by the traditional
presentment of the Ottoman as a polygamy-
enfeebled sensualist. Not one Turk in a thou-
sand has more than one wife ; not one in ten
thousand is given to strong drink; and there
isn’t a regiment in the Turkish army that will
not, stature for stature, compare favorably in
physique with any regiment of any service in
Europe. The sick man may be pretty bad in
his head, but his limbs are yet lusty enough to
give serious trouble even to the Russian Giant,
single-handed. Not till the nations definitively
join hands against him will his hour strike, and
against that he is for the present tolerably well
safe-guarded by their mangy jealousy of each
other about the disposal of his carcase. When
that interesting little matter does come at last
to be attended to, the Great European shindy
may be looked for in short order.
The “ Telegraph ” Gets Something off
its Mind.
Sydney Telegraph has one of its chronic re-
lapses into virulent Toryism :
Lord Onslow has been telling the London County Coun-
cil that if the Progress!vists of that body are given much
more rope there will result such a very costly triumph of
democracy as has taken place in Maoriland. There,
the ex-Governor said, “the politicians began by petting
the workingman and making jobs for him. Now he holds
them in the hollow of his hand. Ministers cannot make
any serious retrenchment in public expenditure. At the
first attempt their majority would desert them.” This
may be a trifle overstated, but it is substantially true.
The direct Labor representative does not now flourish so
well in Maoriland as formerly, but the voting influence
which made him possible has a firm grip of the. Gover-
nment all the same, which is enough for practical pur-
poses. The way that works have been rushed ahead, the
Trades-hall kind of legislation in which the Government
are always dabbling-, the return to a borrowing policy,
and the baits held out to agricultural landowners in the
form of cheap loans, are signs of political decadence
which support Lord Onslow’s case.

The Society of Artists.
The most artistic thing in the N.S.W. Society of
Artists’ exhibition (Sydney) is a water-color—
“ Showers by R. Sydney Cocks. It is a study
of a flat landscape glistening with rain—delicate,
skilful, and true. The treatment of the sky is
remarkably good, nevertheless the sky is dispro-
portionately large for the composition—though
this seems designed chiefly to show sky-effects.
There is almost as much technical skill, but much
less artistic value, in Mr. Cocks’ exhibit “Surf.”
Miss Alice Muskett’s head-and-shoulders study of
a girl (“ The Red Ribbon ”) has also some charm ;
and there is in the treatment a sense of power
given by hardly anything else in the exhibition.
F. P. Mahony’s “ Waiting ” has a touch of the
artistic meaning which one looks for in his work.
The careful pains taken by D. H. Souter in “ In
the Smithy ” produce a result from the realism of
which the purple tint somewhat distracts. Frank
M‘Comas’s work has promise, but remains at
present a little photographic.
G. W. Lambert’s clever pencil-sketches would
arrest a craftsman’s attention anywhere.
In proportion to the number of exhibits, no sec-
tion shows merit as “Sculpture.” Nel-
son Illingworth’s statuette, “ For Honor’s Sake,”
is a strong and attractive piece of work ; and the
modelling of Miss Cowan’s “Dancing Girl,” of her
allegorical group, and of Geo. W. Hirst’s character
study (126), is noticeably good.
The oil-colors are disappointing—often “ nice ”
and “ pretty,” sometimes technically clever, and
as a rule artistically valueless. F. P. Mahony’s
“ When Centaurs Meet ” leaves the impression of
a promising sketch which should be developed on
a much larger canvas. Emily Meston’s flower-
studies are charming, and her chrysanthemums
seem as well painted as it is possible for chrysan-
themums to be ; but the artistic potentialities of
flower-subjects are limited. Walter Gabriel’s head
of “ Marie ” is clever ; but mere heads, unless ex-
alted to the nth power by idealisation or character-
isation, have little human interest or artistic value.
J. R. Ashton’s “ Arrival of the Steamer ” is a
pleasing, natural little picture. For the rest,
there is plenty of promise, and not over-much in
the way of performance.
After all, performance rests very much with the
leisured, wealthy class who should be patrons of
Australian art. Many of the exhibitors have to
struggle for bread-and-butter before they can
begin to embody artistic ideals—which, if realised
at all, are only realised at a great cost of labor and
time. Surely, when one cannot be an artist one’s-
self, there is no more satisfactory way of spending
money than in trying to make someone else an
artist.
At Seymour (Vic.) Free Library, the other
day (local Telegraph report) :—
A suggestion was made that the subscription to the
Town and Country Journal be discontinued, as it did hot
appear to be much used by subscribers, and Mr. Chittick
suggested that The Bulletin be taken in its place. Mr.
Moorhouse objected on the score that The Bulletin was
a disloyal paper, and said that he would not care to act
on a committee that sanctioned the purchase of this pub-
lication. Other members of the committee thought that
as it was constantly being asked for it should be placed on
the library tables, and Mr. Chittick gave notice that he
would move at next meeting The Bulletin be subscribed
for.
The Bulletin implores Mr. Moorhouse to persist
in his objection ; it also implores the other mem-
bers of the committee to retain the T. & C. Jour-
nal and to keep out The Bulletin from their
reading-room. As The Bulletin is out after
dollars, and not in business merely for the good of
its health, it absolutely loathes people who read it
on the cheap. For that reason, the paper is never
supplied free to any public reading-room ; the only
persons placed on the free list are those who, like
the Rev. Mr. Moorhouse, succeed in having it
excluded by reason of its po ,; tieal principles from
free libraries, &c. Should Mr. Moorhouse succeed
itt-having The Bulletin shut out from Seymour
public library, a free copy will be posted to him
for one year. By the way, the fact that The
Bulletin is “ the paper most asked for at public
reading-rooms ” carries with it a certain amount
of antidote : it is also the paper most frequently
stolen from public reading-rooms.
graph’s inference, is distinctly a sign of labor
predominance in politics. Where there is no
Labor-party in politics Governments don’t
borrow ; therefore, Gillies and Parkes and
M'llwraitii never borrowed, and Forrest, in
Westralia, where labor in the main has no
vote at all, isn’t borrowing now. Also,
the working class, though it is a very large
majority of the people, shouldn’t hold the
Government “in the hollow of its hand ” ;
as some party must do that, it should be
done by the propertied minority. Then
the fact that the Government is supplying
cheap money to landowners shows that it is
solely bossed by the working classes—who, in
the main, are not landowners. Likewise an
attempt to reduce interest is a sign of deca-
dence—though attempt to reduce prices
by Freetrade isn’t. And when a Government
legislates as directed by the Labor majority
which returned it to power it “ dabbles ” and
“ holds out baits,”but when a N.S.W. Govern-
ment legislates as directed by the Freetrade
majority which returned it to power it doesn’t
“dabble” or “hold out baits;” it “nobly
fulfils the mandate of the people.” And when
a Reid Government is at the mercy of a Free-
trade majority that is quite a different thing
from a Seddon Government being at the mercy
of a Labor majority. In the Seddon case the
working class “holds the Government in the
hollow of its hand ”; in the Reid case the
Government “enjoys the confidence of the
people,” which sounds better, but is exactly the
same tiling. In each case it means that if the
Government doesn’t do what the people want it
will be rushed out on its ear and its political
skull be banged on the pavement. And it is a
quaint circumstance that the expenditure of
£400,000 on Maoriland public works in one year
should be unduly “rushing” them forward,
and should prove the horrors of Labor domina-
tion, while the expenditure of more than three
times as much in N.S. W. doesn’t show the hor-
rors of Freetrade domination worth a cent. The
Telegraph, presumably, can’t help these
occasional outbreaks of frantic Toryism, but
Lhey are eminently unfortunate. It makes pain-
ful efforts in its leader columns to look like a
liberal and progressive and large-minded
journal, and then a sudden blast of National
Ass adjectives like the above gives it away
hopelessly. Yet it must blast now and then,
or else explode through the inward accumula-
tion of gas.
The Coming St. Blanky.
Even in the eyes of his bitterest enemies —and
The Bulletin certainly isn’t his warmest
admirer— John Bull has hitherto had it
counted unto him for righteousness that he is
comparatively free from the miserable little
clannishness with which Pat clings to Pat and
Sandy to Sandy. That the Anglo-Saxon, as he
persists in miscalling himself, is the salt of the
earth and the hope of the human race, he has
personally no doubt whatever. He blows his
own trumpet with a lusty lung, and preaches
the great Gospel of Bull upon the house-tops
of the world. But at that he leaves it. If
you won’t accept him at his own valua-
tion—why, so much the worse for you ;
he isn’t going to get angry about it.
Neither does he confound theory with practice,
or sentiment with business. The Englishman,
in the relations of every-day life, is no more
favored of the Englishman than is the French-
man or the Fijian. He must hang by his own
tail, and be judged as entirely upon his indivi-
dual merits as if he were a Portuguee or a
Patagonian. Also, over so-called national
commemorations J.B. has not been hitherto
inordinately given to whooping. About St.
George’s Day, for instance, he has in the
past concerned himself so little that probably
not one Englishman in a thousand could specify
the date sacred to that uncertain Cappadocian
person. But a change looms. The fore-cast
shadow of the record reign imbecilities has
fallen upon the Sassenach in our midst, and the
annual Hibernian and Caledonian cantantrums
are moving his bovine soul to unholy emulation.
“The great enthusiasm with which the Irish
Catholic societies have celebrated the feast of
their Apostle has become,” according to Car-
dinal Moran, “ contagious with their English
friends.” But their English friends could not
be congratulated on a very noble or imposing
celebration, since they had merely signalised the
occasion by devouring a big dinner to the
honor of a holy man about whom none of
them appeared to know anything whatever.
“ A good dinner, however,” added the blandly
sarcastic Patrick Francis, “was a great en-
couragement to Englishmen ; and, no doubt,
by the next celebration they would know some-
thing about St. George.” In which respect,
The Bulletin opines, they won’t be any the
better citizens of a community which has
already a lot more than it has any use for in
the way of saint, and shamrock, and bagpipe,
and bare leg, and small, sub-national cock-a-
doodling generally. And when, in due course,
Taffy catches on with his legendary lies, and
his leek, and all the rest of it, there’ll be
nothing left for Australians but to set up, in
self-defence, a tutelary saint of their own.
And the name of that saint will be “Blanky ! ”
Sydney Hotel Servants.
“A barmaid from the Bush” to The Bul-
letin :
One of the mysteries of N.S.W. is why the powers that
regulate such matters allow so-called leading- city hotels,
where fresh air is necessarily scarce, to pack four and five
people into little windowless boxes, lit only by tiny sky-
lights, and which, in the event of fire, would be mere
death-traps ; while country hotels, where good air, as a
rule, is gloriously abundant, must give each lodger about
80 square feet and have all rooms properly windowed,
ventilated, and provided with fire-escapes. Many of
Sydney’s “genial hosts” would be prosecuted for cruelty
to animals were they to confine a brace of pigs all night
in the so-called rooms into which they cheerfully crow’d
four or five country visitors at Easter time. Instead of
hotels being exempt, as I am told I hey are, from the pro-
visions of the Factory Act, they should be dealt with most
strictly. The ordinary factory hands do get a breath of
fresh air going to and coming from work; they do not
have to camp on the premises, and can at least secure an
airy sleeping-room ; but the unhappy hotel assistant has,
in the majority of cases, to work exceedingly long hours
and then rest in small, stuffy rooms reeking with the
stench arising from the grog-soaked bodies of dirty
drunkards. I know of one hotel where four beds
are ranged round the walls and leave only a narrow
passage into the room and enough floor space for
one person to dress at a time. In these four beds
sleep five women, and when they sleep late one dresses on
the floor and the other four on the beds. And the open-
ing-up of the bedrooms ! No noxious-trade factory could
produce a more hideous stench than that of the vast ac-
cumulation of all the various breaths, that drifts from the
hermetically-sealed sleeping-boxes, and rolls through the
cavernous passages, driving the lingering lodger in horror
to the bar for revivers and the chemist for disinfectants.
In such hotels the adipose old cat who nigger-drives the
show expects the assistants to be going at full steam from
5.45 a.m. till midnight, and later if necessary, and then
start afresh before 12 o’clock same morning. An outing
of a couple of hours one, or at the most tw'o, nights per
week is deemed quite enough for the human machines
whose labor gathers in champagne, chicken-pie and other
luxuries for these pampered bungocrats. Perhaps when
N.S.W. women and females obtain the franchise, a strong
domestic union may be able to exercise sufficient pressure
to compel the gang of selfish talkers called “ our legis-
lators ” to transform the present system of household
slavery and slow murder called “ domestic service.”

Bishop Goe in a Lenten lecture :
Few publications have done more to kindle the love of
our country, and to stir the noble feeling of patriotism,
than “Deeds That Won the Empire,” which should be
circulated far and wide.
Slab from the latest gory mess of “ Deeds ” :
Almost at the same moment the finest soldier in our
battery, and the best artilleryman I have ever known,
Sergeant-Major Alexander Lamont, had the whole of his
stomach carried away by a round shot. He looked up to
me for a moment with a piteous expression, but had only
strength to utter two words, “ Oh ! God ! ” when he sank
dead on the road.
One wonders whether it was Parson Fitchett
and Bishop Goe’s God to whom poor Lamont
cried out in his death agony. If it was the
Moloch in question, then very evidently Ser-
geant-Major Alexander Lamont wasted his
last breath. It would be interesting to know
how Bishop Goe would like to have his right
reverend stomach carried away for the glory of
the Hempire. Even supposing that he survived
the shock, life, to him, would not be worth
living afterwards. A Bishop without a stomach
would be a hideous and intolerable anomaly.
When will susceptible Woman become wide-
awake to the worthlessness of the good-looking
young man who talks love preparatory to apply-
ing for a loan? Never. Barmaids are not the
most artless angels on earth, nevertheless their
small savings are considerably at the mercy of
any flash borrower, armed with a glib tongue
and an ardent expression in his whisky-bright
eye—else, how could some actors live whilst
they are “ resting” from engagements ? To the
male sex it is clear that a genuine lover would
rather eat his girl’s boots than ash her directly
or indirectly for a sovereign. To the female
sex, on the other hand, Romeo’s unblushing
appeal for assistance doesn’t seem at all con-
temptible. Tenderly spoken, it sounds like evi-
dence of Romeo’s fine, manly desire not to be
under an obligation to anyone save his own
little Juliet, whom he is proud to “trust”
by asking tick from her. It passes for a
proof of his passion’s sincerity, so, on the
principle that one good turn deserves an-
other from the same quarter, Juliet buys
her bitter experience in instalments, and
is, as a rule, very little wiser by the time she
is cleaned out. Miss Juliet M‘Keown, who
lately came to Melbourne with £lOO cash, with-
drew her confidence from her “ tall, good-look-
ing young man ” when he applied for the final
£2O worth. She said, “ Why, you want all
my money ! ” and her suspicions were aroused,
with the result that John Swales has gone to
gaol for twelve months. John made the acquain-
tance of Juliet aboard the Oruba, where he
talked love and started to borrow money. On
arrival at port he talked more love and increased
his debt to £BO, at which point the lady’s
credulity stopped short. This prudent pause
was the one astounding incident in the case—
from a male point of view. Flash John was
such an obvious scamp that a woman who could
be bamboozled by him at the outset of their
“ engagement ” seemed bound to remain his
victim evermore.

The Women’s Council of Maoriland thinks
of petitioning its beloved Queen to create an
order of merit for women similar to that of the
Bath or the Star of India. But the Women’s
Council must be aware that all the existing
“ orders ” are mainly for women, and would be
much less often accepted if it were not for the
insensate yearning to be called “ my lady ” im-
planted in the bosom of the average female by
the tommyrot of the Family Herald and the
Young Ladies’ Journal. Probably the new
order is demanded in the interests of the frozen
spinster, and the elderly, weak-eyed, pink-
nosed female who renounces man every morning,
and seeks hopefully for him under the bed every
night. The Bulletin rathers favors the idea
of a special order for women. It might afford
a means of escape for the Radical Premier who
is married. He could pass the inevitable title
on to the missus, and remain himself plain Jim
or Bill.
Proprietor David Syme, having undertaken
to pay the cost of sending a Vic. rifle team to
Hingland, the Age proceeded to explain how
accuracy in target-shooting tends to improve
mankind at large. “ It has been said that in a
perfect civilisation there would be no war. The
question arises, however, whether civilisation
does not owe some of its triumphs to war.
After all, progress has only been accomplished
by the forcible subjugation of inferior by
superior races.” Just here, by the way, arises
the same old question as to the bearing of
“Christianity” upon civilisation. Brotherly
love, saith the preacher, is the master
principle of “ Christianity ” from which
sprang modern civilisation. And civilisation,
says the organ of the journalistic potentate
who “shouts” the riflemen’s trip ’Ome, is
largely the result of human slaughter by hired
assassins. Therefore, brotherly love is blown
from the cannon’s mouth, so to speak, and the
sword is mightier than the pulpit, and the first
duty of every Christian missionary is to wipe
out all obstinate heathens who decline to be
subjugated by the blessed Worrd. And seeing
that the emissaries of Christian civilisation in-
variably accomplish the destruction of the pore
heathen by rum, we are brought to the satis-
factory conclusion that drink is not an accursed
thing, after all.
The other day a portrait of the insufferable
Sargood was presented to Vic. National
Gallery, and accepted and hung by the trustees,
so that this Tory rag-merchant, the open enemy
of the democracy, has his inept face with its
expression of a baffled Angora hung amongst
the portraits of the people’s heroes—real and
imaginary ! The scandalous impudence of this
is felt when it is recollected that the inflated
and decorated draper has just been refused
with scorn by the people of Victoria, to whom
he offered himself as a Federation candidate,
and that nothing was known of the disgusting
job until it had been accomplished in the pre-
sence and at the instigation of a couple of score
of fatted councillors and empty, moneyed hum-
bugs, who will all expect him to do as much
with prints of their dreadful faces some
day. It only remains now for Gillies, and
Fitz Gibbon, and Tom Bent, and Benjamin
Benjamin to hire someone to break into the
gallery at dead of night and hang their portraits
on the line.

B.
A respectably-dressed young man, named Denis
M‘Namara, was tried before Mr. Justice Hodges, in the
Criminal Court, yesterday, on a charge of having crimi-
nally assaulted a girl, named Mary M‘Dermott, aged
about 18 years. Mr. Finlayson prosecuted, and Mr.
Eagleson appeared for the defence. The case for the
Crown was that the girl was formerly employed as ser-
vant at a house in Hotham-street, Fitzroy, where accused
was lodging. Early on the morning of New Year’s Day he
entered her room, and improprieties took place. On the
evening of 3rd January the two went out for a walk together,
and entered the Edinburgh Gardens. There accused
forcibly assaulted the girl. Next day M'Dermott told her
married sister what had happened. The latter called on
M‘Namara, and asked him what he meant by leading her
sister astray. He replied that he would marry the girl.
He failed, however, to keep an appointment to call at the
married sister's house that evening, and a warrant was
issued for his arrest. Accused denied the girl’s story. Mr.
Justice Hodges, in summing up, told the jury that if they
believed the evidence for the Crown they might find
accused guilty with mitigating circumstances. The jury,
after a short deliberation, foynd the prisoner guilty of the
offence, but with mitigating circumstances, and strongly
recommended him to meroy. Prisoner was remanded for
sentence. (Age 30/4/97.)
M‘G. to The Bulletin
The perfervid loyalists of Melb. propose to celebrate
Queen Victoria’s 60th year of reign by building a fever
hospital, and, at a first glance, this would appear to be
the very best outlet imaginable for the crawlsome
hysteria of “ Fat ” Melb. For its own sake, Charity
might howl aloud before the pockets of Toorak would
disgorge ; but with prospective knighthoods for the big
donors and reflected honor for the little ’uns, there
appears to be a very fair chance of the fever-hospital
being an accomplished fact. Very few people indeed are
aware of the full facts of the subject, though one
and all know the fearful condition of the pre-
sent accommodation for infectious diseases, so even
the reddest Republicans are inclined to give tacit
support to the scheme. As a matter of fact no further
accommodation is required. It is, the want of mainten-
ance, and not the want of capital, which is responsible for
scarlet - fever patients, diphtheria patients, typhoid
patients, and plain delirium tremens patients being
crowded into the same tents in the grounds of the
Melbourne Hospital to contaminate each other and
present more hideous complications to the surgeons
every day. In the grounds of the Alfred Hospital,
which should certainly be the principal hospital of
Melbourne, is an isolated two-storey wing containing
all equipment for hospital work, and beds and bedding
sufficient for over 100 patients. This building was erected
some years back, but up to now not a case has been
treated in it, and to-day the beds lie slowly rotting. The
huge wing is locked up and deserted for the sole and sim-
ple reason that the Alfred Hospital has not sufficient
income to keep it going. And, instead of applying the
sum they raise to the maintenance of this wing, the
jubilee whoopers purpose frittering away the cash
in some flash architectural triumph, and, after pro-
viding accommodation for perhaps as many patients as
the Alfred wing could take to-morrow, will airily accept
their knighthoods, &c., and go to London leaving a big sur-
gical and nursing and managing staff totally unprovided
for, and another charitable institution to worry along on
a public dole about half enough for its needs.
Kirton, M.L.A. (of Victoria), has at last got
his Old Age’Pension Committee sitting, and
hopes to draw up a workable scheme for pen-
sioning all indigent Victorians over 60. The
committee which sat on the same question in
N.S. Wales was a failure, because it lacked the
courage to make the scheme large enough for
the emergency, and even with regard to the
very small scheme which it did evolve, it hadn’t
the pluck to suggest means for raising tha
necessary funds. The mystery is just this :
To give 10s. a week to every impoverished in-
habitant aged over 60, and, say, 15 years resi-
dent in the province, would cost probably
.£150,000 a year. How is the money to be
raised—by a tote - tax, State lottery, State
drink or tobacco monopoly, tax on absentees,
patent-medicines, foreign fire-insurance co.’s, by
running a State Fire-insurance Department, or
how ? Half-a-dozen of these together might
yield the required revenue. The essential is
that some new method of money-raising must
be devised or the Pension scheme is impos-
sible.
Law reform in N.S.W. is again postponed, the
Govt, alleging that it wants to see how recent
reforms in the other provinces and in England
are answering. Meanwhile, legal procedure
in N.S.W.—on account of its intricacy,
slowness and cost —is stinking in the nos-
trils of a long-suffering public. And no re-
form will be acceptable to that public,
unless it makes court procedure and deal-
ings with property simple, expeditious and
cheap. Such a reform, however, cannot be
palatable to the legal monopolists—and there
are two barristers and two attorneys in the
N.S.W. Cabinet, and plenty of both varieties
of Shark in Parliament. A commission, com-
posed of nine-tenths business men and one-tenth
lawyers, might do some good by threshing the
matter out, and suggesting reforms.
Two New Statues.
No. I.— William Bede Dalley, P.O.
“ William Bede Dailey, P.C., Scholar, States-
man, Patriot.”
So runs the graven legend to the pediment that
holds
The effigy of a man of many attributes. And
there he stands
Turned from the church he should have
cherished, and maybe did ;
From fields of boyhood’s pleasure ; from the
broad seas he loved,
Facing the Imperial North as though to catch
approving glints
Of Her Most Gracious Majesty and goodly
Consort Albert.
Well might the Little Boy at Manly ask :
“ Why stands Bill Dailey thus? ”
Scholar; and yet what native inspirated with
thy great erudition
Echoing thy words of wisdom proudly saith :
“ Thus said the Master ? ”
Statesman ; that rang the changes on the
“ Silken Bonds,” and
Giving lip-loyalty practical effect established
deadly precedent.
Patriot; whose love of country swayed to the
magnetic power of those
Who loved thy land better than thyself. And
so you stand—
The Orator whose golden tongue clarioned the
note untrue,
Thy life the saddest monument of one who
should have been Australian.
No. 2.—Governor Phillip, the First Aus-
tralian Gaoler.
Phillip; thy brazen figure, type of a brazen time,
muffled thus,
Bespeaks in thee more sense of fitness than in
thy brazen originators.
But, Phillip, where are thy bold cobbers of the
present age ?
Who are thy pals in spirit that thus desert thee
in thy dark hour ?
Leaving thee a muffled guy for unresponsive
scoffers who assert
Thy pose is perfect, and hidden thus is thy
appropriate guise,
A cruel reminder of a crueller past canopied
from the present ;
A type that has been seen, but should not be
again.
And who the man of brass that dare unveil thee,
enthuse upon thy times,
Extol thy many virtues, and bid us emulate
them ? There is but one,
The bravest he; in his veined runnels flows the
blood of all the Howards ;
Boldly, and with quick despatch, he’ll execute
the pleasing function
For transient glory of a fiver. Who else in
daring earnest will essay the task ?
We wait the hour. We wait the man. For
echoing songs shall glorify the hero
That shares the fame of Phillip. Worthy alike
of our appreciation
And swift Imperial honor is he who lifts the
torch to convict days.
Sydney.
D. HEALEY.

Melville Stoltz’s company opened at Syd-
ney Royal on Saturday with a great boom. All
the sitting and standing room was occupied, and
by 7.40 the management was reluctantly turning
away good dollars and cents at the front door,
and by 7.55 the American eagle had screamed
himself hoarse explaining things to people who
wanted to get in and couldn’t. The eo. on the
whole justified the boom. Some of it is very bad,
but the most of it is above the average. Vir-
ginia Aragon, the wire-walker, is probably the
cleverest performer of the crowd, though the
Jordans are very close up. This lady walks on
her wire, waltzes and step-dances on it, canters
back and forward along it, hops along it on one
foot, lies down on it, and has sprints and jumps
and convulsions on it, and altogether conducts
herself in a dreadfully reckless fashion at her
giddy elevation. She is a graceful little creature
with the bright, wide smile of the profession, but
as she doesn’t say anything it is impossible to
decide whether she talks through her nose like the
rest of the co. or not. Regarded as an individual
who earns her humble crust on a thread up near
the roof, Miss Virginia is an unqualified success.
The five Flying Jordans are remarkably clever
trapezists, and the triple somersault which the
most utterly reckless Jordan of the crowd turns in
mid-air while going from one end of the big
theatre to the other via the ceiling, is a really
wonderful performance. There is an entire ab-
sence of any effort about their acts ; they skim
through all manner of remarkable doings in the
off-hand fashion of a syndicate dashing off a lying
prospectus about its reef.
* * * •
The Jordans and Miss Aragon put together
lift Brother Stoltz’s show out of the ruck, and
make it something above the ordinary level. The
two Marios, though decidedly clever acrobats, are
less novel ; they are almost exact duplicates of
Wilton and Stack, lately at the Palace. They do
the same tricks to a hair, and the smaller man
once missed his footing on Saturday and sat down
suddenly, which was exactly what the smaller
man of the other pair did on his first night in
Sydney. Also, he sat down on the same side
of the stage with a whack which sounded just
like, the other man’s whack preserved in
spirits. Among the infantry funny men of
the show, the Olifans are really funny, and
have a lot of original ideas. John Burke is
moderately amusing ; John Howard is just good
enough to escape immediate execution and that is
all; and the remaining humorist, Petrie, is dreary
beyond description. Each of these hilarious per-
sons is accompanied by a lady, and they ask the
lady conundrums about soiled underclothing, and
things like that. At least, one shriekful comedian
asks his. particular lady how long her brother
wears his shirt, and she replies “ About three
days,” so The Bulletin assumes that the shirt
will be considerably soiled by that time. If Brother
Stoltz doesn’t want to bring his country into con-
tempt he should suppress this unpleasant in-
formation about how long an American lady
thinks a shirt ought to be worn. The first-night
audience kept up a roar of applause right through,
but the d.c. in this country is a trifle particular
about its shirt, and when it has more time to
think over this item it may cool down somewhat.
** * *
“Skipped by the Light of the Moon ” is
going at Sydney Palace in the presence of
slim audiences. There is very little en-
thusiasm about the individual either, and
so far “Skipped” has fallen flat. The co. isn’t
so good as the one which expounded the griefs
of Obadiah Dingle and Felix Crackle in the days
of the Gourlay and Walton combination, and
the stage is so small that it can’t riot round in
the irresponsible fashion that the case requires.
“Skipped” is mostly riot and sprint, and as
the co. hasn’t room to stretch its legs to the utmost
Gourlay’s musical comedy loses a lot of its force.
When Policeman MTntyre furiously pursues the
two fallen roysterers round about the oyster-stall,
he can’t let himself go for all he is worth for lack of
space ; he has to spurt cautiously round the corners
of the stall for fear of raking down the side-scenes
with his feet; and in many other ways the show
suffers from scarcity of accommodation. Obadiah
Dingle is as bright as ever ; and there are many
good points about Felix Crackle and MTntyre ;
also Miss Dora Mostyn, as Mrs. Dingle, has a lot
to recommend her. As to the rest of the co., this
paper drops a silent tear and heaves them into
well-deserved oblivion.
** * *
The “Prisoner of Zenda ” closes on Friday
night after a run of only three weeks, and the
“ Sign of the Cross ” follows on Saturday. It is
a dangerous thing to venture into the regions of
prophecy, but The Bulletin hereby risks a copper
on its conviction that the “Sign ” will either be a
very big success or a monumental frost. There is
hardly anything that is more uncertain than the
religious drama ; even a red-headed woman or a
stiffened horse or a Reid surplus is reliable by
comparison. There are times when a hysterical
city will pawn its boots to see a religious drama,
and there are other times when it is unanimously
not taking any. The religious sentiment of any
community has more ebbs and flows, more cold fits
and hot ones, and more intervals of blank indiffer-
ence in between than anything else. Or, to be
more accurate, it is not so much a case of having
occasional fits of religious enthusiasm, as of the
community getting occasionally into a receptive
condition which may develope into religious
enthusiasm, or a Soudan Contingent, or a desire
to hang Butler without trial, according to which
inducement happens first. Anyhow, this paper
offers its best wishes for the new venture, and ad-
vises the management to work the advertising
parson vigorously.
** * *
A week of “Private Secretary” is to
follow the present outbreak of “ Charley’s Aunt.”
The reverend gentleman with the cold feet arrives
to-night (Saturday), and his hilarious bath-bun —
the most interesting piece of pastry m modern
drama—has already been shifted down to the
Criterion on a dray in readiness for the occasion.
Manager Lohr has also overhauled the good little
man’s goloshes, so that everything is in an ad-
vanced state of preparation.
** * *
Phil. Newbury is making a longer stay at
Sydney Tivoli than originally intended. The
rotund Philip’s beautiful voice has proved a
powerful attraction; the upper-crust of Sydney
society may not be really musical, but the lower
and cheaper section of it, when some genuine
melody is thrown at in between the burnt-cork
man and the horizontal bar person, appreciates
with all its might. Otherwise, Rickards’ show is
in its ordinary robust state, and is paying shixty
per shent dividends as usual.
** * *
James MacMahon, who has more wiles
concealed under his big hat than there are in a
truck-load of monkeys, was the one Sydney show-
man who made anything out of Hospital Saturday.
He handed over the whole establishment to seven
or eight beautiful collectors and only stipulated
for half the takings, and all day they ran the pas-
sing population into James’s cinematograph for all
they were worth. Naturally these enthusiasts
told everybody that James’s “cin.” was the only
genuine one in the city, and that the rival ones
were only fit to be burnt by the common hangman.
Therefore James smiles underneath the big hat
and is content.
** * *
At Melbourne Bijou the setting up of
Nemesis ” in the place of “The Beggar Student”
is a satisfactory change. Ramie’s musical farce
dates back to an olden time, but the harmless
little joke has not outlived its usefulness ; also the
music, which was selected from various popular
composers in the first instance, is none the worse
for wear nowadays. Another enduring feature of
“ Nemesis ” is the ballet. The ballet ladies
perform one or two irrelevant gambols in
garments that commend them to favorable
notice, and give full play to the imagination
of a grateful public. The ballet is a com-
plete success. Bracy, the automatic and ever-
youthful tenor, provides a fragile plot for “ Neme-
sis ” by becoming engaged to two girls whose
respective fathers glare at one another across the
top of a garden wall, and carry on a somewhat
comic struggle for the coveted son-in-law. Some-
times the rival parents suspend hostilities and
burst into song, assisted by Bracy and the rival
girls. Finally a second young man comes along
to take one of the damsels off the other fellow’s
hands, and although the new arrival is pal-
pably a female in disguise, the opera, or
whatever it may be, appears to end quite
happily. John Forde and Knight Aston per-
form the pair of rampagious fathers between
them, whilst Miss Mabelle Darley and Miss Julia
Spencer play the tuneful daughters in a fresh and
hugsome spirit, Miss Darley, as the prima donna,
being rather more tuneful and hugsome than her
fair competitor. An awful blot upon Saturday’s
performance was a chorus (unaccompanied) of
waiters, from which the discordant notes of the
chief offender rang out until the audience added a
laugh to the tumult. “ Nemesis ”is preceded by
an operetta, and Mr. Rebottaro, a presumably
Italian gentleman, takes the chief part in this
unimportant trouble.
** * *
Mrs. Potter and Bellew started their final
week at Melb. Princess’s with the old story of
how Shylock was victimised by a legal quibble
and reduced to the awful necessity of embracing
Christianity to save his life. It was always a
painful narrative, even when Shylock used to
appear as a greasy, ranting Fagin in a second-
hand gaberdine. Bellew’s performance (copied
from Irving) heaps further infamy upon those
laws of ancient Venice, whereby a justly-irritated
financier of most gentlemanly character and con-
spicuous eloquence when roused could be robbed
of his revenge, his ducats, and his religion at one
fell swoop, simply because he didn’t consult a
solicitor before entering into a bargain with the
Christian larrikin who had voided rheum upon his
beard. The poetic injustice of Shylock’s downfall
was feelingly expressed in last Saturday’s show
and the unfortunate apostle of Thrift‘left the
court accompanied by The Bulletin s sympathv.
Mrs. Potter, Cecil Ward, Miss Ireland, Keigbtley
and others concerned in the shameful attack upon
a picturesque Hebrew, did their best to put their
Christian meanness in a becoming Shakespearian
fight. Probably they were successful. Anyway
a crowded audience didn’t hoot them. It is one
of the peculiarities of classic plays that nobody
seems to take an intelligent interest in the plot or
care a button about what happens to the hero
Ihe average patron is content to sit there and
listen to measured diction as though the immortal
tragedy were an organ recital.
* * * »
The effect of Mrs. Potter in a rapid round
of plays is as variable as the success of the com-
pany generally. Bellew, alone, appears pretty
well suited to his character every time. Mrs.
Potter and the others are good, bad, or indifferent
in their acting, just according to how they barmen
to be placed, with the result that two amateur
critics, discussing the merits of the co. from two
different experiences, snort scornfully at one
another’s notions of histrionic art. “ Francillon ”
showed all, or nearly all, of them to advantage.
“The Ironmaster” showed them mostly other-
wise.
• • * •
Mrs. Potter, likewise Bellew, present
themselves in “David Garrick” and “Caval-
leria ” to-night (Thursday), and to-morrow they
will positively farewell as Juliet and Romeo, so
that the last curtain of the season may descend
upon their double suicide.
* * * *
“ Lelamine ” follows this solemn event on
Saturday. “ Lelamine ” has been looked for with
considerable interest and grave misgiving. It
is an untried comic opera in three acts,
and the company engaged to work it for
six long nights is so strictly unprofessional that
the Earl of Shaftesbury has graciously consented
to play the leading tenor part. A chorus of 80
voices and a reliable amateur ballet will assist this
desperate enterprise, which is in aid of the Society
for Prevention of Cruelty to Children. Therefore
children— The Bulletin presumes—will not be
admitted on the opening night. It were kinder
for the management to wait and see whether the
entertainment is likely to give them pain.
** * *
Nothing has inclined to blight the trium-
phant career of Bland Holt’s latest production at
the Royal. The Panther still entertains the cream
of the demi-monde in her gilded den, and her hus-
band goes on firing the premises as before. Like-
wise the gas is always turned off at the very
moment when that poor inebriate seems about to
brain Cosgrove with a bottle. “In Sight of St.
Paul’s ’ ’ is not a drama that deserves to be studied
three nights in succession for the sake of its literary
merit, but it is certainly a thing to be seen about
once a month, and regarded as an extraordinary
spectacle at the prices.
** * *
In a casual way The Bulletin calls atten-
tion to the fact that the Rickards Variety Co.
prospers as usual at Melb. Opera House. The old
notification has grown so monotonous that it is
occasionally omitted from these columns, in which
cases silence can be accepted as meaning the same
thing. Chirgwin, Jessie de Grey, Whitburn, and
Bellman and Moore are principal names on the
programme just now, the very latest name being
Pat Melton, who seems to have arrived direct
from London, and can show reasonable cause for
his present engagement.
** * *
Charlotte Hemmings’ benefit-concert, atthe
Masonic Hall, provided a large display of enthu-
siasm, floral tributes and powdered shoulders;
another conspicuously naked item being a very
exalted magnate’s shiny head. The benefitted
one marked her gratilude to the Irish party by
giving as encores three foine pieces with a “ divil
mend the rest ” abandon. Regina Nagel, sore-
throated and distressingly nervous, left the critics
concerned about the weakness of her medium
notes. Colonel Bingham simply executed “ Kil-
larney,” Signorina Vollugi let off her high-class
but decidedly hard soprano in Italian opera style,
and showed disposition to laugh outright when
the sassiety Johnny, who was obligating her
second number, broke a string, and dived precipi-
tately from the platform. The obligatist, by the
way, secured another instrument, and turned up
again like a bad penny before the singer had quite
finished. Gladstone Wright, thinner and younger-
than of yore, gave the prologue from
Pagliacci ” with all due force and expression.
Later on, the returned wanderer shared with
Walter Kerby the task of singing the fashionable
mob off the premises with a duet which didn’t
start until long past the usual closing time for
concerts.
* * * *
Miss Hemmings, who is starting business
as a reciter in London, seems to be a much more
promising exportation than Joe Blascheck. Miss
Hemmings has a strong, melodious voice, bright
elocution, and a turn for humor, which is such a
rare thing in women that she is obliged to let her-
self oiU upon subjects intended for male treat-
ment. The supply of funny female recitations is
very limited. When she has somewhat sharpened
and polished her accents this entertaining Vic-
torian girl should—in the language of farewell
banqueteers—“ do credit to the province at ’Ome.”
** # *
A year ago The Bulletin published a
photo, of Miss Regina Nagel (the warbler of
Ben Bolt, in Trilby ”), with sundry remarks
on her splendid vocal possibilities. Nagel the
Nervous is no nearer to what she might be as a
contralto singer than she was then. After several
months of Ben Bolting around Westralia the mis-
guided damsel turned up again in Melbourne the
other night, at Charlotte Henllnings , concert, and
sang a couple of songs in spiritless style, holding
her notes back as though she were afraid of falling
over in the attempt to do herself justice. And
yet she did well enough to fetch an encore. Miss
Nagel needs a Svengalic enthusiast to take her in
hand. Properly trained and encouraged, her ab-
normal voice would rain “rubies, and diamonds
ami pearls ” for herself and manager, but mean-
while she wastes her time in hardening the voice
to a bad method of production and giving her
services to benefit programmes. The Melbourne
public soon loses respect for any promising voice
that performs gratis.

Item from Wide Bay (Q.). Twenty-eight
head of fine cattle were safely conveyed from
Fraser’s Island and landed on the north head of
the Mary River. They stampeded into the water
and swam towards south head. Two incomine
steamers and one outgoing ditto were passing and
the cattle swam for them and got smashed to bits
Only four escaped.
The Tasmanian delegates put in a day or
two in Melbourne before returning to their caves
in the island of political troglodytes, and seeine
several of them together most people took them
for the Eight Hours’ “ identities ” out for an
airing. Two only of the Raspberry contingent
have not run to the sear and yellow, the others
range from 82 to 68. Politically, the tight little
island is prematurely grey.
The recent collapse at the Junction mine, Broken Hill
so scared some of the miners that they attended the Salva-
tion Army in the evening and became converts.
Though the sorrow and compunction
Of the miners of the Junction
For their lives mis-spent and sinful was the fruit
of sudden scare,
Yet no more in godless revel
They’ll indulge, and the grim Devil
Will be mad when for their souls he comes, with
saucer eyes aglare,
And finds them all at prayer !
Oscar Wilde was not released from prison
a few weeks ago, as stated. Latest advices give the
converted sensualist a prospect of freedom this
month (May), when he will, perhaps, make up his
mind not to leave England after all. Oscar’s new
life is an interesting subject for conjecture. In his
old days he scoffed at the idea of “ taking exercise
or becoming a useful member of society,” but now,
after a period of compulsory exercise and prison
discipline, it is possible that he has developed a
grand ambition to turn his talents to the benefit
of British literature. Who shall say what effect
such a punishment has had upon such a man ?
“Ernie” Parker (managing partner of
Huddart, Parker and Co.), speaking at the
luncheon given on board H.P. & Co.’s new S.B.
Westralia in Melbourne the other day :—“Onsuch
an occasion as this I must express the gratitude I
feel towards Sir Frederick Sargood, for I entered
the firm of Sargood, Butler, and Ewen as a boy,
and whatever success I have achieved in life I
owe to that gentleman.” In other words, had
there been no Sargood there would have been no
steam-co. manager Parker. Which explodes, at
one fell pop, the theory that dapper Ernie rose to
eminence on the strength of his moustache. Un-
less, of course, it was Sargood who gave the
moustache its early commercial training.
Some day the Westralian post and tele-
graph department will assuredly fall down and
break its neck through excessive velocity. Not
long since a Condon man wired to a friend at
Cossack that the local steamer was just leaving
for that port. It was an aged and rather asth-
matic steamer, and the telegram had 2h hours
start on a 200-mile journey, nevertheless the vessel
wheezed in abreast of the message and they made
a dead heat of it. At Roebourne the post office
put up a still better record by successfully deliver-
ing on 20th March, ’97, a letter postmarked “Lon-
don, 31st March ’96.” The W.A. department isn’t
at all inflated by these achievements; it does them
with the unobtrusive dignity of an office which
could be a great deal slower still if it wanted to.
Accompanying are designs of the new
N.S.W. postage-stamps, which represent pious
Postmaster Joseph Cook’s own particular little
grovel in the dust of the record-reign. It isn’t
officially known whether the old original pious
Joseph ever put Mrs. Potiphar’s head on a stamp
when he was member of the Egyptian Cabinet,
but, if he did, the stamp was probably a much
more attractive one than Joe Cook’s. Mr. D. H.
Souter, the designer—chief artist of John Sands
and Co.—has on the 2d. stamp actually and
artistically represented Queen Victoria in all her
native ugliness ; and that 2d. stamp is one of the
best arguments yet advanced for the abolition
of monarchy.
