The WILD CAT
COLUMN
Premier Reid has made a statement of the
N.S.W. finances as he expects them to be for the year
ending 30th June next. The estimate is as follows
Expenditure ... ... ... £‘9,635,718
Revenue ... ... ... ... 9,306,669
Deficiency ... ... ... £229,049
This is the result, after collecting two years’ income-tax
in one year. Under ordinary circumstances, with only
one year’s income-tax, the shortage would be £160,000
more, or £389,049. Premier Reid, however, puts it dif-
ferently, thus: —
Revenue ... ... ... ... £9,306,669
Last year’s alleged surplus ... ... 333,514
£9,640,183
Expenditure ... ... ... 9,535,718
Surplus ... ... ... ... £104,465
This is a gross outrage on the famous “cash
basis ” system by which each year’s revenue and expendi-
ture was to stand by itself. Also, by this device, the
alleged surplus of last year is counted twice over. Premier
Reid’s logic is that he had last year a surplus of £333,000.
This year he spends £229,000 of it, leaving only £104,000 ;
therefore he again has a surplus of £104,000. And even
by his own mode of calculation he would have a deficit
but for the expedient of collecting two years’ income-
tax in 12 months. Yet his press supporter, the S.M.
Herald, remarks proudly that there have been “ two
successive years with surpluses”—two surpluses, be it
observed ; not merely one which has already been mostly
used up—and that “ his pledge that he would never be
associated with a deficit has, so far, been faithfully kept.”
Just how it has been kept is shown by Coghlan (Statisti-
cal Register for ’95, pages 24 and 25), and by the present
estimate :
Expenditure. Revenue. Deficit.
1895 (* year) ... £4,844,299 £4,478,515 £365,784
1896 . ... 9,524,343 9,059,607 464,736
1897 (estimate)... 9,535,718 9,306,669 229,049
£23,904,360 £22,844,791 £1,059,569
Premier Reid has put in three financial terms of office,
and presented three financial statements. Two of them,
according to the State statistics published by his own
Government, showed a huge deficit. The third, by his
own estimate, shows a deficit also. The total shortage,
as shown above, is £1,059,569 for years. And Premier
Reid, in the face of his own published statistics and his
own estimate for the current year, has denied the exist-
ence of every one of his three deficits, and pounds a
paean of joy on his own inflated stomach about his
“ surplus.” The Bulletin fully recognises that deficits
will come now and then in times of special depression,
but anybody who was worth calling a Treasurer would
acknowledge them when they come, and not lie about
them and attempt to juggle them away.
• * * »
The same authority (Coghlan s statistics for
1895, pages 24 and 25) tells this story about the late Dibbs
Administration :
Expenditure. Revenue. Surplus.
1892 . £9,931,411 £10,065,602 £134,191
1893 . 9,931,658 9,499,370 (def.) 432,288
1894 ..t 9,178,706 9,300,711 122,005
£29,041,775 £28,865,683 (def.) £176,092
It thus appears, on the evidence of the official statistics
issued from a Government department by a Government
officer, that Dibbs, in three years, compiled a deficit of
£176,092. Also, it is shown by the same authority as
concerns 1895 and 1896, and by his own estimate quoted
above as regards 1897, that Reid in 2£ years piled up a
deficit of £1,059,569. As the Reid Government has never
sacked Statistician Coghlan for gross misrepresentation,
or called his figures in question, The Bulletin presumes
they are correct. Therefore, the country is invited to
consider the picture of the man with the one million
deficit talking pityingly about the finance of the man whose
deficit was less than one-fifth that amount; and howling
ceaselessly about the “ Dibbs deficit,” and calling Dibbs’s
£176,000 a shortage of a million, and describing his own
million shortage as a surplus. Also, Reid’s press sup-
porters, the Herald and Telegraph and Evening News,
howl in exactly the same key. And by dint of much
howling all these authorities have actually persuaded the
country to believe that Reid has a big surplus and that
Dibbs had a huge deficit, the officially published evidence
to the contrary notwithstanding. As an instance of
what unlimited cheek and boundless stomach can do in
the face of adverse circumstances, this is a unique record.
* * • *
Premier Reid, who insists on saying that he
has a surplus of £104,000-though the accounts only
show that he has that amount remaining out of last
year’s alleged surplus of £333,ooo—adds that he is
going to save £250,000 of expenditure next year, and to
levy £lOO,OOO more taxation (by increasing the probate
duties), so his next year’s surplus should, by his own mode
of computation, be £454,000. Query : If G. Reid has any
faith in his £104,000 surplus this year, and he is going to
save £250,000 next year, which would make the surplus
for’9B £354,000, what does he want the additional £lOO,OOO
taxation for, seeing that he doesn’t propose to give up
borrowing and to construct public works out of revenue ?
* * * *
Sydney Telegraph, paving the way for a whoop
•next July, remarks that this is the “ test year” of Free-
trade finance. That is just the one thing which it cer-
tainly is not. This year’s revenue will include two years’
income-tax and receipts from a number of Protective
■duties which are being gradually extinguished, but which
•won't finally disappear for several years. When Reid
gets down to his own tariff, and presents a year’s accounts
with only one year’s taxes in them and with none of the
mysterious “fakes” he has hitherto indulged in, then
the test will come, but not till then.
* * * *
N.S.W. revenue for the 10 months of the finan-
cial year (up to end of April): Revenue, £7,540,424 ; ex-
penditure, £8,115,278; deficiency so far, £574,854. A
month ago, at the end of March, the shortage was
£418,330, so Treasurer Reid has been going to the bad
lately at a high old rate. As he promises that the deficit
at the end of June will be only £229,000, there is a lot of
leeway to make lip in two months, or else a great deal of
hard misrepresentation to attend to.
N.S.W. Tax Commissioners report for the
severalth time (through the mouth of Premier Reid on
this occasion) that their policy of charging “arrears” of
income-tax is strictly in accordance with the Act. What
these monotonous commissioners never explain is—which
clause or clauses of the Act give them the authority they
claim to have. If they have anything like such a good
case as they profess to have, they should at least be able
to show where it comes in.
* * • •
Item from Premier Reid’s financial statement:
Large sums of money were sent over from Victoria to
buy these (N.S.W.) 3 per cents.” Yet Victorians only
subscribe for the 3 per cent, stock of their own State
Land Bank in very small sums. The moral is that a 3 per
cent, stock which has no definite currency and is liable to
be repaid any day isn’t popular; the investor doesn’t
want his money thrown back at him just when he thinks
it is safely planted. The sooner Vic. Government notes
this the better.
* * * *
Premier Seddon says that he will, probably this
year, give up the practice of borrowing the sinking-funds
on local loans for general revenue purposes, and make it
illegal by Act of Parliament. This particular borrow was
the shady, disreputable and shiftless device which Trea-
surer Ward introduced into Maoriland finance, and it
can t be suppressed too soon.
* * * *
According to the Maoriland balance-sheet now
published, the surplus for the year just ended is £125,000,
and £215,000 brought forward from previous surpluses
makes £340,000 in all. £200,000 goes towards public
works, and £50,000 for land purchase and improvement;
and the new year starts with a credit balance of £90,000.
Maoriland and Westralia are the only two provinces which
haven’t a huge accumulated deficit to struggle with. At
June, ’96, the (admitted) shortages were : Victoria,
£2,744,941 ; N.S.W., £2,270,452; S.A., £1,254,728; Tas-
mania, £840,257; Queensland, £292,972 (plus about IJ4
million turned into funded debt). And the lovely total
of the five deficits is £7,403,360.

Joyce, member for Lyttelton (M.L.), proposed
that Premier Seddon should spend part of his time in
London in selling the Bank of N.Z. bodily to English
capitalists. But no one took any notice of him except to
remark inwardly that Joyce was the prize fool of the
House.
* • * *
The Colonial Mutual Life Assurance Society
presents a seriously alarming balance-sheet, accompanied
by the usual congratulations about the progress of the
office. So far as mere revenue is concerned the progress
is right enough. Renewal premiums (£282,839) are £224
more than last year, which is a slight increase, though
not much. New premiums (£24,858) are £3491 more than
last year’s figures. The total income for the year is
£392,705, out of which £117,137 is added to the accu-
mulated funds, making them £2,040,972 —a very large
and solid-looking item. Expenses (£78,180) are equal to
nearly 20 per cent, on total income or 25 per cent, on
premiums—practically the same as last year, and a very
heavy percentage, though much less than the figures for
1894 and previous years. The total revenue is thus about
£lOOO less than it was last year ; the amount added to the
funds is about £13,000 less ; and the expense rate is just
now practically stationary.
These, however, are matters of comparatively
small importance, and show chiefly that times are bad
and competition severe. The utterly disastrous-looking
feature is the interest on investments. Last year
interest received amounted to £89,702. This year, with
£117,000 more funds, it is only £'81,147 —a decrease of
£8555. This means that the rate has fallen in one year
from £4 16s. per cent, to something like £4 ss. Two
years ago the rate of interest realised was over £5 per
cent. There has been a steady decline for a number of
years past, but in this respect the Colonial Mutual has
merely shared the experience of other offices ; it is the
unprecedented slump of the present year which is alarm-
ing. Such a fall, in most cases, signifies that there are
heavy losses not written off, that a considerable propor-
tion of assets represent only losses unprovided for, and
consequently return no interest, and thus drag down the
average. When the Mutual of Victoria had written down
its assets to what was considered strictly their present
value, they yielded £5 Is. 6d. per cent, interest,-and
if this test was applied to the Colonial Mutual it would
require to write down its assets by about £350,000. Last
year the Widows’ Fund realised £4 16s. 9d. per cent.—
considerably more than the Colonial Mutual does now.
Then it proceeded to write off losses and depreciation to
such an extent that the rate of interest on its investments,
at their written-down value, was over £5 6s. per cent. If
the Colonial Mutual applied this test to itself it
would mean writing off nearly £4so,ooo—more than one-
fifth of the entire assurance fund. It certainly looks
absurd, by comparison with the results of other offices,
to suppose that assets which only yield about 4} per cent,
are not put in the balance-sheet at inflated values. The
Colonial Mutual, in five years, has written off £13,299 to
cover bad debts, depreciation, and wear and tear on nearly
two millions worth of property, and has reserved another
£47,865 for the same purpose ; total provision for losses in
the most disastrous five years Australia ever saw. £61,164.
The Widows’ Fund, which is about two-thirds the size of
the Colonial Mutual, wrote off £125,000. The Mutual of
Victoria, which is not quite half the size, last year made
provision for losses to the extent of £102,000. Consider-
ing the internal evidence of the balance-sheet, it certainly
looks as if the Colonial Mutual was only playing with the
situation. If it isn’t, then it is the luckiest society on the
continent.
At the end of 1893 the Colonial Mutual had the
enormous sum of £55,743 “ interest and rents outstanding
and accrued.” This certainly looked as if a lot of the
securities had gone bad to such an extent that the interest
was practically irrecoverable —in which case the principal
is often more or less irrecoverable also. This was a far
worse showing than that of any other large assurance
office in Australasia and certainly suggested that the
Colonial Mutual was in a worse state than any of its lead-
ing contemporaries. The Bulletin drew attention
pointedly to these alarming figures, and thereafter the
item decreased so rapidly that at the end of 1895 it was
only £L7,705. (This year, by the way, it is only £16,178.)
This in itself was re-assuring. Then the Mildura trouble,
and a small dispute with the defunct Mercantile Finance,
Trustees and Agency Co. seemed to reveal as plainly as
possible the astounding circumstance that on certain
small advances, amounting to only about £31,000, the
arrears of interest due to the Colonial Mutual were no less
than £12,750. The Bulletin asked that society (in its
issues of May 9 and 23, 1896) if it really meant the public
to believe that, while there was £12,750 owing on £31,000
advances, the remaining £5OOO shown in the balance-
sheet covered all the rents and interest due and accrued
on the other £1,700,000 or so of investments. This impor-
tant question evoked no explanation. The inference
which many people will draw in the absence of explana-
tion is that the “ interest and rents outstanding and
accrued” as shown in the balance-sheet are not the
whole of these items (the society, by the way, doesn’t
say they are), and that the remainder are lumped
in with the advances. In that case no one can
tell how much the remainder is, or how good or bad
things may be.
The Bulletin knows nothing of the Colonial
Mutual’s affairs beyond what appears in the Mildura
liquidation report, in the reports of its case against the
Mercantile Finance, Trustees and Agency, and in its own
balance sheets. Among them, however, they suggest
that this big concern would be none the worse for suc-
cessfully passing through an independent State audit and
a valuation of its securities.
« # * *
The Metropolitan Bank (Melbourne) sheds a
gleam of light on the dreary path of liquidation. A year
ago the sad, busted Metropolitan owed £307,000; nowit
owes only i 260,000. It sold £18,183 worth of property
during- the year, and, as the property in question fetched
£5500 more than it was valued at in the books, the Metro-
politan is agreeably surprised. It has still 700 suburban
properties and 970 acres of country land, and by a judi-
cious process of swapping properties for deposit-receipts
it reckons that it will ultimately settle with all its credi-
tors and come out with a balance for distribution among
the shareholders. Under these circumstances, the Metro-
politan is getting so beastly inflated with pride that it
will hardly recognise another busted bank when they
meet in the street.
• • * •
Melbourne has five trustees’ and executors’ com-
panies, and only business enough for two or possibly
three, therefore the proposal to amalgamate the Per-
petual Executors and the Trustees, Executors and Agency
Co. is a decidedly good one. The Perpetual has £30,000
paid-up capital, no reserve-fund ; its shares (£1 paid up)
are practically unsaleable, and its last year’s profit (£460)
only equalled per cent, per annum. The T.E. and
A. Co. has £60,000 paid-up capital, £40,000 reserves ; its
£1 shares fetch 125., and its last year’s earnings (£3481)
were just about equal to 5£ per cent, on capital. The
Perpetual makes an enormous loss on its trustee business,
the expenses swallowing up almost all the interest on in-
vestments, while T.E. and A. makes very little. By com-
bining the two all the profits will remain while the ex-
penses should be reduced at least one-third, which would
mean making one good concern out of two bad ones.
* * * *
The full dimensions of the Queensland National
Bank trouble seem to be that, after all the writing-down
of capital and reserve funds, the assets are still £1,673,000
short of the liabilities, and the institution promises to
make this amount good out of future profits. Therefore,
if the Q.N.B. has £50,000 a year profit to apply to this
purpose, it will get square and become a solvent institu-
tion in 33 years—provided nothing else goes wrong in the
interval. Think of 33 years spent in working off a dead
horse—33 years of sackcloth and ashes and repentance
and strict sobriety ! In less than ten the Q.N.B. will be
tired of the weary contract, and will be howling to the
Boodlewraith’s grandson for State assistance.
* * * *
Sydney and Suburban Mutual Permanent
Building 'and Land Investment Association, Limited
(here this paper comes to the surface to breathe), issues a
desolate report. One of its principal assets is accumu-
lated loss £54,364, which is £1204 worse than it was a year
ago. As the capital is £98,542 this means that most of it
is already gone, and when things are realised the rest will
probably go too. The 3 per cent, deposits are offering at
10s. 9d. in the £ without results. In a year’s struggle
with its shareholders it managed to collect just £6l, and
it paid off about £llOO of deposits during the year, at
which rate it will finish settling up with its depositors in
1957. Altogether, the concern with the name that ex-
tends into the next world is having a bad time of it.
* * * •
The Druids’ “Art Union,” in Melbourne, has
thrown some much-needed light upon the length, breadth
and depth of the shilling-show ticket delusion. On the
morning of the draw it was officially announced that all
tickets would be regarded as sold, and therefore entitled
to a chance of winning the precious Golden Druid. The
draw revealed a sale of tickets up to 236,000 at least, so, if
the prizes be accepted at the Druids’ own valuation of
£l5OO, it appears that each shilling ticket represented ljd.
as a lottery-investment. One pound sterling risked in
Adams’ sweep is worth 15s. 6d more than the same coin
squandered upon 20 “ bob ” chances in the Gala gamble.
The Druidical argument, that the show itself gives a fair
return for the ticket, doesn’t answer the objection that
not more than 50,000 tickets were presented at the gates
during Easter week. Something like 200,000 tickets, it
would seem, were bought by the public for the sake,
solely, of their gambling value, which was unknown at
the time, but is now proved to have been each, or less.
200,000 tickets at retail price represents £lO,OOO, and that
amount invested in an Adams sweep would have returned
£9OOO to the g.p.—(g.p. stands for general public and also
for general paralysis, which means idiocy.) The Bulletin
now awaits with interest a statement of the amount re-
turned to public charities by the Gala management.
* * * *
Will someone explain ? In Adelaide wheat is
quoted ss. 4d. a bushel, flour £l2 ss. to £l3 per ton ; in
Sydney, same date, wheat is 4s. 4d. and flour £l2 10s.
Does gristing cost more in N.S.W. ? Of course there is
not such a thing as a millers’ ring or trust. Freetrade
was to make that impossible—“ are not our markets open
to the world?” But it works out curiously. In one pro-
vince the farmer gets 4s. 4d., in the other ss. 4d. for his
wheat, and in both the consumer pays the same price per
loaf.

Victorian State Land Bank has just made its
third issue (£20,200) of 3 per cent, bonds. The public
subscribed £7150, and the Savings Bank took the other
£13,050. Last issue the public took £3200 only, so it
would seem that the new investment is growing in popu-
lar favor.

The erection of a lager-beer brewery in the
neighbourhood of Sydney inspires the question : what sort
of Freetrade is it which imposes 6d. per gallon on imported
beer and will only charge 3d. excise on this locally-made
article, so that the local industry is protected to the ex-
tent of 3d. per gallon ?

The striking absurdity among daily papers’
mining notes at present is the booming of the B.H. Ex-
tended—as before said—a little lead show several miles
out of Broken Hill. The directors are every few days re-
porting this wonderful thing or that marvel about the
deceptively-named concern ; but the crisis was possibly
reached when, the other day, it was announced that, in
consequence of the extraordinary developments, two
more miners were wanted !

“ Rackarock ” says the writer’s reference to the
Hongkong co. operating at Mt. M'Donald, N.S.W., is not
quite correct. It seems it is working a mine on a different
line of reef to that on which much money was wasted by
a former Hongkong co. But is it not practically the
same co. ? Surely Mt. M'Donald cannot boast of two Hong-
kong mining co’s. Is it that, having failed to make the old
Balmoral mine pay, the co. reconstructed and is now try-
ing to pull out with another show in the same camp? If
so, The Bulletin sincerely wishes it luck.

“But for the depression created by the war-
scare, Great Boulder shares,” says a London paper,
“ would be standing at £20.” Rot 1 Why shouldn’t the
war-scare similarly affect all other big mines ? It is not
owned by Greeks or worked by Turks. It is not the little
war that has begun, but the big W.A. boom that has col-
lapsed, which has brought these shares to a fair value,
and as soon as the developments of the mine warrant it
they will go to £2O with or without any war-scare, and
not before.

A Dr. Laver furnished Menzies Miner with a
glowing account of the marvellous richness of the Mount
Margaret mines : “At Crawford’s Patch the surface stuff
even is very rich, and the owners of it have asked £25,000
for the claim. The Great Britain has been taken down
50ft., and the shaft discloses a reef 50ft. wide with good
gold throughout. The richness of this country is really
not known. Of course, lam interested in it, but I assure
you I have not exaggerated its value one iota.”

* * * *
The city of Auckland, M.L., has just been pass-
ing through the phases of a well-defined boom. It started
some two years ago, with the discovery that cyanide was
adapted to the working of the various long-neglected
gold-reefs in Coromandel Peninsula. The whole country-
side was pegged off, and every available chunk thrown at
the head of Bull; the great majority of Auckland’s
citizens occupied themselves in buying and selling one
another’s shares—with great mutual benefit —and declar-
ing that in ten years Auckland would be the boss city of
Australasia. Hopes and house-rents rose enormously.
Dozens of bank-clerks started in business as brokers;
plans were prepared for a new £15,000 Stock Exchange.
For 18 months the town painted itself rose-pink, with
spots of acute scarlet. Then, of course, the end came.
The shares had been passed round till they couldn’t go
any further. Now they’re stuck. Some of the brokers
have already left for parts unknown ; others would like to
go if they could. Men who, six months ago, were fabu-
lously wealthy—on paper—now find it hard to pay their
calls. The new Exchange, if built, will probably be
utilised as a Salvation Barracks or Diamond Jubilee Home
for Incurables. The town, as a whole, is now occupied in
holding its head together and asking itself, “ Where was
I all last night ? ”

Society
Chief Justice Dobson, of Van Dobson
Land :
Her Majesty was a good woman, she had all the charac-
ter that constituted it, and on that quality her higher
qualities had been based. There was no great accident or
ill but you found her sending messages or telegrams giving
expression to her womanly sympathy. Some years ago,
when he was in England, and suffered something in a
railway accident —it was not much —he received two tele-
grams from Her Majesty and one from her Private Secre-
tary, General Ponsonby, inquiring about his state. And,
a few months later on, when he had the honor to appear
before her in person to receive from her the honor of
knighthood, seeing his arm in a sling, her first regard was
for his health. She expressed her regret that he had suf-
fered so long, and hoped he would suffer no permanent
injury. The woman first was manifested, and then the
sovereign. It was this character which made her so be-
loved by her subjects.
The question that comes in here is : Did Victoria
take a direct personal interest in Judge Dobson of
Van Dobson Land, whom she didn’t know from
a crow ? Or does secretary Ponsonby simply enter
in his big book the name of any distinguished
official who arrives in England so as to keep track
of him, and did he add a memo. “ damaged in
railway accident” in a column headed “remarks,”
and get a sub-flunkey to order a sub-sub-flunkey
to send perfunctory telegrams as a matter of
course? And when Dobson of Van Dobson Land
was about to be “ presented,” did the secretary
put the memo. “ damaged on railway ” against
his name on Her Majesty’s list of engagements
so that she might know what to say to him ? Dob-
son’s self-satisfied assumption that Victoria carried
his bruise in her head rests on no legal evidence
at all. A lady who receives so many hundreds of
loyal subjects can hardly be expected to remember
which one was smashed on the railway and which
was run over by a fish-cart.

He hoped they would walk circumspectly while in Eng-
land, and show people there that they had been trained

would, in his opinion, be the Queen’s Life Guards, and he
was sure they would act vigilant!y in that capacity. They

They must therefore be on the alert, and see that no harm
came to her Majesty.
Arise, Sir Joseph—or whatever he is. Probably
Jedediah.
Butler arrived in Sydney on Wednesday
afternoon last, and the evening papers of that date
and all the metropolitan papers of the following
day were filled with astoundingly sensational and
prejudicial yarns relating to his case. On Friday,
however, the daily press dropped Butler entirely—
not one word concerning him appeared in print.
The reason for this sudden change is that on
Thursday the editors of two Sydney papers had an
interview with the Attorney-General, who asked
them plainly whether they really wanted to go to
gaol. They laughed, but were told that it was no
laughing matter; that the conduct of the press
with regard to the accused was outrageous ; and
that King Jack was thinking of proceeding against
them for contempt in prejudicing the fair trial of
accused. Hence the Friday’s silence. Under the
circumstances, Butler’s attorney will have strong
grounds for applying for a long postponment of
the trial.
A little story in this connection. In Sergeant
Ballantine’s reminiscences it is related that Justice
Hawkins, after a prisoner had been acquitted by
the jury, remarked to the man that he might con-
sider himself lucky, as he (the Judge) had before
him on the bench a record of many previous con-
victions against him. Here an official whispered
to the Judge that the acquitted one had yet to be
tried on other charges. Whereupon the Judge
apologised, and said that the trial would be post-
poned, and that accused would meanwhile be
liberated on bail. So keenly did Judge Hawkins
feel the injustice he had unwittingly done that in
the end the man was not further tried at all.
• If we are going to have civilised law, all right.
But if we are going to have lynch-law, let us
grease the rope and ring the bell for the hanging
of some editors. Also, some advertising deteo-
tives.
* * * •
Considering that Butler has been a con-
siderable expense to the N.S.W. Treasury, and
that it is a fair thing to get the money back if
possible, The Bulletin suggests that he should be
tried in the Theatre Royal, with a full orchestra,
admission 10s., 7s. 6d., and ss. It could appear on
the programme that he was tried “by arrange-
ment with William Holloway, Esq.,” as almost
all sensational dramas are produced nowadays by
arrangement with Billolloway. The jury could
be ranged along the wings, and the Judge could
sit with his back to the bit of mountain scenery
which Westmacott used in the “ Enemy’s Camp ”
—that being sufficiently like the Blue Mountains
to pass muster ; and Butler could be confined in
the stage-box that Attorney-General Want had
the long litigation with Moss about. When any
particularly horrifying exhibit was held up, such
as the clothes found in Lee Weller’s grave, the big
drum could give a soulful bang, and there might
be an anguished wail on the violins and a howl
from the trombone. This would emphasise the
horrifying incident. Then the detective could
give his evidence to slow music played in
a breathless undertone, and when he came to the
place where the body was dug up the wind instru-
ments could blow a sudden shriek of agony, fol-
lowed by that impressive dead silence in which
you can hear your hair growing. Then as the
other detective stepped in to give evidence about
Butler’s arrest in San Francisco Bay, a new back-
cloth should come down—the one with the ocean
view in “ Saved from the Sea ; ” and when he ex-
plained how the accused was handed over by the
Californian authorities there would be a pause
while the band played “ Taken from the County
Jail.” If Butler is tried without an orchestra to
throw in the musical accompaniment, it will be a
great waste of effect. Think how much more im-
pressive the burial certificate of the alleged victim
would be if, just as it was handed in, 40 instru-
ments struck up a fierce roar to the tune of “ John
Brown’s body ! ”
NOT TO BE INFERRED.
HE : “ She looks very innocent .”
SHE: “ Oh, yes; but you mustn’t think she’s green just
because she is a grass widow
S.M. Herald’s elephantine effort to gush
over the vice-regal wedding was a most dismal
failure. The “ leading daily’s ” long yarn allegedly
descriptive of the procession and ceremony seemed
to be the work of a set of police-court shorthanders
whose every attempt at ‘ ‘ splurge ” ended up in a
soul-jarring anti-climax. For instance: “The
bride’s toilette was” —well, what do you think
came next? Well, it was “arranged so as to
heighten her beauty. ” At which point that par-
ticular police-court reporter evidently fell down
dead, leaving the great task to be continued by
the next “ Fined AOs.-or-seven-days ” litterateur.
But the Herald’s weekly edition, the Mail,
isn’t built altogether that way. Hark :
Paint to yourselves a woman in the bright light of youth,
traced with aristocratic lines—woven of the ruddy rose
and favorite lily—give the finest proportion to her form,
let a soft and happy smile play around her lips with every
charm tempered with dignity ; paint all this, and yet you
have scarcely a shadow of the bride as she was seen on
her wedding-day in her shimmering robe of satin, which
became her happy face so well. It was not only the varie-
gated colors of the flags, the cheerful sounds, and the
warm breezes of the air. ... It was a true wedding-
day, a day with the sun’s warm rays shining on every-
thing as it passed, and making the uniforms, jewels, and
pretty faces glitter like the morning stars, so full of life
and splendor and joy. The winds had not started work,
and the air (? bed) was well shaken and tempered even to
suit the most fastidious individual.
“ The most fastidious individual ’’—alas, The
Bulletin fears that it was the police-court re-
porter after all.
* * * *
On the whole, The Bulletin is sorry for
Sydney’s statue-boom. The latest effigies set up,
those of Dailey and Phillip, are as unpoetic as the
average Ballarat statue. Anyone who asks him-
self “ What is Art ? ” can get some sort of reply by
first gazing at the unidealised presentment of
Dailey and then marching on to inspect the fine
bronzes of the Prince Consort and Queen Victoria
and those most charming of all Sydney street
ornaments, the - lamp-statues outside the Hotel
Australia.
* * * *
“ At Bundaberg (Q.),” writes a Bulletin
correspondent, “the cheap and reliable Kanaka is
going round town blowing a powdery substance
into white women’s eyes as they pass by. Said
powder is intended as a love-philter, and is used
as such at the islands. Whether it has proved
successful in Bundaberg is not yet known, but a
friend of mine was a few days ago appealed to by
a respectable girl for escort home, alleging that
she was overcome by the smell of the stuff blown
into her nostrils by a Kanaka as she was going
home from work.”
* * * *
A bit of local color from a Melbourne
drowning report: “ The deceased wore a black
skirt, a red silk blouse, and a green straw hat
trimmed with blue ribbon and with a pink flower.
A small yellow dog, which she had left on the
bank, whined piteously, and refused to leave the
body.” There is a lot of pathos in it, and yet, if
the present writer ever drowns himself in a green-
and-blue-and-pink hat and a red blouse, he trusts,
with all due respect to the dead —which will be
himself—that a dog of some more subdued tint
will be waiting for him on the bank when he is
fished out.

A bewchus swindler, convicted recently in
Melb. of obtaining cash by false-pretences from a
girl he had become engaged to under equally men-
dacious circumstances, had been but six weeks
out from England, yet, when his baggage was
searched, no less than 33 love-letters from local
women bearing eight different signatures were dis-
covered. In the same box, half-a-dozen photos,
of Australian damsels of various shapes, sizes and
ages were unearthed with the device “ With love
from Tottie,” etc., on the back of each.

“ Hermes ” to The Bulletin re
the bowelless parents who outrage help,
less children : A woman, hurrying
across Hyde Park (Sydney) to catch
a ’bus, beat a little tot of about five
years in the face till the flesh looked
like liver and the little nose streamed
with blood ; all because the poor mite
could not run as fast as the female
animal that was apparently her
mother. Another hunk of female bru-
tality beat a boy of about seven years
over the head and shoulders with a
pebble - mounted umbrella - handle for
accidentally treading on her skirt. At
a band performance in Hyde Park a
she-devil, when her infant in arms
cried, covered its mouth with her great
paw and pinched its nostrils, so that
she might enjoy the touching strains
of “ Auld Robin Gray.” In Yurong-
street, a girl of less than 12 fell and
broke a jug containing beer, and her
ruffian protector rushed up, knocked
her half-way across the street, and
carried her home over his shoulder by
the leg, head downwards. Children
obey your parents !
* * *
A Sydney park episode. A
drunken man staggering across Hyde
Park, followed by a gaily - dressed
siren, who watched what path he
would select. At last he headed for
3tanley-street, and the siren made a
detour, sat down on a seat which he
would have to pass, and began to
weep. When the drunk came up he
looked at her pityingly, and in a voice
full of sympathy: “ Wot’s up? wot
ver cryin’ for? Bootiful park—hie—
lovely sky, grand stat-yoo, pretty
flowers ; wot yer—hie—cryin’ for?”
Then the siren sang her song. Said
the drunk : “ Money ! Never mind ;
I got plenty—hie—money ; ’ere yer
yar,” and he threw two or three
crumpled notes into her lap. Persuad-
ingly : “ Don’t cry ; nice garden,
pretty flowers, bee-ootiful sky—hie.”
And he rolled on, looking up and
waving his arms about, while the
siren, tieing the notes into a hand
kerchief, went off like a dynamic
cartridge across the park.
A female Yarra-banker before Melbourne
City Court for vagrancy the other day spun a tear-
ful and wondrous story of her downfall, until the
usually sceptical bench seemed to soften. Then
the prisoner maundered on unwittingly about how
she had two daughters well married and “ society
ladies ” living in big marble mansions in a high-
class suburb, and the bench suddenly wakened up
and cut her short with a shout of “ Twelve
months,” and had the aged one hustled to the
cells. The lady was obviously on the point of
rending local sassiety to its foundations by giving
the married names of her two daughters and the
address of their marble halls. Hence the rush to
get her safely into the cells.
* * » *
Times are better inHawkesbury (N.S.W.)
district. Working 20 hours a day, a wood-carter
earns 10s. a week, and, as a few months ago he
could barely pay horse-feed, this is a distinct
gain. Also, the Benevolent Asylum is now
paying parents for the keep of their own children
ss. a week each, so that the larger the family the
greater the affluence. Previously it was necessary
to board other people’s offspring to get the grant,
and many a poor cocky family lived on that. For
12s. will buy the week's tucker for a bush family
of six, and if your neighbor grows pumpkins on
his flat and ’possums are plentiful, you can manage
on less. Orchardists have received upwards of a
shilling a case for fruit ; as the case is included
and costs 9d., there is a net gain of 3d. This
encourages thrift. Altogether, things are looking
up. If the God-fearing bogus-Freetrade Govern-
ment would only grant ss. a week for board-
ing the “old woman,” and ditto for head of
family, it would inaugurate the Golden Age.
* * * *
The surgeons of Alfred Hospital, Melb.,
have demanded new surgical instruments. The old
ironmongery they have been using is, they say, fit
only for the marine stores. One of the instru-
ments, produced as an exhibit, had its handle—
which was in two pieces—tied together with string.

The Warrnambool women have challenged
Vic. Assembly to a charity game of cricket. The
challenge has been accepted, and the match comes
off sometime this month If the women win, it
is understood woman’s rights will have received an
emphatic lift in Victoria. It is the intention of
the she cricketers to challenge the Leg. Council.
New Woman versus the Old.
* * * *
The Vic. prayer-for-rain party has just had
a bit of bad luck. It petitioned Minister Best to
obtain Govt, sanction for a day of humiliation and
prayer, There was some desire to fix Sunday,
April 25, as the day, but for some reason the fol-
lowing Sunday was selected. Then, as chance
would have it, rain began to 'fall on Monday, April
26, and continued till Wednesday, whilst the
righteous gnashed their gums and abused their
luck. Had the day wished for, Sunday, the 25th,
been fixed upon, here would have been a proof of
the efficacy of humiliation and prayer that would
have curled up the most confirmed sceptics.
Truly, those whom the Lord loveth He chasteneth.

Common-sense seems to be percolating
even into divorce-procedure. Judge (G. B.) Simp-
son, the other day, discharged a respondent who
had been in gaol since January last for non-pay-
ment of costs. His Honor said (in effect) that,
though the law forbade the release, under six
months, of an attached divorce-respondent, he
was in this regard much of Mr. Bumble’s immor-
tal opinion about said law, and had been recently
acting upon the opinion of the English courts that
there was no use in trying to get blood from a
stone by locking up a man who couldn’t pay until
he should pay. Which is indeed a truly astound-
ing discovery.
* * * *
It is suggested that the signal for starting
the simultaneous hymn to be sung on next Queen’s
Birthday should be the letter “ S ” telegraphed
from London along every wire in the empire.
“S” in this case stands either for “sing” or
“shriek.” Considering how few of Victoria’s
loyal subjects can sing worth a cent, it most pro-
bably stands for “ shriek.” The Bulletin has an
idea, by the way, that a really imposing Jubilee
row could be made if every man in the empire
who owns a dog stood on its tail, and every woman
who owns a fowl chased it with a stick, and every
child who owns a cat held it up by the ear, and
everybody who has an infant swung it around by
the leg, all at the same moment. The simul-
taneous yelp and cackle and caterwaul would be a
celebration the like of which the Hempire never
heard before.

The bold N.S.W. warriors who camped
out in Victoria Barracks at Easter are loud in
praise of the methods of modern Major-General
French, who broke up camp early on Easter
Monday and let the dandies on parade get home
in time for beauty sleep ere starting out next
morning as plain citizens. General Hutton
always sent them home saddle-worn and weary
just in time to take in the morning milk.
It has been suggested
that it would be a really affect-
ing grovel if the female popu-
lation of the Empire cele-
brated the record reign by
going back to the dress-
fashions of 1837. The pro-
posal, however, fell as flat as
a patent tombstone. In 1837,
the British young female per-
son wore her dress out low in
the streets, and showed an
acre of unclothed back to the
casual passer-by, and very
often she wore no sleeves, and
hung out for public inspection
two red arms that had been
blighted by the cold east
wind. Even the most loyal
she-person isn’t prepared to
have her back blistered by the
sun when it stands at 120 deg.,
or blown upon by the cold
southerly-burster on a bleak
July morning, though the
blessed throne depended on
it. ;

At St. David’s Cathedral, Hobart, it is
proposed to get over the scarcity of choir-boys
who don’t use cigarettes and bad language, and
cheek the organist, and carve the initials of their
latest Flossie on the seats during the sermon, by
swearing-in a surpliced choir of girls. Parson
Haweis, the cocksure little London oddity of
morals celebrity, is quoted as the necessary II
lish precedent. This paper predicts a big rush of
girls of all ages to join that choir. A surplice
covereth a multitude of angularities.

* » * *
A Stanmore (N.S.W.) lady lost by bur-
glary, among other things, a small box from her
dressing-table, which the robber evidently thought
held jewellery. The box, however, was filled with
relics and old letters much valued by the owner,
who despaired of ever seeing them again; but
they came back by post clumsily wrapped up in a
sugar-bag with an envelope taken from one of the
lost letters (on which was written her address)
pasted on the outside. The lady quite forgives
that thoughtful burglar.
R. : “Canon Potter in defence of fined
Chinese washermen, remarks: ‘ After all, my
work does not differ very widely from his. I try
to show you how to keep your souls clean, and he
helps you to keep a clean shirt to your back.’
Well, if Canon Potter cleans souls to ‘ whiter
than snow ’ by spitting large mouthfuls of water
and saliva over them, even as the pigtailed
heathen does over the shirts, then Canon Potter is
welcome alike to his souls and his cleansing
methods.”
Some of the tricks commonly
resorted to at Australian police-courts.
Complainants can delay or
taking out summonses or warrants,
so that the cases will only he heard
before certain magistrates. Thus,
the beak who is known to hold
“ decided ” views on any sub-
ject is seldom given an oppor-
tunity of adjudicating in such
cases. They are held over'
for the favorable jaypee.
The touting-solicitor nuis-
ance is also growing all the
time. It is now the custom
with many people having
frequent police-court busi-
ness—more especially
those engaged in the time-
payment line —to give a
tout the address of defen-
dant. The solicitor gets
the unhappy defen-
dant’s case —bleeds
his client as much
as possible, and,
at the same
time, really does his best for the other side, for
putting the work in his way, and as a means to
secure further favors.
* * * *
An article in the Vie. Summons gives in-
teresting comparisons between the amounts
charged by barristers, &c., 30 years ago and those
charged now. Instructions for brief were then, in
ordinary cases, £5 for a jury of four, and .£lO for
a jury of twelve. They are now very frequently
£5O for the one, and £lOO for the other. The fee
on brief to such men as Michie, Ireland, Dawson,
Fellows, and Wood was 30 guineas, where 50
would be demanded and got now. Advice was
two guineas then and is five guineas now. Yet
common-law clerks got then £8 per week where
they get £4 now. What is responsible for such
differences ? It’s certainly not the calibre of the
present bar. Is it the Judicature Act, or the
personality of the taxing officers, or both ? A
patient public waits the reply of the Law Com-
mission.

In reporting a recent Glen Innes (N.S.W.)

“Stephen Bosher, come with me a minute,”
were the words of hangman Long, at Wellington
(M.L.), the other day, when the last scene of all
came to the cruel murderer of the old couple at
Petone. A minute ! And after Bosher had spoken
unbornbastically from the gallows he met his
death with fortitude. After repeating the
“Miserere” after the priest, the latter ex-
claimed : ‘' Au revoir, mon cher ami, au revoir. ”
Bosher replied, “Ait revoir,” and the hangman
then chipped in with a rough “Say good-bye,
Bosher. ” Bosher said “ Good-bye,” and was killed
at once. If at all imaginative and a believing
Christian and sufficiently versed in English, how
the difference between his countryman’s “Au
revoir ” and the man-butcher’s “ Good-bye ” must
have comforted him ! Hell is the place for hang-
men. It also would seem to be the right place for
people like Bosher who, as a Salvationist bands-
man, followed to the grave the grey-haired couple
he had slain for the sake of gain, and offered prayer
at the graveside, and asked God to deliver the mur-
derer for justice. And the prayer was heard.
Bosher it was who first informed the police of the
crime, and gave them the suspicious points,
which led to the arrest of Shore for the crime;
Shore, a slaughterman, had been drunk on the
night of the murder. At Shore’s trial Bosher
gave damning evidence, but the knife couldn’t be
found ; and, as other things favored Shore, his ac-
quittal followed. It would now almost appear
that the police only arrested Shore to put Bosher
off the scent. Lawyer Wilford defended Shore in
the murder-case —got him off, and then, after
helping to put the net around Bosher by his insinu-
ations when the murderer was under cross-exam-
ination at the Shore trial, defended him also.

Answers to Correspondents.

:Mo notice mil be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted, and in no case will MS. be
returned unless stamps (of any province) accompany.
Neranga : There are enough difficulties in the
way of Federation without throwing dreadful blank
verse at it M.S. (Narrandera): T. Smith’s poem is
best buried in oblivion C.W.O. (Wilcannia): (1) The
addition to note circulation, Govt, deposits and current
accounts isn’t an addition to the bank’s “ resources ” but
to its liabilities. (2) The reduction of fixed deposits is
an addition to the resources, not the other way about.

of manual dexterity who turns out a monotonous series'
of pictures and drawings, technically correct, and with-
out one atom of feeling, one gleam of inspiration, in the
batch? Or is it the man who can’t draw a dog's leg

color or form, not mechanically, but poetically, by trans-

tion with all the underlying facts and principles of Life
and Art ? In a word, is it the brain that makes the artist,

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, May 4, 1897.
Mr Dear Myee—
About Hospital Saturday onslaught the general
impression seems to be that the girls are tired of
collecting; this year they begged languidly and
without much enthusiasm. Several of them stood
as if under protest grasping an umbrella firmly in
one hand and a collecting-box in the other, and
said no word. A man offered one of these mute
appealers half-a-sovereign if she would only ask
him for it, but she still kept her mouth tightly
shut; she got the coin all the same, though.
Numerous men facetiously “ tossed up ” with the
box-holders for theexpected shilling—-it was always
on the “ heads I win, tails you lose ” principle for
the collecting-girl. One woman at a crowded
city-stand thrust her box under the nose of a haw-
hawish looking person, and rejoiced when he
pulled out about =£s in gold, two threepennies
and a copper, but she disgustedly saw him sort
out the gold and throw the rest in with a re-
sounding clank. Ha’pennies were the most popu-
lar tribute of all, particularly from women.
Sometimes a beneficent-looking man approached a
box unasked, gave an extra polished smile in with
his money and lifted his hat politely. But men
who smiled the widest didn’t always part on the
same scale. Perhaps the only persons who
thoroughly enjoyed the day were the neglected
elderly bachelors, too dry and crusty to be taken
any notice of as a general rule, who laid in vast
piles of pennies the day before, and had an un-
accustomed plethora of smiling blandishments all
day long at a penny an ogle.
’Varsity “ sugar ” dropped into the collecting-
boxes from the eapped-and-gowned hobblede-
hoys on their way to Commemoration at
the Town Hall took a liquid form : they
essayed to drop sweet sticky stuff down the
slots. _ At the speechifying the principal
diversion of these dreadfully unoriginal persons
was the loud rustling of a multitude of news-
papers, which made one speaker give in and
sit unheard. A girl-student who received a silver
medal was the most marvelled at by her sex in the
audience for excelling in a long string of jaw-
breaking ologies. The local ’Varsity anthem isn’t
as good as that of Melbourne, where an audacious
fifth-year medical writes fresh verses every year
ridiculing profs., and is regularly “ploughed” at
at every exam. But then there is no Marshall Hall
here. This month there is to be a week of
academic gaiety, including a ball in honor of the
visiting football clubs from Melbourne and Ade-
laide “ shops,” an inter-university boat-race, and
other contests.
The requisite thrill of sympathy with tuber-
culous sufferers was set in motion by the
Govt. House meeting last week, which was
largely attended. It pushed the movement
for establishing hospitals for consumptives one
step further by resolving to hold a public
meeting in the Town Hall, presided over by His
Ex., to advocate the raising of a fund. The
speakers deplored the increase of consumption in
Australia, but none of them noted that consump-
tives from ’Ome who are sent out for the long sea-
voyage and the mild climate really require quaran-
tining as much as small-pox patients and much
more than lepers, if all that latter-day authorities
say is true. _ A phthisis-infested English guest on
a visit for his health has been sometimes known
to more than decimate an Australian family.
The microscopic pictures at the Society of Artists
Gallery in George-street are evidently offered as
samples for buyers to say, “Yes, this is what I
want, give me some of it.” Or perhaps the artists
are merely playing “ Boyne Water ” softly to see
how much impression the public will stand. Any-
how, minute back-block scenes are an inspiration,
for the bush ought always to be taken in homoeo-
pathic doses. A bold, original design showing
Souter pursued by infuriated cats, and another of
Sid. Long cursed by critics are frescoed, “ not for
publication ” behind the red curtain. The show
itself is like a fashionable millinery-shop in color,
all purple and green. A tree with curly blue
trunk in a vague cream-colored landscape looks
like A. Streeton in an unguarded moment. Among
women-exhibitors are Florence Fuller, with a
Frenchy method of expressing feeling in her work,
Alice Muskett, whose cornflowers are true blue,
and Theo. Cowan, our only woman who sculps.
It is an understood thing now that at the
jubilee in June there are to be no fireworks. As
the idea of rejoicing is not complete unless some-
body is prepared to pay up, citizens are requested
to back their loyal feelings by large donations to
all sorts of charities. An autograph circular
signed ‘ Susan Hampden ” is the latest one issued,
inviting subscriptions from one shilling upwards
to the Queen’s Jubilee Fund, inaugurated ten
years ago to relieve distressed women. The annual
meeting of the charity was held last week,
and the report showed that practical help has
been given to necessitous women since March,
’B9, to the extent of over £6OOO. The report
states that in a very large number of cases the
relief given has taken the shape of mangles, sew-
ing-machines, type-writers, &c. The fund seems
to be well-administered; it would appear that
none of it goes to the professional cadger, the
object of the directors being to help those who
desire to help themselves.
At the University on Monday night the girl-
graduates invited many guests to a conversazione,
which long Italian word means that you mustn’t
talk when the music is going on. The programme
was a good one contributed by Mme. Kellermann
Miss Britton, Mr. Strauss, Miss E. Deane, and
others. Miss Macdonald, principal of the women’s
college, shook hands with all comers as hostess.
The authoress of a smart topical novel, who is also
a biking enthusiast, displayed a close crop of hair
topping a clever, strong face with a square jaw ;
her gown, like most University women’s'garments,
was droopy. B.A.’s are never careful about curl-
ing their bangs, and their skirts have a classically
limp hang. Greek eliminates gores and the bino-
mial theorem is the death-knell of a good figure.
Some of the learned damsels don’t altogether de-
spise Man, for I saw one furry-hooded and be-
gowned girl talking confidentially to a man-student
in a hidden corner. The male guests seemed also
sicklied o’er with the pale cast of thought. Alto-
gether it was a cultured function, and the neces-
sary “sweetness and light ” was attained both by
the company and the refreshments.
For one of the forthcoming charity hops the
committee is busily planning fancy “sets” of
Lancers, and, as a brilliant novelty, four
courageous couples are to face the music attired in
costumes copied from popular posters. Girls are
now anxiously studying the fashions on the
hoardings to see whether they can go dressed as
the bicycle-girl in the bathing-suit or the lady in
the Panama-hat and the purling stream. “ Nes-
tle’s Milk ” is fixed on for one set of costumes, and
the men are seriously thinking of personating the
calves.
The city lately has become one vast vice-regal
ad. Photos, of every aspect of the recent bridal
are in some windows, wedding-gifts incite admir-
ing women to flatten their noses against the panes
of other shops, and music named after or dedi-
cated to the bride and bridegroom exhibits on its
covers little cupids fluttering and wedding-rings
tied with true-lovers’ knots, throwing a rosy tint
of Hymeneal blushfulness around the usually
prosaic George-street. Perhaps the most attrac-
tive show of all is the village-cart drawn by a
Liliputian white pony frequently driven by lively
Mrs. Freeman-Thomas. Occasionally her small
son gets an airing in the cart, but his father has
not been much seen about. The Captain is on
the sick-list just now, and at all his public appear-
ances has hobbled in the Gov.’s rear with the aid
of a stick.
Sir William Robinson, Governor, mine-pro-
moter, and talented musician—he composed “Re-
member Me No More ” —has, it is cabled, died in
England of a throat-affection. He had many
Australian friends, and was a thoroughly good-
natured man. Sad to relate, his brother, whose
memory as Sir Hercules Robinson is still green in
Australia, is also dying—of dropsy consequent on
a complication of diseases.
Jewish ladies are again at the top of the tree in
the Hospital Saturday collections. From which
it would appear that the law of Moses is more
benevolent than the Christian laws which, as in-
terpreted by the Balmain clergy, strictly forbid
collections being made for charities on the Chris-
tian—that is to say, the Balmain clergy’s—
Sabbath.
Last year a gigantic tea-meeting, in aid of the
Church Society of Sydney, with Lady Hampden
at the head of affairs, was held in the Town Hall.
This year there will be a repetition, and women
are tumbling over each other in offering their ser-
vices at Govt. House. As Bishop Smith is away
enjoying himself in England, Dr. Camidge, of
Bathurst, will boss the show. Dr. Camidge is the
delight of elderly Christian ladies just now.
Florence Mack, sister of Louise Mack, the
well-known writer, possesses a soprano voice, and,
with Ruby Reynolds, contralto, intends to give a
concert in the Town Hall on May 20th.
“Skipped by the Light of the Moon,” at the
Palace Theatre, gave its first hop on Saturday
night. The Palace stage is so small that Gourlay
can’t skip very far, and when he has all his checks
on there is hardly room for Mrs. Dingle’s crino-
line. She still wears that crinoline, and Obadiah
Dingle is as airy as ever, and wrestles in obscurity
with the wee bit puppy as before. The new
Crackles, Will Watkins, goes in for the fashionable
Elphberg hair —a stage without a red wig looks
strangely cold now we have all become so much
accustomed to the hue. Harry Shine, as the
bobby, wears his crimson on his nose, and has a
beautiful brogue. First night, a hospital girl
walked round the circle and collected moneys
between the acts.
Miss Maud FitzStubbs has given the fashion-
able orange-blossom and wedding-bell effect to her
new waltz by calling it “ The Bridal.” By invita-
tion a select company of connoisseurs listened to a
performance of the new dance-tune scored for
piano and orchestra at the Broadwood Rooms last
Thursday. It is a cheerful and brilliant strain in
the new, lively style of waltz, and has a good
dancing swing. The old wailing and sadful
strains have been quite superseded in all the
“ new ” waltz music.
Tobacco isn’t a circumstance on the bicycle as a
smasher-up of the suburban evening. “Small and
early ” musical agonies are now rapidly wilting
away under the blighting influences of the vagrant
jigger which attracts the youthful swains out into
the fresh air away from stuffy best parlors and in-
digestible coffee-and-sandwich snacks. Girls who
cycle don’t feel the desertion, but the great crowd
who haven’t the necessary coin to pay the weekly
instalments for. the tin gee-gee are learning to
look upon machines as their hated rivals, and long
to express their feelings by unexpected punctures.
The new theory of mind-healing seems to be
making great headway: the cult has been preached
by its prophets all over Australia, and now select
little coteries are to be met with in dainty draw-
ing-rooms, discussing over afternoon tea the new
struggle to make matter subservient to mind It
is noteworthy that the latest cure hails from U.S.
—the acid of hurried and flurried Western civilisa-
tion has had to go for its neutralising alkali to the
immemorial East, for the idea is not new, but an
eclectic blend of the acquired calm of the Asiatic
yogi, combined with Horace’s “equal mind”
under all circumstances and Christian fortitude of
patience on a monument. It is a beautiful and
intellectual notion. Pain can be conquered by
simply making up one’s mind that it shall be, and
there is an immense deal of truth in the idea. If
Mrs. Dombey had made that effort, she might be
living still.
But I fear me that mind-healing is too much in
the regions of pure and abstract thought for ordi-
nary mortals to master effectually. The average
man can’t thrive on mental processes ; his mind
can t assimilate intellectual vol au vent ; he wants
mutton-chops and plenty of them. Besides
though nervous worry induces disease, nervous
agitation means feeling. Quiescent calm marks
the lower organisms. A jelly-fish in all its exist-
ence never has such a bad quarter-of-an-hour as a
lashed ’bus-horse straining up William-street hill,
but I’d rather be a gee-gee than a jelly-fish, for
all that. “Cease to suffer,” says Schopenhauer,
“if you cease to feel.” The true prototype of the
modern man is Prometheus with the vulture gnaw-
ing at his vitals ; he brings down fire from heaven,
but suffers terribly from his liver.
Sydney is all agog over the Gore-Gillon divorce
case, in its way the liveliest that has happened for
a long while.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ Mrs. Turner holds the Premier in wonderful
honor and reverence as the administrator of all
good—public and private—to the people of Vic-
toria. She gave the women-clerks of the post-
office a bad freezing-up a little whde ago when
they roundly refused to buy tickets for a concert
in which she was interested. ‘To think,’ said the
buxom matron, sweeping on them a look of indig-
nation, ‘ to think all of my husband, the Premier,
has done for you ! ’ A prominent official, who was
escorting her over the apartments, hurried the
august lady from the presence of the ingrates lest
she might explode.
“ Mrs. Henry Bracy, hostess at the Salon last
Monday (May 3), had not visited the Club since
that dreadful day when Madame Sterling, who
had been expected at 3 o’clock, bore down
on the rooms at 5 and stayed until 8, harangu-
ing Salonites on religious topics. Nobody
got home to dinner, and when the gushing guest
left she kissed everyone in the room, including
one solitary, hungry young man who had strayed
there somehow and got hemmed in. This victim
bore the same name as a famous poet, and Madame
kissed him, so she said, because she fancied he was
related to his great namesake. However, any
excuse is good enough for kissing a nice young
man. The other day when Mrs. Bracy, after so
long an interval, re-visited the Salon to arrange
for Monday’s tea, she left her son downstairs out
of harm’s way.
“Be the Diamond Jubilee : there are bound to
be jealousies and heartburnings among the Pre-
miers’ wives over the suites assigned to them in
the Hotel Cecil. A competent authority tells me
she has already cast the horoscope of the estab-
lishment for the month of June, and that there
are neither ways of pleasantness nor paths of peace
to be found on the chart. A prominent member
of the feminine contingent said before she left,
‘ Hif they don’t know oo Hi ham in London, Hi’ll
make them know oo Hi ham ! ’ London may put
up with that sort of thing, but sister-premieresses
probably won’t.
“ Some time ago an Australian Governor
visited a ‘ Newsboys’ Try Class ’ in his district.
After inspecting and speechifying, he suffered
from a generous impulse. ‘ Send up to Govern-
ment House to-morrow,’ he said, ‘ and I will
make a little addition to your library.’ The little
boys and their leader were delighted in expecta-
tion of a choice assortment from the Governor’s
innumerable collection, and next day borrowed a
horse-and-trap so as to bring home the books com-
fortably. At the House, a lady came out on the
door-mat and gave them two copies of ‘ The Voyage
of the Sunbeam. ’ Only that and nothing more.
“ The chrysanthemum craze is nearly over—as a
craze. The monstrous blooms that the devotion
of a life is able to produce are no longer the am-
bition of every suburbanite. Canaries dwindled
from being a religion into being merely a sect—
and the chrysanthemum is doing ditto. What is
wanted to revive the interest is some startling
novelty, such as a blue chrysanthemum would be.
The gardener who could find the secret of such a
flower would be able to visit Queen Victoria with
the proceeds, and would barely escape knighthood.
There is a ’mum with the perfume of violets,
called the Mrs. E. W. Clarke, or something like
that, but it has not the capacity to make a whole
suburb yearn. Melb. show people, with lop-sided
balance-sheets and boom assets, are sighing for a
blue chrysan’.
“ People of the snob variety in Melbourne com-
ment on the goodness, generosity, &c., &c., of
Swilliam Clarke and his energetic little wife in
not joining the Jubilee mob who have gone ’Ome.
They have stayed in Victoria to do their share of
entertaining in June, but will visit England next
year. The Clarkes felt it to be their duty to be in
the country in June, and so exercised self-denial.
The tone adopted, however, is as though all Vic-
toria ought to join in a loud Song of Thanksgiving
because the lanky Bart, and his wife are staying
‘to look after the house,’ as it were. Probably
there will be a national movement to add to the
National Anthem a verse beginning ‘ God bless
dear Swilliam Clarke.’

muneration of lady _ assistants in the ‘ seminaries ’
and ‘ academies ’ in and around Melbourne is
about Is. 6d. a week, with a bonus of Is. for each
new pupil brought to the school. If any of these
Miss Nicklebys has a gift of description she should
be able to pen-paint a nice, squalid companion
picture to that of Dotheboys Hall as drawn by
Dickens.

“ The triumph of Worth in Mrs. Potter’s Melb.
stage gownery is a confection where the frock-

chant turned up to St. Vincent’s Hospital meet-
mg the other day at the Town Hall in a most

“The whirlsome wife of a Southern sassiety
man has decided to refrain, for the future, from
striking up a promiscuous friendship with every
breezy wheel-girl who may happen to be pedaling
in her vicinity. The other day, this affable matron
came across a couple of the most engaging crea-
tures, and, after an animated discussion re punc-
tures, gears, and similar matters of mutual inte-
rest, she took them home with her to dinner-
hubby not being expected. The dinner a trois
revealed no cause for suspicion, except that the
guests were more thirsty than might have been
expected in cool weather ; but, just as they wera
wishing their hostess au revoir, and not good-bye
preparatory to leaping into the saddle, three or
four young men, friends of the family, trundled
up the carriage-drive, caught sight of the depart-
ing lydies, and gasped unanimously. Then the
young men held an informal meeting on the lawn
and deputed the gravest of their number to tell
the mistress of the house in gentle tones that
‘ Scotch Annie ’ and her companion were unde-
sirable visitors to have about the premises. The
astonished innocent, blushing right down to her
boots, implored those young men not to breathe a
word of the awful story to anyone, and, Heaven
forgive them ! they swore they wouldn’t. ’
“ The congregation of St. Mark’s, Fitzroy
(Melb.), has presented the incumbent with a
bike. Religion is losing all solemnity. An in-
cumbent who travels the narrow' road to righteous'
ness mounted on a jigger is bound to be followed
by the younger ladies of the Church Guild i n
‘ rationals.’
“ The proposal to abandon khakee uniform for
the Vic. militia, and revert to the old Hinglish
red jacket, is an awful satire on the alleged growth
of military spirit in Australia’s most warlike pro-
vince. ‘ Red,’ says Sir Holled Smith, ‘is needed
to attract young men to the service.’ I n other
words, the soldier’s first object in joining the
ranks is to do a ‘ mash.’ There’s nobody to ‘ kill ’
except servant-girls, therefore put him in gaudy
raiment and leave him to dye, if necessary in
defence of his country.
“A German matron, resident in Melb., lately
testified to the worthiness of her hired linen-
cleanser with a nicely-written ‘character.’ The
document stated—* Mrs. Blank was quite pleased
with this lady for what she done the washing.
She is very honest. I can scent her in any place 1
The English language, in German hands, hi a fine
opportunity for unconscious humor.
A Nice man nowadays wants as elaborate a
wardrobe as a toney woman does. He must have
different costumes for “ riding, walking, driving
shooting, golfing, tennis, bicycling and cricket’
dining, smoking and lounging, football, racing’
and yachting, to say nothing of uniform and court
suit, besides the now developing motor-car cos-
tume.” The above is the clothes-limit placed by
“ Madge,” of London Truth, who has issued a
g - u , ld ? f?„ the wa y B of the Perfect gentleman, en-
titled Manners for Men.” Without all these
changes of raiment no man can aspire to be
considered a sassiety person. Madge’s Manual
describes fully the “scope and limitations
of the tweed suit,” and gives full direc-
tions for dress on all occasions. She depre-
cates the growing carelessness of even high-
toned youths, and shudderingly describes how a
courageous man appeared one night in the stalls
of a London theatre in boating-flannels and a
straw hat. She doesn’t say whether the curtain
was instantly rung down and the audacious
offender cast into the deepest dungeon beneath the
castle moat. The offence naturally happened in
the autumn when all the “ best people ” were out
of town, so sensitive feelings were not lacerated so
much as they might otherwise have been.
One quarter of this guide to society is devoted
to the etiquette of feeding, especially at that
stodgily. British entertainment, a dinner-party.
The aspiring Johnny is begged to have a supply of
small-talk on hand. That, and his many suits,
seem to compose his entire social stock-in-trade.
If he takes ladies to a restaurant his conduct must
imply paying for them to the uttermost farthing,
even if he has to run round the corner to pop his
watch. “Madge” says she has known some
heroes do this, rather than own up to a lack of
small change. Pathetic little side-notes along the
page-margin decree “at a restaurant the man
always pays, though he cannot afford it.” Men
are also solemnly warned never to offer politeness
or an umbrella on a wet day—to a strange
woman. A “ lady ” would inevitably snub
the profferer, and the “ other sort ” might never
return the gamp. The authoress shows a true
Londoner’s distrust and hatred of a stranger ; in-
cidentally, she warns girls to be always on the
offensive in the streets, and to “ fear and dread a
prowling man.” As a matter of fact, the girl
whose mind is constantly on the alert against the
“prowling man,” is bound to be the hysterical
screamer who has an adventure with one of the
dreaded monsters. If a woman has a grain of
common sense, she can go about her business
alone, even in huge overgrown London, and be
quite unconscious of a prowler. Besides, there is
always a policeman, and a Loudon bobby is as
perfect a gentleman as if he h. . got “Madge’s”
little maxims by heart.
Yours affectionately,
CLEO.
Melb. Champion shows its superior wis-
dom by the reflection that Major-Editor Reay’s
voyage to England will convey to him the “ useful
and essential lesson that the world is not a small
piece of land bounded on the north by the Hamil-
ton Spectator, and on the south by the Melb.
Herald.” Seeing that before drifting into jour-
nalism, Reay served before the mast on a
steamer, and saw, probably, more of the world
than Champion has seen, the remark rather lacks
point.
Gov. Brassey, who prides himself on being
hardly ever interviewed, was surprised to find a
five-minutes’ chat with a youthful-looking under-
grad at Melb. University sports result in three
columns of interview in the Australian Cyclist.
Among other things, it is stated that the Gover-
nor’s lately-broken collar-bone has not properly
set, so H.E. is unable to part his back hair, which
function is performed for him by a cold, proud
menial. The Governor prefers tennis to cycling,
but thinks he could do 60 miles a day easily with a
rest after each 20.

Personal Items.
A N.S.W. aboriginal took legal advice in
Sydney last week as to the steps to be taken to
obtain a divorce from a giddy gin.
Premier Kingston effected a reconciliation
with one of his bitterest enemies—Edwin Smith,
M.L.C. —prior to his departure for England.
Olive Schreiner, of the “African Farm,”
recently explained why she married Kronwright:
“ Kronwright laces up one’s boots with so much
broad human sympathy.”
S.A. Chief-Justice Way has been sum-
moned to Windsor to kiss Queen Victoria’s hand
on his installation as P.C. He can now die hap-
pily. The Bulletin fears he will simply explode.
Barton and Isaacs are spoken of as the
persons likely to be commissioned to take the
Commonwealth Constitution Bill “home.” W. C.
Wentworth and E. Deas-Thomson took “ home ”
the N.S.W. Constitution Bill.
Edward Fairfield, of the Colonial Office,
whose death is reported by cable, was the brother
of the Jeames (Charles Fairfield) who writes
“Vanity Fair” from London in Melb. Argus.
Hence the amount of gossip about official persons
in that occasional column.
Premier Kingston cannot yet be accused of
deserting his friends. He signalised his departure
for ’Ome by wearing the aged terai hat so familiar
to even the oldest citizens —the old felt which has
loyally stuck to him through all his troubles. It
was a diplomatic triumph. “ Stick to the felt hat,
Charlie, and they won’t nobble you,” said a West
Adelaide admirer in the crowd. The democratic
future of S.A. is centred in that aged terai.
ANXIOUS TO PLEASE.
THE DONA ( scornfully) : “’Course I’ve chucked yer. Zif I was
goitern ’ter marry a lad what couldn't lick me!"
NIPPER PETE : “ Gerrout! Ain’t I bin takin’ lessons off a pug?"
Miner Wentworth, uncle of the lately-
admitted barrister W. C. Wentworth, of Vau-
cluse, has entirely lost the sight of both eyes. He
was mining on the Murchison (W. A.), and put in
a charge which exploded prematurely. Medical
treatment failed, and the old man is now blind.
From the circulated police description of
an absconding Vic. bank-manager :
Very corpulent, red, fat face . . . top of head bald ...
Bhort fat neck, lobes of ears very thick.
A dreadful warning to intending embezzlers who
are unbeautiful.
