between Premier Kingston and S.A. Register
editor Finlayson. Register, April 27
No great problems press urgently for solution inParlia-
ment .... there will be a general wish that Mr. and
Mrs. Kingston may thoroughly enjoy their holiday.
Even the hospital question is not now a “great
problem pressing urgently for solution ” ! ! ! !
The whole population of the Island of
Tonga is in a ferment over a rumor that its fasti-
dious King is about to take as wife the
whitey-brown daughter of a Samoan woman
and a German trading on the island. His
Majesty has a rival in a European store-clerk em-
ployed in his dominion, but in the betting the
King is well-backed. The lady, who has been
“ finished ” in Sydney, flashes an orthodox dia-
mond engagement ring, keeps half-a-dozen sewing-
machines going on her trousseau, and won t tell
whether her future bridegroom is to be white or
brown.
Demise : A. J. Smith, very well-known
Melb. bookseller —died on a recent Cook’s excur-
sion to Adelaide. His shop was the local Wes-
leyan centre. Smith underwent agonies of torture,
as a profoundly religious man, in fixing the line
at which he ought to compound with the Zolaistic
and Balzackian tendencies of the age ; but he was
brave.
After the fact 1 A Sydney palm-reader
claims to have foretold to Lee Weller that his end
would be sudden, and to have earnestly cautioned
him against mining ventures. At all events, she
exhibits a pair of fancy bottles, inscribed to her
by defunct, “ in appreciation of her skill in palmis-
try.” Credulity seems to have been poor Weller’s
weakness.
A Brisbane barrister was briefed in a case,
to be heard in a country town. Without enough
money to pay his fare, all he could do —and did—
was to walk the distance, 100 miles. And if that
country solicitor had been like his “ professional
brethren ” in Brisbane, he would have had to walk
back. Broadly speaking, only the Crown Solici-
tor thinks it necessary to pay juniors the fees
they earn.
The first woman barrister has just been
admitted to practise in Maoriland. Her name is
Rebecca Benjamin, and, barring the fact that it
might have been Sarah Cohen, this is just what
one naturally expected. Does a promising billet
open anywhere on this earth without some
one of the agile and brainy race of Benjamin
getting there first ? Even in England, which has
more heaped-up capital and more monish out at
interest than any other country, it isn’t John Bull
himself, but his Anglo-Semitic second cousin,
Benjamin Bull Moses, who has the thick end of
the wealth.
Prof. William Crookes be-
lieves in “thought transference.”
Having ascertained the relative
strength of air-waves necessary to
produce light, heat, sound, color,
etc., he has tabled an elaborate
calculation, showing how “ brain-
waves ” can establish telepathic
connection between persons in
close sympathy, but between
whom numberless “solid” ob-
stacles exist. Brain-waves pene-
trate these just like the Rbntgen
rays, which are only another variety
of “ wave ” forces. Crookes (re-
putedly a spiritist) is no mere wild
dreamer. He is one of the most
illustrious scientists of any epoch.
He got the Royal Society’s gold
medal as inventor of the radio-
meter ; and Rontgen’s great dis-
covery is based on the Crookes
tube. He is a pasty-faced, corpse-
like personage, with an eternal-
cigarette in his mouth, and he
works over a wide scientific area,
which includes gold - saving pro-
cesses.
One day some years ago,
when the late Anthony Hordern
was alive, a traveller for a, big
European perfumery house visited
him, gave him his bed-rook prices,
and solicited an order. The old
man was apparently not anxious
to do business, and kept the drum-
mer on the string for a day or so.
At last he remarked : “Now, sup-
pose,l take £5OO worth, can you
cut your price 10 per cent. ? ” The
drummer demurred, said that the-
price quoted was dead bottom,
but, of course, for such an order a
reduction might be made. Would
Mr. H. share the cost of a cable
to enquire ? “No,” said Anthony ;
“ but you cable to them and say
we’ll take =£lo,ooo worth at 20 per
cent. off. Money in London to-
morrow if they want it. ” The flab-
bergasted commercial sent the
cable and the deal eventuated.
That good old Tory, Hon. Henry Scot-
land, in Maoriland Leg. Council: “ The Soudan
Contingent tended to the glorification of one man,
an impudent Irish adventurer named Dailey, who
got the whole thing up.” And yet the average
Uppah House person is never tired of dilating on
the coarseness and want of taste of the average
labah membah.
Those who have said that Attorney-
General Isaacs has his eye on the next vacant
iudgeship, do not know the clever and ambitious
little man (writes an allegedly well-informed
Bulletin correspondent). His sharp, clear eye is
fixed on a political goal for the present, if J ustice
Williams were to resign to-morrow, Isaacs would
not take the vacancy. He gives up everything for
politics, and must have lost thousands from his
once enormous bar income during the past few
years.
Premier Kingston has just received from
Sydney the copy of a memorial addressed by his
maternal grandfather, Col. Cameron, m 1817, to
the British Commander-in-Chief, praying for pro-
motion. It set forth that the colonel, who, more
than 60 years ago, commanded the drd Buns m
Sydney, was present in every engagement m
which his regiment took part from 2nd Oct., 171)9,
when he was wounded in Holland, until the occu-
pation of Paris, after Waterloo. He claims to
have been the first British soldier across the
Douro on 12th May, 1809, and, sad to say, he
makes a boast that, as major, he carried the town
of Plattsburg against the soldiers of the United
States on 6th Sept., 1814. Col. Cameron s daughter
married, in 1841, Sir George Kingston who
had three wives at various times, and Charles
Cameron Kingston appeared on the scene in 1800.
A N.S.W. District Court
Judge relates how, one morn-
ing in the country, he found
before him in the dock a smirk-
ing person with whose face he
seemed familiar. Soon it
struck him that this was the
man who, at dinner the pre-
vious day at a wayside accom-
modation-house, had carved
the leg of mutton, and had
given him (the Judge) a huge
first cut and all the gravy.
“ But I gave him three years
all the same,” adds hisßhada-
manthine Honor.
Australia’s greatest orator
has been at it again, this time
at Gunnedah. A black gin
took possession of a stage-chair
reserved for Dan during a pub-
lic meeting at a recent Minis-
terial visit, and when Dan
asked her to shift he was bar-
racked by the “ blokes at the
back,” Then he ga,ve the
audience a sample of his glow-
ing style of eloquence. ‘ ‘ Young
men,” he said, “it ill becomes
you to laugh at this faded
flower of your fellow country-
men. You should be ashamed
of yourselves, as Australians,
to laugh at one of the fast-
dying race, from whom we
have taken this fair country.
Pity her, don’t jeer at her.
Had she our intelligence and
our education most likely she
would be a princess instead of
a despised black-gin.”
as the orator finished, Sarah
(the gin) chipped in, “ Do’an
yer mine ’em, boss ; wat yer
’avin’ ?—l’m a beer ; come on ”
—and kept beckoning to him as
she was escorted to the door
amid the uproar of the crowd.
That gin received a first-class
education (moral and other-
wise) at La Perouse, Sydney.
David Murray, old time S.A. politician,
lately returned to Adelaide. Murray, when he
became Chief Secretary in the Downer 85 Govt.,
in the first days of economy, electrified the public
service by abolishing barrel pens. But it was
similar Scotch finance which helped to make
Murray a semi-millionaire.
If the poor, lame Ricciotti Garibaldi had
gone filibustering with 10 times his 250 heroes, he
couldn’t have counted for much with the Turk.
As it is, he is a painfully superfluous circumstance.
But Ricciotti always was a bit superfluous. It’s a
terrible thing to have to live up to such a fighting
reputation as old Giuseppe’s 1
SPORTING—STRUCK THE DOUBLE.
(Sketch by Hop's understudy.)
Judge Cooper is one of the three six-feet
judges of the Q. Supreme court, and the dandy of
them all. He is the fairest-minded of the Southern
judges but as fussy as a clucking hen with a china
egg wherever his dignity is concerned, which same
dignity, by the way, he jumped from an attorney-
generalship. Cooper travels his circuits like an
Oriental potentate, wears goodness knows how
many white shirts in a day, and holds the hanging
record—this chiefly because he was for many years
the sole Northern Judge, and capital crimes are
much more frequent in N.Q. than in S.Q.

Rev. Dr. Bruce, the new moderator of the
N.S.W. Presbyterian church, is not popular with a
big section of the “ fathers and brethren.” It was
he who caused something like consternation, a
couple of years ago, by stating that he did not
disapprove of dancing or theatre - going and
kindred amusements, provided they were kept
within judicious limits. His liberal views were
not shared by many of his clerical friends, and
there was talk of his being asked to resign ; but
as he is not dependent on his calling for bread
and butter, and is exceedingly popular with his
North Sydney congregation, the unco guid thought
better of it.

Peacock laugh ? Because he smokes Puf-
eni’s cigarettes. ” “ How to laugh like Pea-
cock ? Drink Boosem’s whisky,” and so on.
The council-clerk of Marsfield (N.S.
Wales), a poor, struggling municipality,
unites the duties of council-clerk, inspector
of nuisances, inspector of works, inspector
of slaughter-houses, and inspector of dairies,
also, in his “ spare time,” he is “expected
to work on the roads and do anything else
that may be required of him.” Salary
(when available) i;B4 per annum ! !
Melb. crape-and-coffin biz. has al-
ways had members with curiously appro-
priate names. The boss suburban under-
taker is Raven. A few years ago Dedman
was the chief tombstone-maker in the
Northern suburbs, and when he became a
real dead man he was succeeds A by Church-
man. Also in Carlton the timber-supplier
to most shell-factories was Grieve.
The average rate of speech of the
’97 Convention delegates wvb 135 words
per minute. Deakin rattled along at
about 165, Barton, Zeal, and Glynn aver-
aged about 150; Gordon, Cockbum, Fysh,
O’Connor, Wise, Turner, Isaacs, and Dob-
son were in the 140—145 brigade. Reid,
Symon, Quick and Trenwith formed a
quartette of deliberate speakers. In the
main debate they averaged under 110
words a minute, but all of them warmed
up to about 125 in committee.
Once, when Governor of Wcstralia,
H.E. “ Bill ” Robinson visited Bishop
Salvado’s NewNorcia Mission and doubted
the statement of the Bishop that there was
a black gin on the mission who was an
expert telegraphiste. “ Try her,” said
Dr. Salvado, and forthwith His Ex., who
was unknown to the black girl, had a wire
sent to Govt. House, Perth, inquiring for
“ Si r W. F. C. Robinson,” and received a
bad set-back when the girl gave the answer
from the wire : “ The Governor is out of
town.” Whereat the aged Bishop laughed
up his sleeve, and helped himself to two
consecutive pinches of snuff.
All the Conventionists in Adelaide affixed
their signatures to morocco-bound copies of the
roll of membership, one of which was given to
each member as a souvenir. The most remarkable
signature of the lot is that of which a copy is
appended. It defies reading, unless one is told m
advance what its running rings are supposed to
stand for. The scrawl is as follows
It is the signature of the Tas. Treasurer, P. 0.
Fysh.

WATSON’S WHISKY.
Does this mean you ?
How many people are there who seem to lack
ambition, life and healthful vigor. You can tell
it by their looks. They shiver with the cold when
the weather is bleak. They have a pinched, sal-
low appearance. They complain of feeling run
down. Their symptoms plainly show that their
blood is thin and poor, that it does not circulate
properly, and that their liver and kidneys are in a
very bad state. That is why they have these
miserable symptoms and complain of
Stomach troubles,
Constipation,
Nervousness,
General weakness,
Headaches and backaches,
Chilly sensations,
Dizziness,
Despondency.
Are you one of this vast number who suffer from
the miserable wearing feelings above described!
If so, why not try to overcome them and get into
a good state of health? You can easily do so.
All you need to do is to take Warner’s Safe Cure
regularly, and it will make you strong and well.
Take it just for a short time and you will soon
notice a change for the better. Every bad symp-
tom will disappear. Your blood will become rich
and pure, your circulation improve, and your liver
and kidneys will do their work. This great
remedy, in short, will put new life and vigor into
you. It will make you feel better than you have
felt for many a day. Will you try it ?*
Warner’s Safe Cure is a Specific for Liter
and Kidney Disease.

POLITICAL
POINTS
The N.S.W. unemployed haunt the Go-
vernment offices. Cabinet chorus : “ Whist !
whist!! whist!!! Here come the Bogan men ! ”
The Times spent £5O a day in having the
Federal Convention proceedings cabled to London.
No other English paper went to the expense of
cables.
The first joint parade of Australian mili-
tary forces took place in Adelaide last week, when
the S.A. and Vic. detachments now 7 on their way
to England marched through the streets together.
The Age, in its diatribe against the railway
privileges extended to all and sundry, conspicu-
ously omitted to make any mention of press rail-
way concessions. On one memorable occasion
“ Tommy ” Bent, as Minister of Railways, at-
tempted to wipe the press privileges out, and the
Age never forgave him for it.
In Adelaide, candidates for Parliament
receive from the dailies one report of half-a-
column free of charge; but they can have as
many subsequent speeches reported as they like if
they choose to pay for their insertion. Thus the
public gets an idea that the remarks of a rich
man are always considered of more value by the
press than those of a person whose bank-balance
is either slender or on the wrong side.
Robert Stout, the Cold-Tea agnostic of
Maoriland, objected to Seddon’s surplus, not on
the ground that it didn’t exist, but because it
shouldn’t exist. “Itis a bad thing to have a sur-
plus,” said Stout, “because it leads to reckless
expenditure.” Which is equivalent to saying that
Stout shouldn’t make money, lest he should spend
it foolishly. Yet the hairless Robert, despite his
fast-approaching old age, toils as hard as ever at
accumulating a surplus for himself.
The senior member of S.A. Parliament is
James Martin, M.L.C., who w 7 as 76 years old on
St. George’s Day, April 23. By placing the business
of his firm in the hands of a limited liability co. he
has been able to retain his seat, despite the fact that
he has received tens of thousands of pounds for
building locomotives for Govt. Yet the other day
Willcox was unseated from the Assembly because
he ; had chaff and mail contracts with the Govt,
amounting in the aggregate to not more than ,£3O
or i£4o a year.
The Age rages about the Melb. May Day
people, declaring in so many words that they cele-
brate a day of dynam'ite. The Age is curiously
like the Argus, and speaks just so, in its thoughts
on socialism, communism, and anarchism—mixing
them all into one hell-broth. It does not see that
May Day is a sign of the solidarity of labor.
And its suggestion that Eight Hours Day is
enough for Vic. ! As if the Eight Hours celebra-
tion were anything but a mad dance on the grave
of dead hopes !
At the first division in the new M.L. House
of Representatives, the Seddon Government mus-
tered 44 votes against the Opposition’s 26, and
only three votes were unaccounted for. Unless
Premier Dick grows too overbearing, his Ministry
would seem to have a long life before it yet.
But Seddon put the first spoke of the session in
his own political wheel by declaring that the
grovelsome address to Queen Victoria, with its 26
repetitions of “ your Majesty,” should go whether
the House approved or not. He carried his point,
and the loathsome thing goes, but Seddon will
wear out the loyalty of his supporters if he hurls
his 16 stone of solid brawn on top of them in this
fashion too often.
Premier Reid guffawed wildly at the sug-
gestion that he should leave his resignation behind
him in case of accidents when he went away to
join in the Jubilee flop. Yet both he and every
other absentee Premier certainly should do so,
and if they don’t their conduct (in the language so
dear to the late Parkes) is unconstitoosh’nal. The
Premier is the only man who can send in the
resignation of the Ministry. If he doesn’t leave
the necessary document behind him it seems that
the Ministry will hold office until his return
even in the face of a unanimous vote of censure,
simply because there is no way by which it
can get out of office. If this isn’t unconsti-
toosh’nal what is the constitoosh’n for ?
Britishah : “Conceive the national de-
lirium of joy over the accession of His
Majesty Edward the Seventh, King of England and
of Greater and Little Britain, and Emperor of
India! The press, for once, will be unanimous re the
enormous benefit of having a sovereign to counter-
act the Czar and German Emperor, confound
their politics, frustrate their knavish tricks. The
newspapers will be brimful of the achievements of
Our Gracious Sovereign, his rapid progress through
the universities as a young man, his unparalleled
ability in working up to the distinction of Field
Marshal, and, above all, the true source of Eng-
land’s greatness, the purity of his domestic life.
Take away this, and the nation dies, on all news-
paper showing.”
The tentacles of the Boodlewraith are far-
reaching. Glassey has been Tozerised, and now
Hoolan— the Hoolan —states that he has seen the
error of his ways, and a soft seat is waiting for
him on the Treasury benches. Q. Opposition will
soon consist of lawyer Drake and wharf-laborer
Turley.
A pathetic person
named Hawken writes
to S.M. Herald about
the ad visability of creat-
ing an Australian peer-
age, so that the gilded
Australian girl who
wants a title can marry
a local Lord Boggabri
or Lord Wollongong,
and thus satisfy her
craving without the
money going out of the
country. Hawken may
as well give up hope.
It takes a century or
two before a new nobil-
ity gets over looking
ridiculous, and can
hang its weak nose out
into the world above
its retreating chin with
even a semblance of
dignity. The first thing
the new peerage would
want to do would be to
fly to England with its
rich girl, and take its
coronet into a more
congenial atmosphere,
where a man can be a
peer without everybody
knowing that he is a
perfectly new peer, and
talking about his old
uncle who got lagged,
and his father-in-law
who kept the low grog-
shop ; so the money
would go out of the
country just the same.
A fine Kingstonian
Oonventioh phrase :
“ It would be very hard
to keep an elected Go-
vernor in his proper
place, and induce him
to take the advice of his
constitutional advisers with the appropriate hu-
mility connected with the discharge of his vice-
regal duties.”
Sargood, Melbourne’s martial garter-mer-
chant, has started for London to represent himself
at the great Imperial corroboree. MTntyre has
gone on the same lay. Australia is witnessing an
exodus of its C.M.G.’s and K.C.M.G.’s. If the
lot could have been induced to go, there would
have been a grand opportunity for imposing a
prohibitive duty on titled persons, or passing an
Alien Titles Exclusion Act.
TOO BISKY.
VISITOR: “ No, mum, I hain’t done a hand's turn for four year
come Christmas."
HOSTESS: “Poor fellow —will you cut up a hit of wood for a
shilling?”
VISITOK : “ I’d like to, mum, hut I carn’t-whilst I’d be wurrking in
yer yard someone might come along wantin’ a man for a
LONG job! ”
The rumors about N.S.W. Principal
Under-Secretary Walker’s retirement or trans-
ference to the Agent-Generalship originated, ac-
cording to a Ministerial confidence, in the expressed
anxiety of the Government to recognise that
officer’s services in some exceptional manner. Par-
liament would not stand increasing his salary and
he cannot be promoted to a higher office.
The 200,000 Greeks who are ordered to be
out of Turkey by the 10th of May ought to be a
godsend to the fatherland at the present pinch ;
but, judging by their unwillingness to shift, they
don’t seem to be taking any patriot at present.
There’s more money in fried fish than in the
Leonidas business!
QUITE IN SHYLOCK’S MANNER.
MISS COAXER: “Oh, here’s a nice, big, fat man; he looks as if he could
spare a little!” —(Not known how many pounds he lost.)
Nelson and Byrnes, at present voyaging
Grovel wards, are the two ablest but laziest mem-
bers of Q. Ministry, while Tozer, romancer though
he be, is the only real toiler of the gang. Nelson
and Byrnes will return from the Grovelee fatter,
and lazier, and more Tory than ever. Meantime,
Tozer, it is said, will placate the Labor-party and
the derelict Opposition by an Electoral Reform
Act, and by despatching some of the disappointed
Ministerialists to the Convention will strengthen
his handin that direction. Whattimethegrovellers
get back Tozer, who is greedily ambitious, reckons
he will have opened a really fine political oyster.
Sydney Telegraph :
“ The country the (Transvaal)
republic occupies is British
country to which it obtained
a slender possessory title
through the incompetence of
English politicians.” When
the D.T. starts out to do a
really good British grovel,
history and small things like
that are not allowed to stand
in the way. The Boers started
their republic hundreds of
miles away beyond any Bri-
tish boundary, and their pos-
sessory title came the same
way that the white man’s title
came in Australia—by efflux-
ion of time —and is of just
about the same value. And
there was a time when British
authorities informed the Boers
that they were not British
subjects or on British terri-
tory, and therefore they must
skirmish for themselves if the
Zulu or any other black
marauder chanced to come
their way.
Townsville (Q.) munici-
pal council recently petitioned
Acting-Premier Tozer for the
release of prisoners serving
sentences for offences commit-
ted during the strike of a few
years ago. Tozer dodged the
appeal on the ground that
“ the Legislature had not em-
powered local authorities to
make requests of that nature,”
and therefore the petition
couldn’t be received. Which
was a weak and foolish excuse,
even for Tozer. There is no
law authorising Tozer to go to
bed at night, so by his own
logic that is illegal, as well
as almost everything else
that he does. The theory
that a thing is unlawful unless
it is specifically permitted by
Act of Parliament is a new
version of things. Where is
the Act of Parliament author-
ising Tozer to have indiges-
tion, for instance, or to wear
a macintosh when it rains ?
It seems like the uprising of the ghost of
a very old dead friend to see in the N.S.W.
Ministerial programme for this session the old
familiar item “ A bill extending the area of
Local Government.” It is probably 30 years since
a session opened in N.S.W. without some refer-
ence to that Local Government Bill which has
never got through to this day.
Premier Reid, in the course of his finan-
cial statement, remarked proudly on the enormous
increase in shipping at Sydney under Freetrade
during the last nine months. Of course there has
been an enormous increase of shipping. Some-
thing like three dozen vessels loaded with foreign
grain alone have arrived within the last year, and
dumped down their vast cargoes on the Sydney
wharves to make the local farmer’s produce un-
saleable. The mere arrival of ships isn’t neces-
sarily a sign of prosperity. It is very often a
sign of famine. And quite a phenomenal ship-
ping-record has been put up by a foreign fleet
dropping in and bombarding a place.
Last year, Maoriland’s population showed
a larger percentage of increase than that of Vic-
toria, N.S.W. or S. A. Including Westralia,
which had the phenomenal advance of 36 per cent.,
the average increase was 2 per cent, all round, and
Maoriland, with nearly per cent, was well
ahead of the average. Considering that Maori-
land has tried more ‘ ‘ mad socialistic experiments ”
than all the other provinces together, including
State Bank, State Land Bank, female suffrage,
drastic factory legislation, Protection, graduated
land-tax, absentee-tax, resumption of land for
closer settlement, State life insurance, &c., and is
now preparing to experiment on old-age pensions,
a fair-rent system, and other things, this is not
a bad result.
Premier Seddon, when in London, is going
to “confer” with the debenture-holders of the
busted Midland Railway, and endeavor to fix up
an “amicable settlement.” Which means that
the financiers in question will bite the ear of some
influential person, and he will diplomatically bite
Seddon’s ear, and at some appropriate time
perhaps when Seddon has just been made a
K.C.M.G. —they will “ confer ” through a genial
haze of champagne and cigar-smoke, and come to
a settlement. Considering that the Midland Rail-
way syndicate broke its contract in a dozen places,
and that arbitrator Blake decided it had no
shadow of a case, Maoriland has everything to
lose and nothing to gain by conferring, unless it is
done on a hard, business-like basis. And no Pre-
mier can be trusted with such a matter at Jubilee
time. Jubilee and business don’t go well together.
Liberty, the organ of N.S..W. National
Ass, sees a dreadful bad time coming when the
people who owe money to the State Land Bank
will be a majority of the voting power, and will
consequently decide that they needn’t pay interest
or repay the principal unless'they like. Similarly
the people who owe the public debt and who own
the railways, being the whole voting-power, will
probably enact that they will travel free, and that
they will pay no more interest, and that the princi-
pal may go hang. Also, the people who owe mnnev
privately, being far more numerous than timoe
who have money owing to them, will pass a law
that all debts shall be irrecoverable. But as the
people show no inclination to swindle the foreign
creditor, the assumption that they are always
lying in wait for a chance to swindle themselves
when they are their own creditor is pretty thin.
Also, it is pretty stupid.

grovel:—
To the Queen’s Most Excellent Majesty. Most Gracious
Sovereign, —We, the members of the Legislative Council
and House of Representatives of the colony of New Zea-
land in Parliament assembled, desire to render to the
Almighty an expression of our gratitude for so prolonging
your Majesty’s life that your Majesty’s reign has exceeded
that of any of your predecessors on the Throne. We beg
your Majesty to accept our heartfelt congratulations on
your having occupied that Throne for sixty years ; and we
rejoice that upon this anniversary of your Majesty’s
accession your Majesty's subjects in all portions of your
dominions are afforded an opportunity of expressing
their feelings of loyalty and affection. The colony of
New Zealand, which was founded shortly after your
Majesty’s accession, is the first-born of the colonies of
your Majesty’s reign ; and we desire to assure your
Maj
Sudden stoppage just here while The Bulletin
has convulsions on the floor. There are 26 “ your
Majesty’s ” in the document altogether.


While retrenchment is the watchword, why
do Australian Parliaments continue to maintain,
in all his opera-bouffe dignity, the functionary
known as “ Black Rod ” ?
Britain pays half-a-million a year for
Queen Victoria and her relations merely because
it rejoices that she is as honest as every other
woman ought to be.”

Public Notices.
pUBLIO SERVIC E BOARD.
60 Young-street, Sydney,
9th April, 1897.
The Public Service Board hereby notify that the next
COMPETITIVE EXAMINATION for JUNIOR CLERK-
SHIPS in the PUBLIC SERVICE of the colony will be
held on the 28th, 29th, and 30th days of JUNE next, at
various centres, which will be fixed to suit the conveni-
ence of candidates. The estimated number of appoint-
ments to be made during the year is 50. Candidates must
be over sixteen and under twenty-one years of age on the
first day of examination.be of good moral character, in
sound health, and have resided in the colony for at least
three years at the date of application.
Every person who desires to submit himself to examin-
ation must forward to the Secretary to the Board, to
reach him not later than the 7th June, 1897, a statement,
in his own handwriting, giving his full name and address,
and his age, and notifying in which of the principal towns
of the colony he wishes to be examined. He must also
submit, with his application, an extract from the register
in which his birth is recorded, or other satisfactory evi-
dence of his age ; a certificate to the effect that he" is of
good moral character, signed by his teacher, if he is at-
tending school, or has ceased to attend school for a shorter
period than six months ; by a clergyman or Justice of the
Peace or other responsible person' if it is more than six
months since he left school; and a certificate from a repu-
table person to the effect that the applicant has resided
in the colony for at least three years.
A list of registered candidates will be advertised, with
the times and places of examination ; and, in addition,
every candidate registered will be personally notified of the
time and place when and where he must present himself.
Every candidate whose name is not registered by the
Board will be so informed, with the reason why registra-
tion is refused.
The subjects in which candidates will be examined are
—Handwriting, English (dictation, grammar, and com-
position), Arithmetic (full course), Geometry (Euclid,
First Book), Outlines of Physical and Political Geography,
and the Geography of the Australasian Colonies in
moderate detail, English History from the middle of the
Eighteenth Century to the present time, especially with
reference to the colonies and dependencies. Candidates
will also be allowed to take any tw’o, but not more than
two, of the following subjects : —Mathematics, English—a
prescribed author—(the same as that prescribed for
the Junior Examination of the current year), Latin,
French, German, Elementary Chemistry, and Elementary
Geology. Every candidate who wishes to be examined in
the extra subjects must give notice of his desire in his
application.
The fee for examination will be 10st, which must be paid
to a Clerk of Petty Sessions or into the Treasury, and the
receipt lodged with the Secretary of the Board 10 days
before the examination. Every successful candidate must
produce a medical certificate to the effect that he is in
sound health, and free from physical defects, before he will
be allowed to enter upon his duties.
The regulations governing the examinations appeared
in a supplement to the Government Gazette dated Ist
April, 1897.
• By order-of the Board,
J. VV. HOLLIMAN-, Secretary.

The total paid-up capital of the Bank now stands at £1,965,366 17s. 9d., being an increase of
£47,155 Bs. during the half-year.
The long-dated fixed deposits have been reduced from £3,265,633 12s. 6d. to £3,015,563 10s. 2d.
during the six months, and encouraged by the success last year in paying off before maturity part of

The ordinary general.meeting will be held on Wednesday, sth May, at noon, at the Head Office,
Collins-street, Melbourne.
In conformity with the articles of association, the Honorable Joseph Major Pratt, M.L.C., and
the Honorable Sir William Austin Zeal, K.C.M.G., M.L.C., retire from the board on that occasion.
These gentlemen both offer themselves for re-election. There are no other candidates.
The shareholders will also be called on to appoint two auditors for the year, and to fix their
remuneration. The gentlemen now acting in that capacity are Mr. W. H. Tuckett and Mr. Andrew
Burns. —By order of the Board of Directors. JOHN G. ADDISON, Acting Chief Manager.

AUDITORS’ REPORT.
To the Shareholders of the National Bank of Australasia Limited.
Ladies and Gentlemen, —
We repoort that we have have audited the accounts of your Bank for the half-year ending
31st March, 1897, and that in our opinion the balance-sheet and accounts have been drawn up in
accordance with the provisions of the Companies Acts, and present a correct view of the state of the
Company’s affairs.
The London office return to 27th February last, duly audited by Messrs. Price, Waterhouse and
Co., has been submitted to us, the assets and liabilities at the branches being taken on the certificates
of the managers and accountants.
We have received every assistance from the officers of the Bank, and they have supplied us with
all information sought by us in course of the audit. —We are, Ladies and Gentlemen, yours faithfully,
W. H. TUCKETT \ .

I, JOHN GOLLAN ADDISON, Acting Chief Manager of THE NATIONAL BANK OF
AUSTRALASIA LIMITED, do solemnly and sincerely declare—
That I am the Acting Chief Manager of The National Bank of Australasia Limited.
That the liability of the members of the Company is limited.
That the capital of the Company is £4,000,000, divided into 400,000 shares of £lO each, of which
150,000 are preference shares, and the remaining 250,000 are ordinary shares.

A SAILOR’S NARROW ESCAPE.
HEAT) STRUCK A PIER WHILE ROWING
ASHORE WITH THE LINE FROM A
SHIP, AND FELL INTO THE WATER
BRUISED AND INSENSIBLE.
When a member of our staff went in search of
Mr. John Smith, of 4 Wilmot-street, Sydney, he
fortunately found him at home, and on his re-
marking that the object of his visit was to obtain
particulars of a narrow escape from death he had
heard Mr. Smith had lately experienced, found no
difficulty in obtaining the same.
“Yes, it is quite true that I have had a narrow
escape, ” said Mr. Smith, “ and if you want to hear
about it, I will tell you with pleasure. I was down
at Howard Smith’s wharf on the 19th of Novem-
ber last, where I was generally employed, and at
the time I met my accident was taking a line ashore
from an incoming steamer. Through colliding
with a post whilst in the boat I got thrown into
the water, receiving a severe injury to my head in
addition to being half drowned by the quantity of
dirty water I swallowed. I was take nto the
Sydney Hospital, where I remained three weeks. ”
“ Did they do you much good, Mr. Smith ?”
“No, indeed ; they only patched me up for the
time being, and it was lucky for me that I got a
bit of an easy job minding a gate at the wharf, for
I was so desperately weak and debilitated that I
could not attempt to take on hard work. How-
ever, I managed to stay on for five weeks, and
returned home for three or four days rest, which I
was badly in need of. I then went to see the
Government Medical Officer, who said my case
was incurable, and wanted me to go to Liverpool
Asylum, but this I refused to do, so I ultimately
returned to Sydney Hospital, where I remained
three weeks.
“ Did you feel any better after that, Mr. Smith ? ”
“ No, decidedly worse ; in fact, twice over worse,
and was glad to return home again.”
“How did you feel affected, Mr. Smith ?”
‘ Chiefly spitting of blood, which would keep me
awake day and night. Mrs. Rees, my landlady,
can tell you how bad I was; in fact, she thought
it was all over with me and that I should not be
long for this world.”
“ How did you hear of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills,
which we were told was what pulled you together
again ? ”
“ Through a pamphlet being left at the house.
I thought at first that the Pills were too expensive
for me, but Mrs. Rees persuaded me to try them,
so I got a box and started to take them according
to the printed directions. After the first box I
felt wonderfully better, the blood-spitting almost
left me, my appetite returned ; indeed, I was
better than before my accident. It is a remarkably
rare thing for me to spit blood at all now.”
“ Do you attribute this beneficial effect to Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills ? ”
“ I do entirely ; in fact, it must be so for I have
taken no other medicine. Before my accident I
always enjoyed the best of health, having led a
healthy outdoor life, so it proves my accident must
have been a very serious one to knock me up as it
did. I have the firmest belief in the virtues of Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills and so has Mrs. Rees, my
landlady. (Mrs. Rees here certainly corroborated
Mr. Smith’s statements in every particular.) I
have,” continued Mr. Smith, “strongly recom-
mended these Pills to all my friends and ac-
quaintances, and they have also a strong advocate
in Mrs. Rees, who is a firm believer in them.”
(Signed) JOHN SMITH,
4 Wilmot-street,
Off Liverpool-street,
Sydney.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People are not
a patent medicine, but a thoroughly scientific
preparation, the result of years of careful study on
the part of an eminent Edinburgh University phy-
sician, and they were successfully used by him in
his every day practice for years before being
offered for general sale. They positively cure
Rheumatism, Sciatica, Lumbago, and Neuralgia,
and Influenza’s evil after effects, and are a specific
for all diseases of women, such as ansemia, poor
and watery blood, female irregularities, nervous
headache and hysteria. They are not a purgative
medicine, but brace up and permanently strengthen
the whole system.

At Poverty Point.
As benefits are plentiful just now, what’s the matter
with one for poor old Hasker, the oldest of Australian
actors, not even excepting George Coppin, The Bulletin
believes? The veteran, who is now getting on towards 80,
has others dependent on him, and is in a most pitiable
state of poverty.
An Australian journalist abroad writes a Bulletin man
re two English idols, Irving and Terry : “ Their perform-
ance showed me how clever English advertising fools the
world. I saw them in ‘ The Merchant of Venice.’ Irving’s
Shylock was absolutely the stagiest performance I have
ever seen. It was a solo all through, the other characters
simply standing off to give him air. In the trial scene his
acting was positive burlesque. His exits were marvels of
misapplied ingenuity ; he had evidently studied hard to
go off as no sane man free of blind-staggers or stringhalt
ever before went out of any door on earth below. Ellen
Terry is an elderly, slouchy, faded, hoarse person, who
walks as if afflicted with confluent bunions She smiles
and smiles continuously with an indulgent good-nature
at benighted audiences that, in the name of art and
common sense, should drive her away totheold armchair by
the fireside of her sweet grandchildren, whom she is now
eminently qualified to entertain.”
“ When you play in Sydney,” said Butler to Maggie
Moore, “ send me a pass and I shall be there in a front
seat.” “ No, you shall not,” replied the actress ; “ you
shall have a box.”
Miss Mabel Hardy, one of the girls whom the ill-fated
Dacres brought out with them to Australia, and who
went back to London after the Sydney tragedy, has
abandoned the stage and taken to shorthand and type-
writing in the Big Smoke. Miss Hardy was a well-read
girl, and had brains, but not brains of the actress order.
Moreover, she hadn’t a face for the stage.
Walter Kirby’s appearance on the Melb. concert plat-
form predisposes people in his disfavor. A very sheepish
look, an aggressive forelock, pigeon-toes, ill-made
clothes, move an audience to irritability; but his tenor
fetches them.
S.S. : “ I am grateful for Mrs. Brown-Potter’s gowns by
Worth, of Paris, or whoever may have succeeded that
worthy as Mrs. B.P.’s collaborator, and she wears them
wonderfully well. The Bulletin has piled derision upon
the association of the names of 8.-Potter and Worth on
the playbill, and, as a consequence, the artist who drapes
the actress is now never mentioned in any society. Mrs.
Potter performing is sometimes irritating, and—because
of the great variety and astonishing originality of her
errors, like flea visits, and the sudden, dazzling touches
of perfection, all illusive and incoherent—she cannot be
located artistically. She worries one and wearies one,
but has charms of method and flashes of genius that deny
one the consolations of complete resentment. As an
actress you cannot love her, you cannot hate her, and you
cannot be indifferent; but as a ‘gown-trotter’ she is
invariably delightful, and surely to select and wear a
dress and take a position so as to convey to an artistic
perception that swift, subtle, living, lasting delight a’
beautiful painting gives is an ability deserving of grateful
praise and some eminence among the plastic arts. It is
an ability rare enough to be precious, Heaven knows ! I
will never forget the picture of Cora 8.-P. in the 4th act
of ‘The Ironmaster,’ coming down the broad stairway at
the back of the stage, looking like the pale maiden of
‘ Love’s Pilgrimage ’ in Melb. Art Gallery, clothed in sor-
row and contrition, and jewelled with starry tears.”
Bellew has been wearing his own hair rather freety in
Melbourne of late, so that the young heroes in recent
performances have nearly all been grey-headed, which
may account for the fact that those handsome, white-
haired fossils, the Tasmanian Convention delegates, never
appeared in public in Melbourne without a score or more
of adoring maidens in tow.
A strange fatality seems to attach to the Opera House
at Wellington (M.L.), one of the finest and most con-
venient oi Australasian playhouses. Twice have buildings
erected there been burnt down. A few months ago cus-
todian G. Coulson, w'hilst pulling one of the stage ropes,
broke a blood vessel and dropped dead; and, just the
other day, Frank Roberts, a well-known athlete, who suc-
ceeded Coulson in the custodianship, was found alongside
the same rope, also with the blood pouring from a vessel,
as in Coulson’s case. When the mail left, Roberts was
lying on the threshold of the Unknown. A Maori
tohunga is said to have declared, when the first building
was erected on the site, that it would never prosper, be-
cause it was on taboo’d ground, and the old seer saw
pretty clearly.
Neither “ Forget-Me-Not ” nor “ Thbrbse ” will be pro-
duced during the present Potter-Bellew Melb. season.
The first not being played, the public will be prevented
from seeing the play that was to accompany it—the work
of the embryo Haddon Chambers, of whom the world
hears so much and sees so little. The withdrawal of
“ Th6rfese ” will be a disappointment to many, prurient
and otherwise. Next to “ Francillon,” it is undoubtedly
the most artistic thing the pair do, and the play, accord-
ing to Wm. Archer, is the best psychological drammer up
to date —and he ought to know.
Poor old Sims Reeves figured again in the Bankruptcy
Court lately with unsecured liabilities amounting to
£1042 against assets practically nil. The financial state
of the worn-out idol should appeal to the English musical
world. He was not a worthy idol, judged by modern
opinion of the trashy ballads which he made famous, but
he sang to suit the times in which his voice flourished,
and, anyway, the original adorer of “ Pretty Jane ” and
the sympathetic friend of “Tom Bowling ought not to
be left to drag the tail-end of his career through the dust.
Lieut.-Colonel Bingham, alas 1 has decided not to appear
in the forthcoming production of “ Lelamine,” at Melb.
Princess’. The gallant Colonel sang “ Killarney ” at a
public concert, by way of testing the effect of his muffled
baritone upon an unbiassed audience, and next day he
scratched himself for the comic-opera engagement. Mr.
E. H. ShacWl is to fill the billet vacated bv the prudent
warrior.
In view of Nat Goodwin’s free-and-easy fabrications re-
garding his Australian experiences, and his adverse
opinions upon our public, some of his remarks while still
with us are worth recalling. “So far as the public is con-
cerned,” he told a Melb. interviewer, “ I have never
played before more appreciative audiences, and the pieces
I have put on have never gone better anywhere. . . .
The public gets puzzled and no doubt prejudiced by the
peculiar press notices of the plays that are published on
Monday mornings. . . . Criticism is a valuable friend
to actors when it is sound, but when it chiefly consists of
arrogant assumption, inspired by ignorance, it serves no
purpose whatever except to fill up a certain space set
apart for it in a newspaper.” Humph !
Bellew prides himself on his attention to minute detail,
and always ostentatiously displays a copy of Le Petit
Journal in “ Francillon.” But fancy the aristocratic
Comte de Riverolles reading that dull bourgeois rag with
its circulation of half a million !
Should “Charlotte Corday” be produced in London,
Malcolm Watson, or some other literary hack, will pro-
bably polish it up. At present it has only one stale, soli-
tary joke in it—about a dog being painted so naturally
that he bit the artist, who died—and that owes its birth
to the comedian who first played the painfully humorous
barber. Bellew originally intended to play “ Adam Lux,”
Marat’s big melodramatic scene only being sandwiched in
at the last moment. The Potters were specially anxious
to play “Corday” on the recent M.L. tour, but its failure
in Melb. and Adelaide put J.C.W. dead against it, so after
its one-night production in Sydney it was heard of no
more.
Biking down Mordialloc road (Melb.) the other day, Miss
Elizabeth Watson noticed a small boy seated on a rail in
an attitude of eager expectation. Just then the doomed
actress arrived at an awkward turn in the thoroughfare,
and over went the bike. “My word, you was lucky ! ”
said the boy, “ I’ve seen firteen uvvers come down worse’n
that." The awful infant had perched himself there, with
his lunch in his pocket, to watch for catastrophes, and the
idea of a “ gel ” not getting hurt was almost more than he
could bear. Miss W. left him grumbling away to himself,
“ My word, she was lucky !”
Forbes Robertson and Mrs. Pat Campbell seem to
strike, with Nelson and Lady Hamilton. In N.Y. “ George
IV.” is just staged. Edifying. The next»step will be a
play about William IV. and Dora Jordan, ancestress of
the Duke of Fife. Apropos the duke and his great-grand-
mother (Dora Jordan, the actress). Observe how Fife’s
adoption into the Royal Family has legitimatised Dora’s
kingly bastards. Thus Time’s whirligig brings his
revenges.
Melb. : “ Mrs. G. B. W. Lewis’s dramatic pupils, about
60 in number, have given her a group of their photos.,
now on view. All are, or have been, stage-struck, pre-
tending they were not.”
An original curtain-raiser, by a member of the Potter-
Bellew Co., called “ A Studio Episode,” was to have been
played this Melb. season, but it is now out of the bills.
Cora P. says she hasn’t the necessary dresses. The play-
let was once performed in Melbourne, and went well, but
has, to an experienced managerial eye, the great defect
of an unhappy ending. Tragedy doesn’t condense itself
successfully on the Australian stage. “ Cavalleria ” would
have been twice as popular here had it ended with Sau-
tuzza’s wedding.
The good-hearted Maggie Moore samr to prisoner Butler
on the voyage from ’Frisco. Butler responded with
“Mother Would Comfort. -Me” and “Sweet Rosy
O’Grady,” “ and the detectives joined in the chorus.”
Pro. : “Bland Holt finds horses lucky. Yet he was un-
lucky with the first one, introduced for the ‘Union Jack,’
at Melb. Royal, a production which lost him £2000.”
Potter and Bellew have booked their passage by the
Victoria. They leave in her on the 12th prox., after a
month in Bendigo, Ballarat and Adelaide.
Melb. : “The Pottew-Belter combination, or the Bellow-
Potluck, ought to have given its ‘Thdrfese Raquin ’ just
this once. But J.C.W. is so confoundedly pious.”
“ Musical comedy mostly arises because a fool is in love
with a chorus-girl, and has half-a-crown in a syndicate.
He loses both.”
A certain Australian girl singer, of whom much was to
be expected, is said to have taken to drink. Which is
more than was to be expected.
“Nothing wrong inside the dress—judging by what
appears outside.”— Francillon.
Old friend Fred Kaye gets reprimanded by London
D.T. for foisting his brusque “take-away-the -tea”
method upon a character in Justin M'Carthy’s new
comedy “My Friend the Prince” (adapted from a
’Murkan farce). The critic says : “ Mr. Fred Kaye’s style
may, perhaps, be too boisterous, self-assertive, and noisy
for the old millionaire. It is the shout of the barrack-
yard rather than the country house, and no doubt
popularity in plays of another pattern has deadened the
actor —dry, funny, all the same —to a sense of proportion.”
Harper’s Weekly (last file) says Palmer’s Trilby co.,
which came to Australia, was superior to Beerbohm Tree
and his co.
Miss Lucy Don, a daughter of George Coppin, and
younger sister of Miss Daisy, figures in the fire drama at
Melb. Royal as “ the Panther’s ” maid.
Melb. : “ A thing of beauty and joy for ever : Eight
ladies in line with bare heads, Princess amphitheatre,
last night.”
Lilian Tree has been singing at London Palace of Varie-
ties. She sang as leading soprano with the Mendelssohn-
Barry Concert Co. She was also on in Grand Italian
Opera last season. The soprano, however, from latest
advices, is not overjoyed with her lot, and seriously con-
templates resigning secular life for a religious vocation.
Several nunneries have already intimated their willing-
ness to receive her as musical directress.
Melba and Marchesi are not the staunch friends they
were. A dispute has arisen re the effects of cycling upon
the vocal cords. Melba, persuaded by Jean de Reszke,
has taken to this pastime, against Marchesi’s injunctions.
Marchesi contends that the attitude assumed in cycling is
injurious to the chest and lungs, and that rapid pro-
gression through the air is detrimental to the throat.
The other day Sydney Amateur Orchestral Society
offered a prize of £lO 10s. for the best original march.
Delaney, Sydney Moss, and Hazon, adjudicators, are of
opinion that none of the compositions sent in are worthy
of a prize. A lack of originality and weak orchestration
seem to have pervaded the eight marches submitted.
The musical community of Sydney will welcome Mr.
John Lemmone, the famous flautist and accompanist to
Madame Melba, on his return from England, at the Hotel
Australia, this (Saturday) night, May 15.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
When the totalisator is being legalised in Vic-
toria and N.S.W., which will be shortly, at least 10 per
cent, of its profits should be devoted by Parliament to the
fund for jockeys’ distressed widows and mothers. Will
the Govt. Statists please oblige in future with the race-
courses’ annual butcher’s bill? The extent of jockey-
slaughter is simply appalling, but very little is said about
it. The poor devil is carted off in that expectant hearse,
the ambulance ; a few people say “ How dreadful! ” a few
sovereigns are collected for the 'widow or the mother at
the settling ; and it is all over but this—
DEATH.
Gardiner.— At Kensington Racecourse, Saturday,
May 1, Tom Gardiner, thrown whilst riding Idica in
Hurdle Race ; died in transit to hospital.

S.A.J.C. Autumn meeting began on Saturday
last at Adelaide with some first-class racing. The New-
market failure, Staffa, is not so good or reliable as his
connections believe, for he was beaten in Goodwood
Handicap by Orient at a difference of 41b., whilst in Feb.,
at Sandown Park, he easily gave that animal 61b. Fleet
Admiral (Richmond—Footstep) carried his 141 b. penalty
to victory in S.A. Stakes, and paid £4 7s. on the tote.
Even money would have been Ikey’s best offer. Thunder
Queen accounted for the St. Leger, paying £3. Ikey, in
this case, would have wanted" a shade of odds. Hova
broke down badly in Elder Stakes, won by N.S.W. cast-
off Lieutenant.

R. Lewis, the crack Vic. lightweight, rode
three winners straight off the reel at Adelaide last Satur-
day. Out of 120 mounts this season, he has been first on
30 occasions. A good horseman and a “ straight goer,”
this lad has the promise of a big future.
* * » *
At pony-racing Kensington the Leger patrons,
who pay but one shilling, are favored with a board dis-
playing jockeys’ names, &c. ; but at its next-door neigh-
bor, Randwick, the Legerite is bled for 3s. and has to
find out the names of the riders as best he can. At
Flemington, Vic., where the “ flatite ” pays nothing, an
elaborate board has of late been provided. Another
grievance the humbler patrons of racing in N.S.W. have
is the stiff charge, in almost every case, of a shilling for a
race-book. The sixpenny card could well be introduced
* * * *
The M.L. two-year-old Multiform (Hotchkiss—
Formo) has won the record sum of £2700 this season. As
his stable companion, Gold Medallist, is a few lb. his
superior, most of the chief three-year-old events should
fall to Mr. G. G. Stead next season.
Re “ Surcingle,” was not the Leviathan book-
maker Davis a Hebrew? A number of the English lead-
ing ready-money bookies belong to the Chosen ; though
the majority of Tatt.’s members are non-Semitic.
* * * *
The English Derby promises to be unusually
interesting this year, as, barring accidents, the three
cracks, Velasquez, Galtee More, and Vesuvian will com-
pete. Though Vesuvian is the worst favorite of the trio,
’tis on the cards that he will win, as he was very green as
a two-year-old, but is now reported as having developed
into a splendid colt. Wood will ride Galtee More ; J.
Watts, Velasquez; and M. Cannon may be seen’ on
Vesuvian.
* * * *
If Rebel Queen starts in Warwick Earm handi-
cap to-day (Sat.), she will probably prove that there was
reason for her being so heavily backed in the Final at
recent Randwick Meeting. Mr. Kelso, whose luck was
dead out at A.J.O. meeting, promises to be dangerous
in several of to-day’s events.
* * * *
That promising hurdle-racer, Jack Hanwell,
who won a race at the recent A.J.O. meet, has gone into
Scobie’s stable, Ballarat. If well-treated by the handi-
capper he should have a great say in the Flemington G.N.
Hurdles.
* * * *
If the handicapper at ’Oine treats the flying
Maluma in the Royal Hunt Cup at Ascot with the same
leniency he did in the City 7 and Suburban, the Jersey
Lily’s colors will likely be carried first past the post, as
the straight mile will just suit the long-striding daughter
of Malua.
■ * * *
Bathurst has the best half-mile track in N.S.W.
—level as a billiard-table and with well-banked turns—
and it was on this course Mr. Buckland brought his
phenomenal trotter, Fritz, out each day of the Show for
an exhibition mile. Mr. B. assured the writer that his
horse had only been in the sulky three times in the pre-
vious three weeks, and Fritz showed a little of his cranky
Van Cleve blood by refusing to make a start until coaxed
with a lead from a saddle-horse. Once started, however,
the wonder cut out a mile in 2.18. On the second day he
trotted the mile in exactly the same time ; but on the
third day, with a couple of galloping horses alongside, he
went over the distance in 2.16. Such a performance by
an untrained horse, on a half-mile course, makes one
begin to wonder what he could knock off in pink of con-
dition and on a kite-shaped track such as Yankeedom
makes its records upon.
Mr. Buckland turned up with a team such as
might be expected from a crack Yankee stable. Fritz;
St. Louis, his stallion, by Burlington B. ; Burlington
Junior, a great raking 17-hands three-year-old chestnut
pacing stallion, by the same sire; and Viva, Vida, and
Hark, three mares by Van Cleve, the latter standing about
13.3. Hark’s fame as a good little ’un had spread amongst
trotting men, but she did not show anything wonderful,
as, although she won the three-minute class from scratch,
she did not prove herself better than 2.50, a gait insuf-
ficient to extend little ones such as Edith or Isabel, so
well-known in the past on Sydney courses.
Dr. Slate took up Silver Cloud and his hand-
some little Huon stallion, Narragansett, and Mr. Harry
Knight had one representative in the winner of the Syd-
ney show double, Siren 11. The time-handicap race in
heats, best two out of three, was a terribly dished-up
affair, quite unworkable, and showed that the committee
is in need of advice by a practical trotting man in forming
its programme. Perhaps the greatest blot upon the
meeting was in not putting back St. Louis and Hark for
their wins on the previous day. It did not make any dif-
ference to St. Louis, as he collided with a post, throwing
out his driver, Piper, and smashing up a new pneumatic
sulky; but as regards Hark, it left her to win with ease
what might otherwise have proved an exciting race.
In the heat handicap something went wrong
with Siren 11., and she tore off a couple of her boots,
looking at one time very much like “going to market,”
but she soon settled down. In the final two-mile handi-
cap Siren was handicapped at 20sec. behind, Viva at 25,
and on performances it certainly looked as if the handi-
capper had made a mistake in placing Viva on 25 instead
of 35. But Mr. Buckland drove the mare badly, and, taking
her off her legs before she had time to settle into her
stride, she showed temper and performed more like a
green colt in harness for the first time. Siren 11. showed
her true form, and, trotting like a piece of machinery in
the style shown by her at Sydney Show, won, pulling up
in 5.29, which, taking off her 20see. handicap, makes her
record 2.34 A. Siren is by Van Cleve, and was bred by
Mr. Buckland and sold when a yearling for 100 guineas.
Had Mr. Buckland let Viva settle down, and she had
trotted up to her form, what a finish the half-sisters
would have made of it. Mr. Buckland had certainly a
great outing. Of the six events on the programme he
won five, and the sixth was won by a mare bred by him.
The jumping at Bathurst Show was certainly
very fine, and far ahead of the Sydney Show, no doubt
the splendid exhibition-ground having much to do with
it. In the high jump a boy-rider was unearthed who pro-
bably now holds a world’s record. He is a son of Mr.
Walter Riddle, a well-known Cowra man very fond of a
jumping horse. Mr. Riddle’s 12-3 7 ear-old son, Dave, rode
two horses in the high jump—Rainbow, a little horse that
performed so well at Sydney Show, and Streak-o’-Light,
a big bay owned by his father, which, by the way, was
only five days out of the paddock. Rainbow retired at
sft. Bin., an inch higher than he got over in Sydney, but
young Riddle kept Streak-o’-Light going until* only
another horse, Lawyer, was left in, and at dusk on Friday
each of the pair had 6ft. |in. to its credit. It was
decided to jump it off next morning on account of the
failing light. On Saturday morning Lawyer again cleared
the bar at 6ft. |in., but young Riddle failed to get Streak-
o’-Light over again.

Druid : “ The V.R.C. has cut down its gatemen

* * * *
Many wondered who Godfrey, who batted for
Giffen’s team against Coolgardie, could be. It was <J. G.
Godfrey, now an Adelaide broker, who made a cen-
tury for S.A. against Vernon’s English team years ago.
Giffen and Godfrey were batting over-night,' with the
game against S.A., but some patriotic Crow-eater watered
the wicket during the night, and the two put on a big
score on a perfect wicket next day, and saved the match!
Godfrey’s innings was one of the finest played on Adelaide
Oval.

Clem Hill probably holds a big-match record in
stumping a batsman off Jones, the lightning-streak.
Kelly wisely stands back to him and thus saves byes and
takes catches. Apropos .- Brunton, Jonah’s S. Adelaide
club-mate, recently put up an unenviable record by
allowing 48 byes out of a score cf 150.

Dear Bulletin,—“ Melbourne ” chips into the
Artful Union discussion. He says the Druids’ Gala helps
on the cause of the “Druids as a whole,” because indivi-
dual Druids get a stiff commission (he says 33, I thought
it was 25, per cent.) out of the proceeds. He might as
well say that the cause of “Australia as a whole” is
helped on because the David Henrys-of the place get big
sums as usury. .Anyhow, why should a friendly society
have gambling privileges which would be denied to any
individual member of same ?—Gambler.
Why do some Sydney papers still speak of
George Seale as “the amateur champion ? ” According to
the recognised law the man who teaches athletics is as
much a pro. as he who lives by fighting matches.
* * ♦ *
It appears that Fitz. settled Corbett with tha
blow over the heart. This, first popularised by Jem
Smith, and often used with great effect by Black Jackson,
should revolutionise boxing. For instance, it can have
little effect on the boxer who adopts the unusual attitude
of George Seale and a few others who shape with the right
hand and foot forward.

Frosty Face Fogo : “In reports of Fitzsimmons
v. Corbett, I miss the flowery language of the vore-time
Bell’s Life, with the classic aroma its vocabulary gave,
quite Choctaw to the outside world. Thus the fists were
mauleys, dukes, bunches o’ fives; the eyes, ogles, peepers;
the mouth, the gob or ’tater-trap ; the throat, the squeeze;
the nose, the conk; the head, the knowledge-box ; the
stomach, the bread-basket; and blood was always claret.”
» * * *
Mrs. Fitzsimmons occupied a box at the big
fight, and when her Robert banged J. Corbett she clapped
her hands and laughed, but when J. Corbett bathed her
hubby in gore she wept and told Robert to “go for the
body, as it was the only way to whip the hound.” Such
is the second Mrs. Fitzsimmons—who seems to have a
level head, for it is on her advice that hubby now retires
from the ring.
• • * •
Fitzsimmons has sent Mr. Larry Foley a pair
of diamond-studded sleeve-links inscribed: “From the
Champion Boxer to the Champion Teacher.” By the way,
John L. Sullivan ascribes Fitz’s victory to Mrs. F.’s excel-
lent and continuous advice during the fight—to “go for
Corbett’s ribs—it’s no good hitting him on the jaw.” The
lady evidently measured man’s jaw with a feminine
bushel.

How about an aluminium-bike ? The metal is
strong and tough as steel, would not need enamelling or
plating, and would reduce the weight of a racer to, say,
161 b.

On the day Harris landed from Australia he
won the mile scratch at Coventry—which speaks well for
his staying form. Harris was the first to race a trotting-
horse in Australia. The result, however, was a failure,
Mystery winning both heats easily.


Many of the Hospital - Saturday lady
collectors charmed with an aroma like that of
Araby the Blest—the fact being that Pinaud’s
firm had presented each lady with six bottles of
their famous perfumes.*
The first shipment of cocoa-nut oil from
the new Sydney factory of Lever Brothers was
despatched by the Lord Ripon, and with that
noble British instinct which celebrates every im-
portant event with victuals, a massive lunch was
given to celebrate the occasion. Lever Brothers’
enterprise promises, so far, to be an unqualified
success, and it has already been decided to double
the machinery and greatly enlarge the output.
Indirectly, too, it should have an important bear-
ing on the N.S.W. dairy-industry. Cocoa-nut
cake, which is one of the bye-products of the in-
dustry, is an excellent cow-food, and in a land
where the grass-supply goes down to nothing
in the frequent times of drought the need of
some such artificial food is urgent. In Europe,
the annual product of the cow is about 500 gallons
per annum; in N.S.W., it is about 275 gallons,
and the difference mainly arises through allowing
the precarious animal to shift for itself in good
seasons and bad. A whole lot of cornstalks, who
hadn’t energy enough to start the cocoanut-oil in-
dustry themselves, despite the fact that the raw
material is at their doors, turned up to applaud
the British firm which has seized the opportunity,
and the usual remarks about the advantages of
having British capital in the country, and of
having our industries run for us by the more ener-
getic Bull, and of seeing the profits depart for
Britain, were made in the usual feeling manner.

A New Australian Novel ,
Harold EfFermere : A
Story of Queensland bush life, by M.
COSTELLO. 3/6 ; post, 4/-.
OPINIONS OF THE PRESS :
THE SYDNEY HORNING HERALD.—“One of the
most faithful studies of bush life is Mr. Michael Costello’s
story, ‘ Harold Effermere ’ (George Robertson & Co.). The
ups and downs of the city boy, who is sent to a Queens-
land cattle - station and bolts, afterwards learning his
colonial experience of all kinds in company with a swindler
on the turf, as billiard-marker in a bush township, as
swagsman, and last as station-hand, manager, and owner,
are well told in a succession of easy and natural incident,
introducing many effective bush types. Evidently the
book is written from the fullness of personal knowledge.”
THE DAILY TELEGRAPH. —“ A story of the Queens-
land bush, by a new writer. . . . He has knowledge of the
bush, a sympathy with movement and adventure, an evi-
dently keen feeling for literature, both classic and modern
. . . and a large amount of very true sentiment. . . .
Harold Effermere, a waif and stray ... his father a sort
of unpolished and frankly dishonest Harold Skimpole. . . .
The pair go through some strange adventures together.”
THE EVENING NEWS. —“The hero of this story is a
town-bred boy . . . sent in an experimental way to see if
he would be likely to make anything of life on a station.
He has, as may be imagined, some rough experiences, all
of which, however, are turned to excellent account in the
end.”
THE RICHMOND AND WINDSOR GAZETTE.—“ A
well-written story of colonial life and its trials and tribu-
lations . . . should be read by all who take an interest in
promoting the growth and perfection of a literature which
should be—to paraphrase an old saying— * Of Australia,
for Australia, and by Australia.’ The book is published
by George Robertson and Sons, and is nicely turned out
indeed.”
THE TOWN AND COUNTRY JOURNAL.—“Mr. Cos-
tello has accomplished the feat . . . with a result that will
agreeably surprise the reader. He is thoroughly ac-
quainted with the scenes, incidents, and characters of the
life he describes, and represents them vividly. Practically,
the principal personage of the story is a crack-brained
fellow who combines knavery with folly in equal propor-
tions. ... As a picture of bush and station life such as in
Queensland still retains many of the traditional charac-
teristics fast disappearing in the more Southern colonies,
no less than for its incidents or for the romance. . . .
' Harold Effermere ’ is a good specimen of the Australian
novel, being both completely Australian and as novel as
its subject allows.”

The trustees of St. Kilda (Melb.) cemetery-
are about to establish a crematorium at a cost of
-£2000. In about ten years, N.S.W. may be ex-
pected to follow suit. It takes, on the average,
seven years for the N.S.W. courts to interpret a
new Act of Parliament, and ten years for a new
reform to reach N.S.W.
A publican out at thirsty Lawler’s, W.A.,
which is hundreds of miles from anywhere in par-
ticular, was lately fined ,£50 for Sunday trading—
that being the lowest legal penalty. From which
Thu Bulletin infers that the Act is practically a
dead letter in places where there isn’t a virtuous
policeman or a teetotal magistrate.
Figures will prove anything. For instance,
when it suddenly rains in Sydney Domain on a
Sunday, the crowd rushes for shelter into the
adjacent Art Gallery, sorely taxing the turnstile-
man, who carefully records every entrance; and
the people who run to get out of the rain go to swell
the official monthly return of “visitors to the
gallery.”
The Max of the World, a London
weekly, under the title of “The Old Man’s
Stake,” prints a column or more of verse which
originally appeared in Tmc Bulletin four or five
years ago as “ The Bookmaker’s Father.” Neither
the name of the author (Edmund Fisher) nor that
of The Bulletin is acknowledged.
Barnyard fowls have a most powerful
digestion. An ingenious French physician pro-
poses, therefore, to give some fowls large quantities
of iron in their food, which their digestive force
will render back into the albumen of their eggs.
Taese can then be administered to invalids, instead
of feeding the sick folks on indigestible iron salts
and fluids, which pass otf almost unused with the
general body-waste.

At Melbourne police court, the other day,
an alleged embezzler was brought up to go through
the preliminary hearing of a matter relating to a
very large shortage in the cash. 1 le was naturally
supposed to be in rigorous custody. When lunch-
time came the bench went out to refresh the inner
beak. The prosecuting attorneys went forth to
procure victuals at some adjacent hotel. So did
the defence and the police and the rest of the
show. The prisoner, left alone and forgotten,
thought the matter out for a while, and then he
naturally went out and lunched also. He had the
grace to come back, but if instead of doing so be
had got into an ancient suit of clothes and a pair of
burst boots and a ruined hat, and covered himself
with grime, and departed out of town with a swag
and a billy, he would have caused a great and
sudden sitting-up in the Melbourne legal system
that night.

S. M. Herald persistently sneers in its
elephantine way about the folly of M. L. and Vic-
torian factory legislation on the ground that it
prohibits an energetic man doing more than a
certain amount of work. So it does, in a sense,
but for one case where it prevents a man over-
■working himself voluntarily, there are at least a
thousand where it prevents him being overworked
against his will by an employer, and this aspect of
the case is never mentioned by the dreary Jib of
literature at the corner of Hunter-street, Sydney.
There is no law which doesn’t aggrieve somebody;
but if a law does good to 99 people and only
aggrieves the other one it is good enough. Theo-
retically, of course, every man has a right to work
16 hours a day for 16s. a week if he wants to ; also,
he has a,n equal right to bind himself down to work
all his life for nothing at all—which is the old idea
of personal slavery. One right is as indissolubly

MARVELLOUS TESTIMONIALS.
FLATTERING PRESS OPINIONS.
- - - MIRACULOUS CURES.
A few words as to the progress and growth in
public favour of the Registered Alfaline Herbal
Remedies would not be out of place here, and
would be additional evidence as to their real
genuineness and great virtues as claimed for them
by Herr Rassmussen, their discoverer and sole
proprietor.
The great virtues of the Alfaline Herbal Reme-
dies are so well known that it is quite unnecessary
to say much in regard to them in this notice; the
very fact of their having been before the publio
for so many years, and continually growing in
popularity, is quite sufficient evidence that they
are all they are claimed to be. Taking ir.to con-
sideration the fact that they have been awarded
the Parisian Gold Medal, and that so many thou-
sands of original and unsolicited testimonials have
been received, and may be seen at the Head Office,
some of which appear in his pamphlets, there
should be no difficulty in predicting a great future
for these health-giving Remedies unprecedented
in the History of Herbal Remedies.
About twelve years ago Herr Rassmussen intro-
duced these Remedies to the Australian public by
opening a Depot for their sale at 547 George
Street, Sydney, N.S.W., his present Head Office,
and although at that time he did not advertise in
any journal, the wonderful virtues of these Reme-
dies were known far and wide within a few
months. Orders for them came from every
quarter, not only from all the Australian colonies,
but, from all over the world. In 1891 the clamour
for the Alfaline Herbal Remedies had become so
great that it became impossible to supply everyone
from the Sydney Depot. Letters arrived by thou-
sands, many orders came by telegraph, and some
people even travelled over a thousand miles to
make sure of obtaining some of these God-inspired
Remedies. What was to be done ? It was Herr
Rassmussen’s duty to see that every sufferer from
the Blood, Skin, Nerves, Liver, and other affec-
tions, whose very life and happiness depended on
these Remedies, should be able to obtain same
without undue delay. Herr Rassmussen had thus
no other alternative than to open Branch Depots
for the sale of the Alfaline Remedies in every
colony, and, if possible, in every country in the
world, as he desired to keep the sale of his Herbal
Remedies in his own hands entirely, and thus
avoid the agency of chemists, who generally have
a substitute which they prefer to sell instead of
the real article. A start was made at once in that
direction, and a very large and palatial Depot
was opened at 150 Bourke Street, Melbourne, in
1891. Immediately after, a fine Branch was
established at 154 Rundle Street, Adelaide, fol-
lowed by the opening of an extensive Branch at
91 Lambton Quay, Wellington, New Zealand. A
large Branch was then established at the corner of
Hay and William Streets, Perth, W.A., and
shortly after another Branch, next to Coolgardie
Hotel, Bailey Street, Coolgardie, and N 0.2 Lin-
dell’s Chambers, Hannan street, Kalgoorlie, W.A.,
not to forget the very extensive Branch opened
some seven years ago at 160 Queen Street, Bris-
bane, which is now the Head Depot for Queens-
land, and finally a Branch just opened at Charters
'lowers, North Queensland.
It is not unreasonable to ask, “Is it possible
such success should follow unless these well-known
I lerbal Remedies are all they are claimed to be,
and is it likely that their popularity should keep
growing to such an extent, unless they contained
exceptional virtues out of the common ? ” The
evidence that Herr Rassmussen’s Herbal Remedies
contain wonderful virtues is simply overwhelming,
as lie possesses thousands of Testimonials to that
effect.
The whole of these Branches are each doing a
large and flourishing business. There is not a
country on the- face of the globe where these Re-
medies have not Been heard of, and orders arrive
daily from the uttermost parts of the earth.

Mr. Button writes later:- “The Pills I had from you
were of great benefit to me and others. In one case,
when three hunters and trappers were travelling about
the ranges near my place on the Rose River, one was taken
very ill ; his mates intended taking him in a waggon to
Wangaratta (about 60 miles) next morning to see a doctor.
I gave him a few of your Pills. I am pleased to inform
you that, having to pass their camp next day, I found
him up and pegging-out his skins and looking well. This
speaks volumes for your valuable Remedies.”

When Premier Kingston was farewelled
by Adelaide. City Council the other day some of
the speakers said they hoped to see him come back
knighted. He made no reference to this in his
reply. Ominous? Let “ Charley ” beware of (to
quote G. Republican Dibbs) “obliging a lady.”
Many of his little troubles have arisen from that
eort of thing.
The snufflesome Kerristian party in Hing-
land must be deeply mortified at the news of the
Unspeakable Turk’s behaviour in Larissa. No
ravishing, no hoisting of babies upon bayonets,
no atrocities whatever. “ Acts of pillage by the
-troops are punished by death, and the banks and
the residences of the wealthy classes are protected
by sentinels.”
The imported Adelaide hospital doctors,
Ramsay Smith and Napier, have been called upon
by the British Medical Association to resign their
membership because they have “ acted in opposition
to the strongly-expressed feeling and opinion of
the medical profession in Adelaide. ” This highly
original charge is the outcome of action taken by
one or two of the old hospital staff, but S. A. Govt,
has instructed a London firm of solicitors to legally
prevent the B.M.A. from striking the doctors off
the roll.

H. : “Moses, Jesus, and Mahomet are all
strong on fasting, which is quite omitted from
Protestant instruction. If a man wants a stimu-
lant to write, I say try that of hunger, on the ex-
ample of Balzac, Sand, Hugo, and Trollope, while
it is an element in the best work of the war cor-
respondents. Fasting is the Turkish bath of the
inside body. It is hygienic, too, with the great
religious leaders.” Who was it that said: “No
literary career in London for the man who break-
fasts ” ?
A butcher in a Westralian mining town
kept a black gin to mind the sheep—and sometimes
she did the cooking also. While the butcher and
his assistant were away, she was to cook some
sausages for supper, and, finding them good, ate
them all herself. To make more sausages for her
master, she took a piece of steak and cut it up in
thin strips about the length of a fair-sized sausage
and rolled them on her bare leg so as to make
them nice and round. The boss came home and
the sausages were dished up. “Rather tough,”
said the boss to Mary, “ where you bin gettem
sausage?” “Oh,” replied poor unsophisticated
Mary, “me bin makum this way,”—lifting her
dress and rubbing her skinny leg.
At Deal, in Kent (writes a Bulletin
correspondent), there are usually about 1800
marines in barracks. Most of these are mere
lads—recruits undergoing their first eight months
training before being drafted to Portsmouth,
Chatham, and Devonport. At Christmas some
considerable number got drunk and were awarded
the distinction of C.B. “ But, after all,” said an
Australian visitor to the place to a local trades-
man’s wife, “ these jollies seem a very quiet lot;
and I don’t see any women of bad character about
the place—in fact, there appear to be none.”
“ Quite true, sir, there isn’t. This is a very moral
place, is Deal. You see, sir, these ere young
marines only gets about two shillings a week, and
so we isn’t afraid of a-lettin’ our girls go out o’
nights. But in Canterbury, where them cavalry
chaps are, it’s very different. It ain’t a safe place,
is Canterbury, for a respectable girl. Why, some
of them cavalry officers gets as much as seven
shillings a week. ” Now, let a philosopher moralise
on this—two shillings a week to John Smith in
the marines keeps down illegitimacy ; seven shil-
lings a week— and the uniform —increases it. Per-
haps, therefore, there is wisdom in only paying
jollies two bob a week.

Dear Bulletin, —The author, like Sir
Edwin Arnold, who complains because his verse
is sandwiched between a couple of patent-pill
advertisements, shows poor gratitude towards the
advertisers who have made and still are making
money for the writers. The pecuniary rewards
of successful writers nowadays are large because
the profits of successful newspaper-proprietors are
gigantic. The great wits and playwrights of last
century considered themselves lucky if their
works brought them but the necessaries of life
with occasional luxuries, and in those days it was
only the truly great who could earn a living by
his pen alone. Nowadays, almost any intelligent
person can exist as a professional pen-man. For
all this, thanks are due chiefly to the enterprising
advertisers who furnish the staff of life to every
popular magazine or journal published. The
general public or reading fraternity have little to
do with the modern author’s prosperity. The
public is fickle and likes variety; it will buy a
writer’s work only so long as he remains at his
best. It is the ever-faithful advertiser whose
money, invested in a celebrated publication, first
enables the author to make a successful debut
and pays him for his subsequent services,
and whose influence enables him _ to find a
market for the inferior wares of his declining
years. No single writer’s stories can maintain a
popular journal’s circulation and influence. It is
his incorporation with those of his rivals and
superiors in the advertisers’ sheet that gives him
long life and prosperity.— Laege Adveetisee.
