“Mr. W. G. HEARNE.—Dear Sir, —Your medicine has
cured me of bronchitis and asthma, from which I suffered
for upwards of seven years, during which period I was
scarcely ever free from coughs, and frequently the diffi-
culty of breathing was so distressing that for nights in
succession I had to sit up. I write you this acknowledg-
ment from a sense of duty, as in my case every other
treatment had failed. For a year previous I had been
getting very much worse, and at the time I obtained your
medicine I was confined to bed suffering from a most
violent cough, expectorating blood and matter, and
apparently beyond hope of recovery. The first dose of
the medicine gave me welcome relief, and I steadily
improved as I continued the treatment, until I became,
as I am now, quite well.—Yours sincerely, H. WALKER,
Balmain, Sydney.’’

“The Wicked Flea.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
(Proverbs xxviii. 1.)
There’s a gully in the foot-hills, just beyond the
mountain rains,
Where the tableland is merging in the big and
black-soil plains ;
There they raise the greenish copper-stone —those
busy human bees —
Some forcible profanity and many billion fleas.
Those fat and fighting fleas ! —they nibble where
they please ;
They’re always feeding furiously —they rob you of
your ease.
Up your blessed legs they sneak and nip you till
you squeak ;
They’ve jaws like any bulldog, have the fleas of
Copper Creek.
You are speaking of your prospects and the water
that you lack,
When an old man tries to burrow in the hollow of
your back.
Then you say a little collect and you back ag’in a
post,
And you rub and pray with vigor till he antes-
up the ghost.
But his mates arrive in seas and they harry and
they teaze ;
They’re swarming in your breeches, but the deuce
a one you’ll seize.
Begad ! they’ll make you speak ; they’ll make you
preach in Greek—
They stimulate devotion, do the fleas of Copper
Creek.
Sometimes they have their races—you can feel
them start and stop ;
They’re stayers, yes, and clever —you can’t catch
’em on the hop.
They’ve a bit of blood inside ’em, and their breed-
ing can’t be matched—
And yet they’ll have a flutter, though they’ve only
just been scratched.
Then they settle down to feed—you rehearse your
solemn creed
That all hoppers go to Hades, for then it’s Hell
indeed ;
Old Nick will groan in pique for his tortures tame
and weak,
When Shed’s gates are opened to the fleas of
Copper Creek.
When you seek your bunk at evening, exhausted
by your graft,
Prepare to meet your boarders, for they’ll storm
you fore and aft;
Then you ask for many blessings that you’d never
get in Heaven,
And you catch and squeeze a lodger till you make
him chuck the seven ;
But the others all show fight, and they gnaw and
tear and bite —
You’ve only got to scratch and itch and yearn for
morning light;
For each one’s got a beak that will go through
steel and teak,
So what’s a cuticle before the fleas of Copper
Creek ?
When, perhaps, a little slumber comes before
you’re quite insane,
You dream of fleas as big as bulls, and bugs as
big again.
One has grabbed you for a mouthful and you see
his pointed teeth,
When you wake and note your bedding disap-
pearing underneath.
(From your carcase they will yank it; they will
mosey with your blanket;
And then it’s dash and blank it, and condemn the
gaudy fleas:
From your hut you rush and shank it; if Hell
opened you would thank it,
For they’ve tapped your blood and drank it, and
they’ve only left the lees.)
O ! we catch them here in traps, but we meet with
some mishaps,
For they downed a man the other day—we’ll get
his bones, perhaps.
They’re full of vice and cheek ; they’d eat you in
a week;
They’re real holy terrors, are the fleas of Copper
Creek !
CECIL POOLE.
Copper Creek, Bingara, N.S.W.
“Out Back.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Dusr-storms ? ” queried the old hand, talking to
the new-chum, as a solid-looking bank of dust
rolled towards them. “Oh, that ain’t a dust-
storm —leastways, nothing to speak of.”
Then a pause and a puff. “Now, we did have
a storm last September twelve month,” he con-
tinued. “ I was dam-sinkin’ on Netallie and
drivin’ a horse-scoop, when it come on—a cloud
like that, only thicker. We met. Of course all
was darkness. Then them horses pulled like
blazes. We kep’ goin’up and up. ‘Damn it,’ I
says, ‘ I never noticed this bank so steep before.’
Then we started to go down sharp, at a trot.
Over the scoop goes. Then down and down.
‘Strike me,’ says I, ‘this bank’s high enough to
hold water enough for the whole flamin’ station.’
Then we bottomed it with a jolt.
“ Just at that minute the sky cleared. I looked
back at the cloud still sweeping on, and stouch me
if there wasn’t our tracks clean over it ! ”
“Bad,” the new-chum said, encouragingly.
“ ’Myes ; but I had the luck to meet it, you see.
If I’d ’a’ been goin’ with it we’d ’a’ been flattened
out under it like wax matches under a injun.”
Then the storm, that was only a pretence at a
storm, came on them, and they covered their heads
with their blankets.
RED-BACK.
A Tale of Nine.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was a railway-camp, after the evening
meal; and a group of more or less contented men
had gathered round one of the fires, engaged in
“swopping ” yarns and burning tobacco. A lank
and sad-faced man stood a little apart—a new-
comer. He was interested in the conversation,
although his gaze would now and then stray away
from the group and seek out a lighted tent on the
other side of the creek, on the canvas side of
which a woman’s figure was clearly outlined. By-
and-bye a remark attracted his attention. He
drew near, an eager expression on his face.
“ Yes, you’re right, old man,” he broke in,
“there is sich a thing as luck. I know.” The
man addressed nodded approval ; one or two
sceptics, younger than the others, smiled pity-
ingly ; the rest of the group appeared mildly in-
terested. The new man was not discouraged.
“Aye, luck there is, all right. Listen here!
My luck as a young chap was mostly bad. It
changed when I got married though. ’Twas then
I found out I’d a lucky number. Nine it is.
Knew the missus nine weeks ; married her on the
ninth of the month ; missed the train we was
goin’ away by and had to wait nine hours for the
next ”
‘ ‘ Any porters about ? ” queried one of the young
men. The group was amused. The narrator let
the interruption pass unheeded.
“The train we missed,” he continued, “was
smashed up by another one —you’ve heard tell of
the Burro way smash-up? That proved it. Yes,
nine’s my lucky number right enough.”
The other believer in luck was interested. He
leant forward. “ Has it stuck to you all through,
matey ? ” he asked. “ How’s your luck been ever
since ? ”
The lank man reflected. The canvas in the dis-
tance reflected other figures now—two small ones
with clasped hands and upturned faces.
“ Well, no,” he admitted, reluctantly, “ it ain’t.
Leastways, I dunno. I’ve got a, good missus and
two kids—l’ve buried six ; the times has been out-
of-the-way bad ever since, that’s all.”
One of the sceptics laughed immoderately. The
man’s faith tickled him. The lank man eyed him
for a moment sorrowfully, then turned slowly
away in the direction of his tent. Luck’s advo-
cate rose and followed him. Together they
walked a little way in silence. Then the lank
man said, “ I was a fool to say anything
about it there, but I couldn’t help myself. I felt
I had to say it, but that jackass spoilt it. You’ll
understand, though.” And then he told his com-
panion that he had great faith in the future. He
had only started work in the quarry on Monday,
and the number he had allotted him was
nine. Wasn’t that a good sign? Yes, and
more! Within the next couple of days, all
going well, his ninth child would be born.
This was to be the turning-point in his fortunes.
His lucky number! In the morning he was
going to get up early and get aboard the “up
goods” that passed the quarry siding at five
o’clock —it generally slowed down a little there —
and go to the township ahead to see if he could
get a “ handy-woman ” to come and see the missus
through her trouble. Thus they came to the tent,
and parted.
* * + *
“Half the ropes on these brake-vans are
well rotten,” said the boss of the fettlers on the
length the following morning, as he superintended
the placing of a much-mangled figure on a hastily-
improvised litter. “ It’s a damned disgrace,
that’s what it is.”
The five o’clock “goods” had passed a couple
of hours ago : it had not slowed down, however ;
the piece of rotten rope in the victim’s hands ex-
plained everything. The fettlers raised the litter,
the ganger took up a position alongside, and the
little procession set off in the direction of the
camp. Halfway there they stopped to change
hands. .Tust then the figure showed signs of life.
They raised the head and moistened the dried
lips with a little cold tea.
“ Run —over —wasn’t 1 ? ”
“ Yes,” they told him.
“ By No. 9 goods,” the ganger added, with strict
Governmental attention to detail.
The man raised himself almost erect. “ You —
don’t say? No. 9, eh? Then that—accounts.
Any other—would ha’—killed me. That’s my—
lucky— number.” He sank back exhausted. The
ganger bent over and wiped the blood from his
lips.
The procession moved on. Within a few yards
of the victim’s tent Luck’s advocate of the pre-
vious night encountered it. He broke the news
to the widow.
L. R. MACLEOD.
J. Fdnie Brown, Parkes’s appointee, who
was rather unceremoniously cold-shouldered out
of N.S. Wales, seems to have settled down for a
long spell in Westralia. When appointing him
Conservator of Forests there the Govt, told him
he could remain while the timber for export lasted.
Ednie’s latest report says the supply will last 200
years.
A Frenchman, visiting London, describes
his business-call upon the head of the house of
Rothschild :
A long passage, narrow and dark, brings you to an
obscure old mansion. The lane (St. Swithin’s) reminds
one of Venice, and the Baron spends his days there.
Every day he sits at his table from 11 a.m. to 7 p.m. No
chairs are provided for visitors (Arons editor Haddon’s
plan); standing discourages long interviews. The paralysis
in liis legs is in sharp contrast to the activity of his mind.
The face is a fine one, but the impression of it that abides
is of something inexorable as a mathematical calculation.
It is by gaslight that you visit this pawnshop for crowned
heads.

A General’s Daughter.
CURED OF RHEUMATIC AND NEURALGIC PAINS,
ANAEMIA, NERVOUS HEADACHE, AND GENERAL
WEAKNESS BY DR. WILLIAMS’ PINK PILLS.
(Erom the Hampshire Independent.)
“ There is nothing more interesting, nay, fascinating, than the
fta.lV of our brave defenders, who have served their Queen and
country in far-distant lands. To talk with an Indian officer,
hearing his reminiscences and
adventures, is what those who
have enjoyed it always appre-
ciate. Consequently (writes a
special reporter of the Hamp-
shire Independent) _ I was
delighted to receive instruc-
tions to interview Lieutenant-
General Shaw, who has won
his spurs in India, and is now
living in honorable retirement.
The General’s house is the
model of a haven of rest. En-
closed in its own grounds, it
rests beneath the shadow of
the sacred edifice from which
it obtains its name, ‘ The
Vicarage.’ In the trees were
nests of sweet songsters, who
are never disturbed. But there
was no time to wax poetic. I
had grasped the bell-pull and
given it one tug when the door opened and the General stood before
me. You knew he was a soldier at once. His manly, upright bear-
ing, his smile, his pleasant voice—all told you that you stood in
the presence of one of Nature’s gentlemen. He ushered me in and
at once put me at my ease by his affable conversation.
“‘I am afraid,’he said, ‘ that you have come a long distance ;
but let me know the precise object of your visit.”
“ I explained to the General that I was most anxious, with
his consent, to obtain some explanation as to the narrow escape I
had heard his daughter had recently experienced.
“At that he brightened visibly. ‘You must know,’ he said,
* I’m just a bit of an enthusiast on this point; but the tale is very
short. Nearly two years ago my daughter—the one you referred
to —came home from India, and when I joined her in London I
found her ill in bed. She had rheumatic and neuralgic pains ; she
was perfectly bloodless, listless, and in a generally weak and pros-
trate condition. We called in a doctor, and lam bound to say
that he succeeded in getting her out of bed; but she was still
absolutely colorless, was in great wretchedness, and suffering from
what I should call anaemia or bloodlessness. She had a kind of
fever, nervous headache, and other pains. Well, we went to
Bembridge, in the Isle of Wight, and there I first heard of Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People. Indeed, I was recom-
mended to try them, and I did so. My daughter took the first
(£*■-
'i'W,
•i.-v
Lieutenant-General Shaw.
box, and they had a marvellous effect upon her. She regained her
color; she lost her pains; and became altogether different, and
after she had been taking them some time she came in one day
after her walk and said to me, ‘ Why, father, I feel quite a glow
through me.’ She went on taking the Pills, and I am glad to tell
you that she recovered completely. I recommended Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills to all with whom I came in contact, and all who took
them derived great benefit therefrom. We came to live at
Shanklin, and just now my daughter has had another attack. She
resorted again to Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People, and
she is getting better very rapidly.’
“‘You said, General,’ I remarked, ‘that you heard of Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People at Bembridge. Would you
mind telling me how that came about ? ’
“ ‘ Oh, yes,’ he replied. ‘ I have a sister at Jersey, and she
has taken them for a very long time, and has always recommended
them to other people, and found them to do her a great deal of
good, and all to whom she has recommended them have been
benefited ; and I myself, when I have heard of people being ill,
have taken them or sent them some of these Pills.’
“ ‘ With what result ? ’ I asked.
“‘ Well,’ was the reply, ‘my daughter’s recovery was due to
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People without a doubt. Their
effects were marvellous, and I did not speak without first ascer-
taining the facts. I have had a great deal to do with medical men
in this country and in India, and they laugh at the idea that
patent medicines cure any disease. But I have given you this
case, and that I know; I won't deal with any beyond my own
personal experience and knowledge further than to say I believe
the results to have been satisfactory.’
“ ‘ Then, I may say, General, that your daughter’s recovery
was attributable to Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills ? ’
“ ‘Decidedly,’replied the General, ‘and, as I said, I have
heard of other cases of a similar character, but into them I will
not go, as I prefer only to tell you what I know to be true from my
own personal observation.’
“ ‘ In the second place,’ I said, ‘ may I make your name public
as giving me the information? Your name and standing would
show how valuable is the testimony.’
“ * Oh,’ replied the General, ‘ I see no reason for withholding
it. Yes, you may publish it, and I wish I could have given you
more information.’ ”
EIGHTEEN MONTHS AFTER.
It may be interesting, and it is certainly important, to record
that this remarkable cure proved permanent. The events de-
scribed in the above interview occurred a year and a half ago.
Miss Shaw has been personally visited at her father’s house at St.
Leonards, within the last few days, and remains in robust and
perfect health.
For Female Troubles.
Pure blood is the secret of health and beauty. The features
may be regular, the form perfect, but no woman can be beautiful
in the full sense of the word while suffering from any of the
peculiar ailments of her sex. Disease destroys the complexion, is
productive of wrinkles and premature old age. Regular monthly
action is necessary to every woman’s health, and if this
function of life is checked, disease, a pale and sallow com-
plexion, and a feeling of exhaustion, are the result. The action
must continue from puberty to the turn of life without un-
natural obstruction. Any breach of this law of nature will result
in the distressing symptoms which make the lives of seven-tenths
of the women of this country miserable, almost unbearable. A
few of these symptoms are severe headaches, loss of appetite,
pale or sallow complexion, palpitation of the heart, swollen

His Mate’s Wife .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The January sun had blazed down all day upon
Abram Poldew’s three-roomed hessian-lined gal-
vanised-iron cottage; and suffocating dust still
poured in through crevices visible and invisible.
The pattern of the cheap oil-cloth on the floor
was obliterated wherever it had not been worn off
by the four youngsters who scuttled over it.
This was one of Central Australia’s worst days
too bad even for these youngsters to play out of
doors. And partly for this reason their mother,
by nature a woman abominating dirt and disorder,
was utterly worn out and miserable and querulous.
It was 3 o’clock, and she had not yet cut her
man’s “crib,” and he sat waiting uneasily for it.
For this week he was on afternoon shift, working
from 4 o’clock till midnight.
The children, born in unfavorable surroundings
and never out of them, were weakly ; and to-day,
cooped up in the filthy hot-house, they quarrelled
incessantly.
“ Oh, dash these brats ! Can’t you cut this
bread yourself? I’d give something to be a man.
You’ll sneak off into a cool mine, and please your-
self whether you work or smoke. Oh, yes, con-
tractin’s a fine thing—for the man, isn’t it! And
his family doesn’t matter. But me—l’ll have
these howling brats hanging on to me till 9, and
it’ll be dust and sweep till you come home. Then
you must have your sleep till 8 or 9—they’ll be
pestering me at 5. Oh, it’s —it’s —it’s hell—that’s
what it is ! There ! ”
* * * *
The man remembered that the stope where he
must work would be so hot that the sweat would
run from him in streams, and that the lead-dust
and ’fracteur smoke would hang about, sickening
him till he was forced out of the working “ one-
face ”to vomit. Yet he loved the woman, and
was silent—to her great annoyance.
Joe Driver, Poldew’s mate, pushed open the
door and stood within, crib-can in hand. She
took no notice of him.
“ It’s a bad day,” he ventured.
“ Bad? Isn’t it all bad?” she answered, fiercely.
“ It’s hell, or worse ; and only for the children you
wouldn’t find me here when you came off. If you
were half a man ” —this to her husband —“ you’d
get out of it—yes, if you had to work your hands
raw to do it—get to the West, or—or—oh, or any-
where out of this hole.”
A blast of fiery wind filled the room with
dust, and the hessian ceiling and walls flapped
dismally, jarring upon her strained nerves.
“ Listen ! Isn’t it a place to ask a woman to
live in—and have your children in. Haugh ! I’m
dead-sick of it—sick of everything. ”
“ Never mind, old girl; it won’t last for ever,”
he said vaguely, as he put his crib into his can.
He was going to lay his arm encouragingly upon
her shoulders, but she pulled herself back.
“ Never mind ! ” she echoed, with slow savage,
sarcastic emphasis, and then laughed a half-scorn-
ful, half-delirious laugh.
* * » *
They pulled their hats over their eyes and
pushed through the blinding dust - storm in
silence. The fierce heat - blasts scorched their
faces, and the driven dust stung them. Only
when they stood in the lee of the engine-house did
Poldew speak. “ Don’t ever bring a wife to
Silver Gully, Joe,” he said, sadly. “It isn’t fair
to her or the kids.”
They took their two mates’ places on their six-
men’s contract “ pitch,” in which, a separate pair
worked each eight-hour shift.
“ She’s only fairly steady now,” the outgoers
said. “ Might be as well to put in a false set of
timber and keep her wedged up. ’
But Driver heard only the poor passionate
wife’s pleadings to be taken out of the intolerable
heat and dust of Silver Gully ; and he knew that
their only chance of going was for the party to
make “ a bit more than wages.”
He tapped the roof.
“Oh, she’ll do,” he said. For he knew that to
putina “false set” took much tinie, for which
there was no extra payment. If they could take
out a couple of feet more without temporary
timber, it was as good as half a shift apiece to
them.
Yet he was not easy ; he knew the risk, and he
was the mate in charge. He remembered that
Poldew was a married man with youngsters, and,
besides, was the less agile.
“Abe,” he said, “you sort out this stuff the
day-shift threw back, will you, and clean up a
bit.” And he went into the face himself.
* * *
A clatter behind told Driver that the shift-boss
was on his rounds. He drew back, anxious to
engage the boss’s attention, and, if possible, to so
keep him out of the face ; for he knew that other-
wise timber would be ordered in.
Poldew had not spoken a word ; but he went at
his work with a resolute energy which Driver
understood. And now, when Driver suddenly
came back out of the face, Poldew stepped in,
took up the pick and swung it vigorously. At
the second blow the fall came.
He was gasping when they dragged him out,
and he died on the flat as they waited for the
cage.
The jury said that Abram Poldew came to his
death by his own neglect, and that on the witness
Driver’s own evidence he too was guilty of
neglect which, in their opinion, amounted almost
to manslaughter, and they absolved the company’s
officials from all blame.
And the coroner severely lectured Driver ; and
the owners’ newspaper remarked that it was
another instance of the cupidity of workmen, com-
bined with their folly in defying and setting at
nought the wholesome rules of the mine.
* * * *
Driver, full of anguish, followed the body to
the morgue; and in that gloomy vault he came
face to face with the dead man’s wife.
She sprang towards him.
“You wretch—you cowardly wretch! You—
you left him to take the danger—you without
ties, and him with a family ; you sent him in to
take the risk while you stood back ! Oh, you
sneak ! you mean, selfish, cowardly cur ! You a
mate ! ”
And she threw her arms upon the slab, and
buried her face and her sobs.
' Yet how could he tell her the truth, even if she
would listen—tell her that she had driven them
both to it!
“ Amounted almost to manslaughter!” she com-
mented on the verdict. “It was murder—a
cowardly murder ! ”
P.
A late visitor declares that the aspect of
the country around Illawarra must remind every-
one who has seen Tipperary of that portion of
fighting Ireland. But hardly ever is begorrah
heard in the land, and never a shillelah raps a
skull. What a waste of beautiful country !
Down in Honolulu.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
’Twas down in Honolulu,
Way off one night afar,
The sea-breeze coinin’cooler
Across the coral bar,
When Lulu’s eyes were brighter
Than any girl’s I knew,
When Lulu’s teeth were whiter
Than any coral, too.
Oh, Lulu, Lulu, Lulu,
My warm Pacific pearl,
My lovely, lively Lulu—:
My own Kanaka girl.
I kissed her for her mother,
I gev’ her one, two, three ;
I squoze her for her brother
’Twas all the same to me.
The moon went settin’, later,
Below the mango trees,
One horn towards the crater,
One pointin’ to the. seas.
Oh, Lulu, Lulu, Lulu,
I taste them kisses still;
That tropic moon’s a-settin’
Beyond the darkened hill.
For, oh ! your heart was beat in’,
For, oh ! your breath was sweet,
And you was good for eatin’,
If gals was good to eat.
And, oh ! your lips was cherry,
And, oh ! your teeth was white —
I’ve tried in vain to bury
The memory of that night.
Ah, Lulu, Lulu, Lulu,
I’d give my life, I vow,
To live that starlight over,
I know I loved you now.
We heard the ripples feelin’
The white edge of the sand,
The good, kind music stealin’—
That Yankee war-ship’s band ;
I never hear them playin’
That old star-spangled air
But ’neath the trees I’m layin’,
And you, my girl, are there.
Oh ! Lulu, Lulu, Lulu,
Wherever you may be,
That old “Star-spangled Banner”
Still brings you back to me.
The sea-breeze, perfume-laden,
It rustled through the palms
The night, the night I laid in
Your warm, soft, twining arms.
You swore to love me ever,
I swore to love you true
Forever an’forever—
The way we used to do.
Oh ! Lulu, Lulu, Lulu,
’Twas years and years ago ;
I don’t forget it, somehow,
Although I ought, I know.
We heard the Chinkies prattle
Way up in China Town.
We heard the hawse-chains rattle
That let the anchor down,
“Eight bells,” I hear them falling—
The Yankee’s bugles blow,
“ Eight bells,” the bosun’s calling—
Dear Love, I’ve got to go.
Oh, Lulu, Lulu, Lulu,
Don’t cling so awful tight;
The old man’s got his papers,
Good-bye ! Ah, no ! good night.
I feel your arms still clinging—
Oh, what’s the use to cry ?
It’s “Homeward Bound” they’re sinking
I’ll come back by-and-bye.
Eight bells ! It’s done and over ;
While ships still sail the sea,
A sailor man’s a rover—
Good-bye, and think of me.
Oh, Lulu, Lulu, Lulu,
I broke the sailor’s vow ;
I want to live it over,
I know I loved you now.
’Twas down in Honolulu,
Way back in other years,
I left you, lovely Lulu,
The starlight and the tears.
But, oh, your face was fairer
Than any face I’ve met,
And oh, your charms were rarer
Than any woman’s yet.
And, Lulu, Lulu, Lulu,
Wherever you may be,
My brown Kanaka Lulu—
Do you remember me ?
Sydney, 1897.
E. J. BRAHY.
History is getting to be vulgarised by
slang. When Cecil Rhodes received news of the
failure of the Rand raiders, he bowed his head and
murmured, “ Poor old Jameson has upset my
apple cart.”
A r ic. Labor-member Prendergast is often a
wearisome and gaseous politician, but lie has pro-
bably done more good work in enforcing the
minimum-wage law than any other man in the pro-
vince. In course of time, no doubt, the local boss
will grow accustomed to the new order of things
and acquiesce in it from force of habit, but at [ire-
sent the enforcement of the minimum wage is an
uphill struggle. And Prendergast does°inost of
the fighting, with very little reward except the
whole-souled hatred of the bell-toppered class in
general.

<£19,500, on the Melb. Y. M.C.A. Hall, at one

they are paid.”
